Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

The Word مَن: Generality, Particularity, and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Analyzes the word مَن as a generalizer, with a Hanafi ikhtilaf on its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the word مَن when combined with partitive مِن, and apply the usul principles of generality (ʿumūm) and particularity (khuṣūṣ) to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
When مَن (whoever) is combined with مِن (partitive), the Hanafi school splits: the Sahibayn read it as generality, Abu Hanifa reads it as partiality.

This passage explores how the word مَن, which normally indicates generality, can become particular when paired with a partitive preposition. The dispute between Abu Hanifa and his students shows the importance of linguistic context in Hanafi usul. By examining the master's statement 'Free whomever I wish among my slaves,' you will see how two valid interpretations arise from the same text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text and operative words
The source text is the master's statement: 'مَن شئت من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه'. Identify the key words: مَن (general relative pronoun for rational beings), شئت (I wish), من (partitive meaning 'from among'), and عتقه (free him). Note that the expression combines مَن (general) with من (partitive).
Focus on the combination of مَن and مِن, which is the catalyst for the dispute.
2
Classify each word according to Hanafi usul
مَن: a relative pronoun that is originally general (ʿāmm), indicating 'whoever'. مِن: a preposition that can indicate tabʿīḍ (partiality) or bayān (specification). The Sahibayn treat مِن as bayān (distinguishing slaves from others), while Abu Hanifa treats it as tabʿīḍ, indicating a part.
The classification determines the scope of the command.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle for combining general and partitive
Hanafi usul holds that when a general word (ʿāmm) is combined with a partitive (tabʿīḍ), the general is restricted to some (not all) of its instances. Abu Hanifa applies this literally: the command covers some slaves generally, but not all. The Sahibayn see no such restriction because مِن here is not partitive but merely distinguishing.
The principle: الجمع بين العموم والتبعيض يوجب التبعيض (combining generality and partiality necessitates partiality).
4
Compare the two positions and their proofs
Sahibayn: The agent may free all slaves, analogizing to Quran 22:30 'فَاجْتَنِبُوا الرِّجْسَ مِنَ الْأَوْثَنِ' where مِن distinguishes idols from others without limiting generality. Abu Hanifa: The agent may free all except one, because treating من as tabʿīḍ requires leaving at least one slave unfreed to give effect to both words.
The practical difference (thamarah): one slave remains unfreed in Abu Hanifa's view.
5
Note the application from al-Siyar al-Kabir
In the fortress example, 'مَن دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً' (whoever among you enters this fortress first) – the word أولاً (first) specifies particularity, so only one person can earn the spoil. If two enter together, the condition is unmet and the spoil is void. This shows how context can determine particularity even when مَن is general.
Contrast this with the earlier scenario: here the particularity comes from the word 'first', not from مِن.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : كلمة: مَن، وهي تحتمل العموم والخصوص، قال الله تعالى: وَمِنْهُم مَّن يَسْتَمِعُونَ إِلَيْكَ ..... وَمِنْهُم مَّن يَنظُرُ إِلَيْكَ (يونس). وأصلها العموم، قال النبيُّ صلى الله عليه و سلم: «من دخل دار أبي سفيان: فهو آمن». وقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله فيمن قال لعبيده مَن شاء من عبيدي العتق : فهو حر، فشاؤوا جميعاً : عتقوا. فأما إذا قال: مَن شئت من عبيدي عتقه : فأعتقه: فقد قال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : للمأمور أن يُعتقهم جميعاً ؛ لأن كلمة من عامة وكلمة من التمييز عبيده من غيرهم، مثل قوله :تعالى ﴿فَاجْتَنِبُوا الرِّجْسَ مِنَ الْأَوْثَنِ ﴾ (الحج / ٣٠). وقال أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه : له أن يُعتقهم إلا واحداً منهم؛ لأن المولى جَمَعَ بين كلمة العموم والتبعيض، فصار الأمر متناولاً بعضاً عاماً، وإذا قصر عن الكل بواحد : كان عملاً بهما ، وهذا حقيقة التبعيض. وروى ابن أبي حاتم عن مجاهد : الطائفة: رجل. والله أعلم. وكذلك قوله : مَن شاء من عبيدي عنقه : فهو حر: يتناول البعض، إلا أنه موصوف بصفة عامة فسقط بها الخصوص، وهذه الكلمة تحتمل الخصوص ؛ لأنها وُضعت مبهمةً في ذوات من يعقل. مثاله : ما قال في السير الكبير : من دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النَّفَل كذا، فدخل واحد منهم فله نَفَلُه ، وإن دخل اثنان معاً فصاعداً: بطل النَّفَلُ ؛ لأن الأول اسم للفرد السابق، فلما قَرَنَه بهذه الكلمة : دل ذلك على الخصوص، فتعين به احتمال الخصوص، وسقط العموم ، فلم يجب النَّفَلُ إلا لواحد متقدم، ولم يوجد.
Among that is the word ‘مَن’ (whoever), which can indicate generality and particularity. Allah said: ‘And among them are those who listen to you... and among them are those who look at you’ (Yunus). Its origin is generality. The Prophet ﷺ said: ‘Whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan is safe.’ Our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said: If someone says to his slaves, ‘Whoever among my slaves wishes freedom, he is free,’ and they all wish it, they are all freed. As for if he says, ‘Whoever of my slaves I wish to free, free him,’ and he gives permission, then Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them both) said: The one commanded may free all of them, because the word ‘مَن’ is general and the word ‘مِن’ distinguishes his slaves from others, like His saying: ‘So avoid the abomination of idols’ (al-Hajj 30). Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) said: He may free all except one, because the master combined a word of generality with one of partiality, so the command applies to some generally, and if it falls short of all by one, that is acting on both, and this is the reality of partiality. Ibn Abi Hatim narrated from Mujahid: ‘the party (طائفة) is one man.’ And Allah knows best. Likewise, his saying: ‘Whoever among my slaves wishes to be free, he is free’ applies to some, but it is described with a general description, so particularity falls, and this word can indicate particularity because it was placed as ambiguous concerning rational beings. Example: What is said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: ‘Whoever among you enters this fortress first, he has such-and-such from the spoils.’ If one of them enters, he has his spoil; if two enter together or more, the spoil is void. Because ‘first’ is the name for the preceding individual; when he connected it with the word ‘مَن’, that indicated particularity, so the possibility of particularity was determined, generality fell, and the spoil is not due except for one who preceded, and he did not exist.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage examines the word ‘مَن’ (whoever), which can signify both generality and particularity. The author starts by citing Quranic verses (Yunus) where ‘مَن’ is used in a context of generality, and a hadith where it is general (‘Whoever enters... is safe’). He then presents a Hanafi fiqh scenario: a master says to his slaves ‘Whoever among my slaves wishes freedom, he is free.’ If all wish, all are freed, showing generality. But if he says ‘Whoever of my slaves I wish to free, free him,’ a dispute arises: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the agent can free all, as ‘مَن’ is general and ‘مِن’ distinguishes his slaves from others. Abu Hanifa holds that only all except one can be freed, because the combination of general (مَن) and partitive (مِن) requires partiality—the command applies to some generally, but not all. The author then mentions that Ibn Abi Hatim reported from Mujahid that ‘طائفة’ can mean one person. Next, he analyzes a similar phrase: ‘Whoever among my slaves wishes to be free, he is free’—it applies to some, but the general description of all slaves negates particularity. Finally, he gives an example from al-Siyar al-Kabir about entering a fortress: if the prize is for ‘whoever enters first’, only one can qualify; if two enter together, the condition of ‘first’ is not met, so no prize. This shows how ‘مَن’ combined with a specific condition (‘first’) indicates particularity.

Key Terms
مَن (man): A relative pronoun meaning 'whoever', can indicate generality or particularity; its origin is generality.
التبعيض (al-tab'id): Partiality; indicating a part of a whole, as distinct from generality.
العموم (al-umum): Generality; a word that encompasses all individuals to which it applies without exception.
الخصوص (al-khusus): Particularity; a word that applies to a specific subset of individuals, not all.
التمييز (al-tamyiz): Distinction; using a word to specify or distinguish one category from another without necessarily indicating partiality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute about 'مَن شئت من عبيدي عتقه فأعتقه', what is Abu Hanifa's ruling?
A) The agent can free all slaves
B) The agent can free all except one
C) The agent can free only one slave
D) The agent cannot free any without explicit permission

Key Takeaway
The word مَن is general by origin, but can become particular when combined with partitive من, leading to ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

The Word كُلّ: Comprehensive Generality with Individuality
Definition of كُلّ and its usage in indicating generality with individuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the word كُلّ in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguish it from مَن, and apply its meaning to identify general rulings in Quranic verses and legal statements.

Core Idea
كُلّ indicates every individual separately; مَن indicates the group collectively.

This lesson introduces the word كُلّ, one of the key Arabic words that indicate generality (عموم) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Understanding its precise meaning is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts and formulating rulings. The passage defines كُلّ as indicating encompassment on the basis of individuality, a unique feature that distinguishes it from other general expressions like مَن.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its function
Recognize that كُلّ is a noun indicating encompassing completeness, but its unique feature is that it applies to each individual separately (على سبيل الإفراد).
The word is always attached to another noun or verb via a connector.
2
Understand 'الإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد'
This phrase means comprehensive coverage in the manner of singling out each individual. For example, in 'كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ', each soul is considered individually.
3
Note the requirement of a connector
كُلّ is never used alone; it must be connected to something (a noun or a verb via a connector like ما). Without a connector, its meaning is incomplete.
When attached to a verb, it uses a connector like 'كلما'.
4
Distinguish from مَن
Both can indicate generality, but كُلّ stresses individuality (إفراد) for each referent, whereas مَن is general without that individual emphasis.
Example: 'كل امرأة أتزوجها' means every individual woman, not a group.
5
Apply to Quranic example
Analyze the verse 'كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ' to see that it obligates death for every individual soul, highlighting the individual scope.
This verse is used as evidence for the generality of كُلّ in fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم آخر : وهي كلمة : كلّ، وهي للإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد. قال الله تعالى: كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ (آل عمران/١٨٥)، ومعنى الإفراد أن يُعتبر كلَّ مسمى منفرداً كان ليس معه غيره وهذا معنى ثبت بهذه الكلمة لغةً فيما أُضيفت إليه، كأنها صلة، حتى لم تُستعمل مفردةً. وهي تحتمل الخصوص أيضاً مثلُ كلمة: مَن، إلا أنها عند العموم تخالفها في إيجاب الإفراد، فإذا دخلت على النكرة: أوجبت عموم الأسماء، مثل قول الرجل : كلُّ امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق. ولا تصحب الأفعال إلا بصلة، فإذا وصلت : أوجبت عموم الأفعال، مثل قول الله سبحانه وتعالى: كُلما نَضِجَتْ جُلُودُهُم بَدَّلْتَهُمْ جُلُودًا غَيْرَهَا (النساء /٥٦). وعلى هذا مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله.
Another category: the word ‘كُلّ’ (every/all), which indicates encompassing in the manner of individuality. Allah said: ‘Every soul shall taste death’ (Aal-e-Imran 185). The meaning of individuality is that each named being is considered individually, as if nothing else is with it. This meaning is linguistically established by this word when it is added to something, as if it is a connector, such that it is not used alone. It can also indicate particularity like the word ‘مَن’, but it differs from it in requiring individuality when indicating generality. So when it enters upon an indefinite noun, it necessitates generality of nouns, like a man saying: ‘Every woman I marry is divorced.’ It does not accompany verbs except with a connector; when it does attach, it necessitates generality of verbs, like Allah’s saying: ‘Whenever their skins are roasted, We replace them with other skins’ (al-Nisa 56). Upon this are based the cases of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them).
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines the word ‘كُلّ’ (every/all). The author explains that it indicates encompassing (إحاطة) but in the manner of individuality (على سبيل الإفراد). That is, it applies to each individual item separately, not as a collective. He supports this with the Quranic verse ‘كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ’ (Every soul shall taste death), showing that each soul is considered individually. The linguistic feature is that ‘كُلّ’ is not used alone; it is always connected to something (usually a noun). It can also indicate particularity like ‘مَن’, but when it indicates generality, it differs by stressing individuality. For example, when attached to an indefinite noun (like ‘كل امرأة’), it makes the noun general—every individual woman. It can also attach to verbs via a connector (like ‘كلما’), making the verb general, as in the verse about roasting skins. The author concludes that the Hanafi scholars (Sahibayn) base their rulings on this principle.

Key Terms
كُلّ (kull): A word indicating encompassment of all individuals, with each considered individually; requires a connector and can be general or particular.
الإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد (al-ihata 'ala sabil al-ifrad): Comprehensive coverage in the manner of singling out each individual.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) كُلّ cannot indicate particularity, while مَن can
B) كُلّ requires individuality (إفراد) for each referent, while مَن does not
C) كُلّ can be used without a connector, while مَن cannot
D) كُلّ only applies to indefinite nouns, while مَن only to definite

Key Takeaway
The word كُلّ in Hanafi usul indicates comprehensive coverage of each individual item separately, a key distinction from other general terms.

The Word 'Man' and Its Indication of Particularity
Defining 'man' as general yet capable of specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the word 'man' (من) indicates particularity rather than generality in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The word 'man' is general but can be specified by context or qualifiers, affecting legal rulings.

The word 'man' appears frequently in the Quran and hadith. In Hanafi usul, it is classified as 'amm (general) in form but capable of specification (takhsis) when attached to a qualifying attribute. This lesson examines how a single word can shift from general to particular, with a concrete legal example from the Hanafi fiqh work al-Siyar al-Kabir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default generality of 'man'
In Arabic, 'man' is a general word covering all rational beings. Recognize that it is ambiguous (mubham) until qualified.
The text states 'man' is 'mubhamah fi dhawat man ya'qil' (ambiguous for rational beings).
2
Spot the qualifier that triggers particularity
Look for any attribute or description attached to 'man' that restricts its scope. In the example, 'first' (awwalan) is such a qualifier.
The qualifier can be an adjective, a condition, or a relative clause.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle of specification
When a qualifier is present, 'man' shifts from general ('umum) to particular (khhusus), and the ruling applies only to the specific subset.
Hanafi usul treats 'umum as qat'i until specified, but specification is accepted readily.
4
Derive the legal ruling from the specified meaning
In the example, the promise of extra spoils (nafal) is only for a single first entrant, not for two or more entering together. The ruling is that the nafal is void for simultaneous entry.
The text says 'batala al-nafal' (the extra spoils become void) for two or more.
5
Contrast with the opposing view on generality
Note that the Shafi'i school considers 'umum as zanni, which can affect how readily specification is applied. However, both schools accept specification in principle.
This cross-madhhab comparison is important for understanding the epistemological weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك قوله : مَن شاء من عبيدي عنقه : فهو حر: يتناول البعض، إلا أنه موصوف بصفة عامة فسقط بها الخصوص، وهذه الكلمة تحتمل الخصوص ؛ لأنها وُضعت مبهمةً في ذوات من يعقل. مثاله : ما قال في السير الكبير : من دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً فله من النَّفَل كذا، فدخل واحد منهم فله نَفَلُه ، وإن دخل اثنان معاً فصاعداً: بطل النَّفَلُ ؛ لأن الأول اسم للفرد السابق، فلما قَرَنَه بهذه الكلمة : دل ذلك على الخصوص، فتعين به احتمال الخصوص، وسقط العموم ، فلم يجب النَّفَلُ إلا لواحد متقدم، ولم يوجد.
And likewise His saying: 'Whoever among My slaves wishes freedom' so he is free: it indicates some [individuals], except that it is qualified with a general description, so particularity falls due to it. And this word is capable of particularity because it was placed ambiguously for rational beings. Example: What Muhammad said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'Whoever among you first enters this fortress, for him is such-and-such extra spoils.' So if one of them enters, he has his extra spoils. But if two enter together or more, the extra spoils are void because 'first' is a name for the preceding individual, and when he connected it to this word, it indicates particularity; so the possibility of particularity is determined, generality falls, and the extra spoils are only due to a single preceding person, and it did not occur.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'man' (من) as a general word for rational beings that can be specified (تخصيص) when attached to a qualifying attribute. The example from al-Siyar al-Kabir, a primary work of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, illustrates the legal effect: a promise of extra spoils (نفل) to the first entrant is fulfilled only for the first single entrant, not for a group, because 'man' with 'first' indicates particularity. This shows that in Hanafi usul, general words are not always absolute; they can be restricted by context.

Key Terms
من (man): A general word for rational beings that can indicate particularity when qualified.
عموم (umum): Generality; the word covers all individuals of its category.
خصوص (khhusus): Particularity; the word applies to a specific subset rather than all.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They split it.

Key Takeaway
The word 'man' is general in form but becomes particular when qualified by a specific attribute, a key principle in Hanafi usul.

The Word Kull: Individual Comprehensive Coverage in Hanafi Usul
Defining 'kull' as comprehensive individual coverage with Quranic proof and a practical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'kull' as a general word requiring individual coverage, distinguish it from 'man', and apply the Hanafi principle using the example from al-Siyar al-Kabir.

Core Idea
Kull covers every individual separately—each gets the full ruling, unlike 'man' which groups them.

In this passage, Imam al-Pazdawi explains a key distinction between two types of general words in Hanafi usul: 'kull' (every/all) and 'man' (whoever). While both can indicate generality, 'kull' imposes individuality—each member addressed receives the ruling independently. This has practical consequences in contracts and rewards, as shown by Imam Muhammad's example from al-Siyar al-Kabir. Let's parse the definition and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
State the Arabic term 'kull' and its definition as 'al-ihata 'ala sabab al-ifrad'.
Ifrad means each entity individually, not collectively.
2
Locate the Quranic evidence
Find the verse cited (3:185) and note how it demonstrates individual coverage.
The verse: 'Every soul shall taste death'.
3
Analyze the contrast with 'man'
Understand that while 'man' considers the group, 'kull' considers each singly.
This is a key distinction in Hanafi usul.
4
Work through al-Siyar al-Kabir example
Apply the rule: if ten enter together under 'kull', each gets full reward; under 'man', reward is void.
The example illustrates the practical difference.
5
Note the exception: specificity
Recognize that 'kull' can become particular (when context restricts it), so if ten enter individually, only the first gets reward.
This shows the nuanced application of general words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم آخر : وهي كلمة : كلّ، وهي للإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد. قال الله تعالى: كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ ﴾ آل عمران/١٨٥، ومعنى الإفراد أن يُعتبر كلَّ مسمى منفرداً كان ليس معه غيره وهذا معنى ثبت بهذه الكلمة لغةً فيما أُضيفت إليه، كأنها صلة، حتى لم تُستعمل مفردةً. وهي تحتمل الخصوص أيضاً مثلُ كلمة: مَن، إلا أنها عند العموم تخالفها في إيجاب الإفراد، فإذا دخلت على النكرة: أوجبت عموم الأسماء، مثل قول الرجل : كلُّ امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق. ولا تصحب الأفعال إلا بصلة، فإذا وصلت : أوجبت عموم الأفعال، مثل قول الله سبحانه وتعالى: كلما نَضِجَتْ جُلُودُهُم بَدَّلْتَهُمْ جُلُودًا غَيْرَهَا ) النساء /٥٦. وعلى هذا مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله. * وبيان ما قلنا من الفرق بين كلمة كل و مَن فيما قاله محمد رحمه الله في السَّير الكبير»: من دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً فله النَّفَل كذا، فدخله جماعةٌ : بطل النَّفَل. ولو قال : كلَّ مَن دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً : فله كذا، فدخل عشرةً معاً: وجب لكل رجل منهم النَّفَلُ كاملاً على حياله؛ لما قلنا إنها توجب الإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد فاعتُبر كل واحد منهم على حياله، وهو أول حق من تخلف من الناس وفي كلمة من وجب اعتبار جماعتهم، وذلك ينافي الأولية. ولو دخل العشرة فرادى في مسألة : كل: كان النَّفَل للأول؛ لأنه الأول من كل وجه، وهي تحتمل الخصوص، فسقط عنها الإحاطة، وصارت للخصوص
And another category: the word 'kull' (every/all). It is for comprehensive coverage on the basis of individuality. Allah Most High says: 'Every soul shall taste death' (3:185). The meaning of 'individuality' is that each named entity is considered individually, alone, not with others. This meaning is linguistically established for this word when attached to something, as if it is a connector, such that it is not used alone. And it also admits particularity like the word 'man', but when used generally, it differs from 'man' in requiring individuality. So when it enters upon an indefinite noun, it obligates generality of nouns, like a man saying: 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' And it does not accompany verbs except with a connector; when so connected, it obligates generality of verbs, like Allah's saying: 'Whenever their skins are roasted, We replace them with other skins' (4:56). Upon this are the rulings of our companions, may Allah have mercy on them. And an explanation of what we said about the difference between 'kull' and 'man' is what Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'Whoever enters this fortress first, for him is such-and-such extra spoils.' If a group enters, the extra spoils are void. But if he said: 'Every person who enters this fortress first, for him is such-and-such,' and ten enter together, then each man among them gets the full extra spoils individually, because, as we said, it obligates comprehensive coverage on the basis of individuality, so each one of them is considered individually. And the first right from the people has lapsed, while with the word 'man', consideration of their group is necessary, which contradicts priority. And if the ten enter individually in the case of 'kull', then the extra spoils go to the first, because he is the first in every respect, and 'kull' admits particularity, so comprehensive coverage falls away and it becomes particular.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This section defines 'kull' (كل) as a word of comprehensive coverage (إحاطة) that operates on an individual basis (على سبيل الإفراد). It contrasts with 'man' which considers the group. The example from al-Siyar al-Kabir demonstrates: under 'kull', if ten enter together, each gets the full reward individually; under 'man', reward is void if more than one enters together. However, if they enter individually under 'kull', only the first gets reward because 'kull' can be specified (تحتمل الخصوص). This shows the nuanced application of general words in Hanafi legal theory, derived from the Zahir al-Riwayah works.

Key Terms
كلّ (kull): A word implying comprehensive coverage of each individual separately.
إحاطة (ihata): Comprehensive coverage; the word encompasses all members.
إفراد (ifrad): Individuality; each member is considered singly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Pazdawi, what is the meaning of ifrad in the definition of 'kull'?
A) Each member is considered as part of a collective group.
B) Each member is considered individually and separately from others.
C) The word 'kull' refers to the entire set as a whole.
D) 'Kull' has no specific meaning ifrad.

Key Takeaway
The word 'kull' obligates individual coverage (ifrad), distinguishing it from words like 'man' that consider the group, and it can be specified by context.

The Word 'Al-Jami' and Its Collective Implication
Defining 'al-jami' as a general word requiring collective action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the word 'al-jami' as a general word requiring collective action and apply its legal implication to differentiate between collective and individual actions in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Al-jami' demands collectivity — a group acting together is treated as a single unit.

Words in the Arabic language carry precise legal implications. One such word is 'al-jami', which, like 'kull', denotes generality but uniquely requires collective action. In this lesson, we examine how the Hanafi school defines 'al-jami' and how its meaning affects rulings in practical examples from the works of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word 'al-jami' and its general nature
Recognize that 'al-jami' is categorized as a general word (amm) in Hanafi usul, similar to 'kull' but with an additional requirement of collectivity.
It is not a specific or partial word; it applies to all members of a group as a unit.
2
Compare 'al-jami' with 'kull' and 'man'
Note that 'man' is general for individuals separately, while 'kull' is general for each individual. In contrast, 'al-jami' treats the group as a single entity, requiring collective action.
This distinction is fundamental to applying the correct ruling.
3
Understand the requirement of collectivity (ijtima')
The word 'al-jami' inherently means 'together as a group'. It does not permit individual or separate actions under its literal sense.
If used metaphorically to mean 'kull', the collective requirement may be lost.
4
Apply to the example from al-Siyar al-Kabir
Muhammad al-Shaybani's ruling: if a reward is offered to 'all together who enter first' and ten enter together, they share one reward collectively. If they enter individually, the first gets it as 'al-jami' is interpreted as 'kull'.
This example illustrates both the literal and metaphorical uses.
5
Recognize the metaphorical borrowing
The author notes that 'al-jami' can be borrowed to mean 'kull' in certain contexts, allowing individual application when collective action is not feasible.
This nuance prevents mechanical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم آخر كلمة الجميع، وهي عامة مثل كلمة : كل، إلا أنها توجب الاجتماع دون الانفراد، فصارت بهذا المعنى مخالفة للقسمين الأولين، ولذلك صارت مؤكّدةً لكلمة : كل. وبيان ذلك في قول محمد رحمه الله في السير الكبير جميع من دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله كذا، فدخل عشرةٌ منهم : إن لهم نَفَلاً واحداً بينهم جميعاً بالشركة ، ويصير النَّفَلُ واجباً لأول جماعة تدخل. فإن دخلوا فرادى : كان النَّفَلُ للأول ؛ لأن الجميع : يحتمل أن يُستعار المعنى الكل.
And another category: the word 'al-jami' (all together). It is general like the word 'kull', except that it requires collectivity (social) not individuality (infirad). So in this meaning it differs from the first two categories, and therefore it serves as an emphasis for the word 'kull'. An explanation of that is in the statement of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'All together who enter this fortress first, for them is such-and-such.' So if ten of them enter, they have one extra share among them all in partnership, and the extra share becomes obligatory for the first group that enters. And if they enter individually, the extra share goes to the first, because 'al-jami' can be borrowed to mean 'kull'.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-jami' (الجميع) as a general word requiring collective distribution (اجتماع) rather than individual (انفراد). It differs from 'man' and 'kull' . The example from al-Siyar al-Kabir shows that under 'al-jami', a group entering together gets one share collectively; if they enter individually, the first gets it because 'jami' may be used metaphorically to mean 'kull'. This illustrates Hanafi usul's careful attention to linguistic nuances.

Key Terms
الجميع (al-jami'): A general word implying collective (not individual) application.
اجتماع (ijtima'): Collectivity; the word treats the group as a unit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary difference between 'al-jami' and 'kull'?
A) 'Al-jami' is more general than 'kull'
B) 'Al-jami' requires collectivity, 'kull' does not
C) 'Kull' requires collectivity, 'al-jami' does not
D) Both are identical in meaning

Key Takeaway
Al-jami' is a general word that compels collective action, distinguishing it from 'kull' which may apply individually.

Scope of the Word 'Ma' in Hanafi Usul
Defining 'ma' as a general word for non-rational beings and attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scope of the word 'ma' in Hanafi usul and distinguish it from 'man'.

Core Idea
'Ma' covers non-rational things and qualities, not persons.

In Hanafi legal theory, the words we use to interpret divine texts have precise scopes. The particle 'ma' (ما) is a general word for non-rational entities and for the attributes of rational beings. This classification helps the jurist correctly apply Quranic verses where 'ma' appears.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Locate the word 'ma' (ما) in the text. Note that it is a general word (عامة) used in Arabic for certain categories.
2
Determine its scope
The text states 'ma' is general for two categories: non-rational entities (ذوات ما لا يعقل) and attributes of rational beings (صفات من يعقل). Memorize these two categories.
Attributes include qualities like knowledge, power, and justice.
3
Contrast with 'man'
Recall that 'man' (من) is the general word for rational beings. Understanding this contrast prevents misapplication when interpreting Qur'an and Sunnah.
For example, 'ma' is used for objects and abstract concepts, while 'man' is used for persons.

The Generality of 'Kull' as Individual Inclusivity
Defining the usul category of 'kull' and its legal effect through a reward example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'kull' requires individual inclusivity to derive the ruling in a conditional reward contract.

Core Idea
كُل generalizes each individual separately, not the group as a whole.

This passage from the Usul of al-Bazdawi clarifies how the word 'kull' (every) operates in legal language. In Hanafi usul, 'kull' imposes generality by encompassing each individual independently (isti'abah 'ala sabab al-ifrad). This has direct consequences for contracts, oaths, and rewards. The example given uses a military leader's promise of spoil (nafal) to illustrate the difference between 'kull' and other words like 'man' (who).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal phrase
Locate the conditional reward phrase: 'kullu man dakhala…' (every one who enters…). Recognize that the word 'kull' is the key term.
The phrase includes both 'kull' and 'man'. Note how each affects the ruling.
2
Classify the word 'kull'
In Hanafi usul, 'kull' is a lafz 'amm (general term) that signifies generality as individual inclusion (isti'abah 'ala sabab al-ifrad). This means it applies to every individual separately, not collectively.
The translation of the passage confirms: 'it necessitates encompassing individually' (tūjib al-iḥāṭa 'alā sabīl al-ifrād).
3
Apply to the scenario of simultaneous entry
When ten people enter the fort together, each is considered independently under 'kull'. Therefore, each of the ten is entitled to the full spoil (nafal), because each is individually covered by the reward condition.
The passage explicitly states: 'each man among them gets the full spoil individually'.
4
Contrast with the word 'man'
If the phrase used 'man' instead of 'kull', the group would be considered collectively (i'tibār jamā'atihim). In that case, entering together would not entitle any individual to the full spoil, because the condition of being 'first' would apply to the group as a whole.
The passage says: 'In the word 'man', their collectivity is considered, which contradicts priority.'
5
Handle individual entry with 'kull'
When the ten enter individually (one by one), the first entrant is unmistakably the first in every respect, so he alone gets the spoil. 'Kull' here admits particularity (tah'tamil al-khuṣūṣ) because context restricts it to the 'first' condition, overriding the general applicability.
The passage explains: 'if the ten entered individually… the spoil is for the first, because it sustains particularity.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال : كلَّ مَن دخل منكم هذا الحصن أولاً : فله كذا، فدخل عشرةً معاً: وجب لكل رجل منهم النَّفَلُ كاملاً على حياله؛ لما قلنا إنها توجب الإحاطة على سبيل الإفراد فاعتُبر كل واحد منهم على حياله، وهو أول حق من تخلف من الناس وفي كلمة من وجب اعتبار جماعتهم، وذلك ينافي الأولية. ولو دخل العشرة فرادى في مسألة : كل: كان النَّفَل للأول؛ لأنه الأول من كل وجه، وهي تحتمل الخصوص، فسقط عنها الإحاطة، وصارت للخصوص
And if he said: 'Every [kull] one of you who enters this fort first gets such-and-such spoil,' then if ten entered together, each man among them gets the full spoil individually, because we said that it necessitates encompassing individually, so each one of them is considered by himself. And this is the first right of those who lag behind among the people. And in the word 'who [man]', their collectivity is considered, which contradicts priority. And if the ten entered individually in the case of 'kull', the spoil is for the first, because he is first in every respect, and it sustains particularity, so the encompassing falls and it becomes particular.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'kull' in Hanafi usul. It shows that 'kull' implies generality as individual inclusion (isti'abah ala sabab al-ifrad). When used in a promise of spoil (nafal) for the first entrant, if all ten enter together, each gets the full spoil because each is independently included. In contrast, if 'man' (who) is used, the group is considered collectively and no one gets the full spoil because they are not individually first. When they enter individually with 'kull', the first is the only one entitled. The passage illustrates the linguistic and legal effect of 'kull' in conditional contracts.

Key Terms
كل (kull): A word indicating generality that encompasses each individual separately (isti'ābāh 'alā sabīl al-ifrād).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only one spoil is given to the group.
B) Each of the ten gets a full spoil individually.
C) No spoil is given because they are not first.
D) The first among them gets the spoil.

Key Takeaway
The word 'kull' in Hanafi usul requires individual inclusivity, so each individual covered by the term is entitled to the full ruling independently.

The Word 'Jami'' and Its Collective Generality in Hanafi Usul
Distinguishing the generality of جميع as requiring collectivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the word 'jami'' as a general term requiring collectivity and distinguish it from 'kull' in a Hanafi usul analysis.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, 'jami'' demands collectivity — the group as a whole — unlike 'kull' which covers individuals.

In this lesson, we examine a distinct category of general words: 'jami''. While it resembles 'kull' in indicating generality, it carries an additional nuance — it necessitates that the group be considered collectively, not individually. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts that employ 'jami'', as shown in a ruling by Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its claimed property
Locate the word 'jami'' in the passage. Note that it is described as being general like 'kull' but with an extra condition: it requires collectivity (ijtima').
jami' is the Arabic word for 'all' but with a specific technical usage.
2
Compare 'jami'' with 'kull'
Contrast the rulings: 'kull' indicates each individual separately, whereas 'jami'' indicates the group together. This is why 'jami'' is used to confirm and strengthen 'kull'.
The passage states: 'صارت مؤكدة لكلمة كل'.
3
Understand the consequence: collectivity vs. individuality
If a text uses 'jami'' to refer to a group action, the ruling applies to the group collectively. For individual entry, 'jami'' is interpreted metaphorically as 'kull'.
This distinction produces different fiqh outcomes.
4
Apply to the example from al-Siyar al-Kabir
Work through Imam Muhammad's example: 'All (jami') who enter this fort first get such-and-such spoil.' If ten enter together, they share one spoil because 'jami'' necessitates collectivity. If they enter individually, the first gets the spoil because 'jami'' is metaphorical.
The phrase 'جميع من دخل' includes the term 'jami''.
5
Recognize when 'jami'' is metaphorical
Note the exception: when entry is not collective, 'jami'' may be understood in the sense of 'kull' (each individual). This prevents absurdity.
The passage says: 'يحتمل أن يستعار المعنى الكل'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم آخر كلمة الجميع، وهي عامة مثل كلمة : كل، إلا أنها توجب الاجتماع دون الانفراد، فصارت بهذا المعنى مخالفة للقسمين الأولين، ولذلك صارت مؤكّدةً لكلمة : كل. وبيان ذلك في قول محمد رحمه الله في السير الكبير جميع من ((دخل هذا الحصن أولاً فله كذا، فدخل عشرةٌ منهم : إن لهم نَفَلاً واحداً بينهم جميعاً بالشركة ، ويصير النَّفَلُ واجباً لأول جماعة تدخل. فإن دخلوا فرادى : كان النَّفَلُ للأول ؛ لأن الجميع : يحتمل أن يُستعار المعنى الكل.
And another section: the word 'jami'' [all] is general like the word 'kull', except that it necessitates collectivity (ijtima') rather than individuality. So in this sense it differs from the first two sections, and therefore it serves as a confirmation of 'kull'. And its clarification is in the statement of Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'All (jami') of those who enter this fort first get such-and-such spoil.' So if ten among them entered, they have a single spoil among them all in partnership, and the spoil becomes due to the first group that enters. If they entered individually, the spoil is for the first, because 'jami'' may be used metaphorically in the sense of 'kull'.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'jami'' in Hanafi usul. Unlike 'kull', 'jami'' requires the inclusion of the group as a whole (ijtima'). When used in the spoil example from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir, if ten enter together, they share one spoil because 'jami'' considers them collectively. If they enter individually, it is treated like 'kull' metaphorically, and the first gets the spoil. Thus 'jami'' is more emphatic in insisting on collectivity and is understood as a confirmation of 'kull'.

Key Terms
جميع (jami'): A word indicating generality that necessitates inclusion of the group together (ijtima') as a whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Generality of 'Ma' and 'Alladhi' in Hanafi Usul
Defining the scope of 'ma' and 'alladhi' as general words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scope of generality of the word 'ma' and apply it to Hanafi legal examples involving conditionals and divorce.

Core Idea
'Ma' is general for non-rational entities and attributes — its scope determines rulings in conditional manumission and divorce.

In this passage, the author defines two key words of generality: 'ma' (what) and 'alladhi' (which/who). Understanding these terms is essential for parsing legal texts and deriving rulings correctly. The passage uses linguistic examples and a Quranic verse to establish the generality of 'ma', then applies it to two Hanafi cases: a slave-girl's conditional freedom and a wife's delegated divorce. There is also an intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding the extent of 'ma's generality in the divorce context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories covered by 'ma'
Read the first sentence: 'ma is general for non-rational beings (dhat ma la ya'qil) and attributes of rational beings (sifat man ya'qil).' Note that it is not general for rational beings themselves (that is 'man').
2
Analyse the linguistic and Quranic examples
The author gives examples: 'What is in the house?' (non-rational) and 'What is Zaid?' (attribute). The Quranic verse (2:284) is cited to show that 'ma' encompasses all entities in the heavens and earth, both non-rational and attributes.
The verse reinforces the generality of 'ma' in the divine context.
3
Apply the rule to the slave-girl case
A master says: 'If what (ma) is in your womb is a male, you are free.' Because 'ma' is general and covers all contents, the condition is only met if everything in the womb is male. Birth of twins (male and female) fails the condition.
This shows that 'ma' is taken as comprehensive (shamil).
4
Contrast the views on 'ma' in the divorce case
A man tells his wife: 'Divorce yourself from the three what (ma) you wish.' The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) interpret 'ma' as general, allowing her to divorce all three times. Abu Hanifa limits to once or twice, implying that 'ma' here is not fully general or is particularized by context.
This dispute is revisited from an earlier discussion; it shows that the scope of 'ma' can be contested.
5
Note the metaphorical use of 'ma'
The passage concludes that 'ma' can be used metaphorically in the sense of 'man' (i.e., for rational beings), but that is a secondary usage. The primary usage is as defined.
This is important for interpreting texts where 'ma' appears in contexts that normally take 'man'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم آخر كلمة : ما وهي عامَّةٌ في ذوات ما لا يعقل، وصفاتِ من يعقل. تقول : ما في الدار ؟ وجوابه شاة ، أو فرس. وتقول : ما زيد؟ وجوابه : عاقل، أو عالم. وقال أصحابنا رحمهم الله فيمن قال لأمته : إن كان ما في بطنك غلاماً فأنت حرة، فولدت غلاماً وجارية لم تعتق؛ لأن الشرط أن يكون جميع ما في البطن غلاماً، قال الله تعالى: لِلَّهِ مَا فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَمَا فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ البقرة / ٢٨٤. وكذلك كلمة الذي في مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله. وهذه في احتمال الخصوص مثل كلمة : من. وعلى هذا يُخرج قولُ الرجل لامرأته : طلقي نفسك من الثلاث ما شئت أن على قولهما تُطلق نفسها ثلاثاً، وعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله : واحدةً، أو ثنتين ؛ لما قلنا في الفصل الأول. ويجوز أن تستعار كلمة ما بمعنى مَن، وهذه كلمات موضوعةٌ غير معلولة.
And another section: the word 'ma' [what] is general for non-rational beings (dhāt mā lā ya‘qil) and attributes of rational beings. You say: 'What is in the house?' and the answer is 'a sheep' or 'a horse.' And you say: 'What is Zaid?' and the answer is 'intelligent' or 'learned.' And our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) said concerning one who said to his slave-girl: 'If what is in your womb is a male, then you are free,' and she gave birth to a male and a female, she is not freed because the condition is that all of what is in the womb is male. Allah Most High says: 'To Allah belongs what is in the heavens and what is in the earth' (al-Baqara 2:284). Similarly the word 'alladhi' [which/who] in the issues of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them). And these sustain particularity like the word 'man'. And on this is based the saying of a man to his wife: 'Divorce yourself from the three what you wish' that according to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) view she divorces herself thrice, and according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) once or twice, as we said in the first chapter. And it is permissible for the word 'ma' to be used metaphorically in the sense of 'man'. These words are established (mawḍū‘ah) and not rationally derived (ghayr ma‘lūlah).
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines two words of generality: 'ma' and 'alladhi'. 'Ma' is general for non-rational beings and attributes of rational beings, as shown by linguistic examples. Its generality is seen in the Quranic verse about Allah's ownership of all in heavens and earth. In Hanafi fiqh, if a master says 'if what (ma) is in your womb is a male, you are free,' the condition is only fulfilled if all contents are male, due to the generality of 'ma'. The word 'alladhi' is like 'man' in that it can sustain particularity. An example from divorce: if a man tells his wife 'divorce yourself from the three what (ma) you wish,' the sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say she can divorce all three times because 'ma' is general, but Abu Hanifa limits to one or two because he sees it as particular. The passage also notes 'ma' can be metaphorically used for 'man'.

Key Terms
ما (mā): A word of generality used for irrational entities (dhāt mā lā ya‘qil) and attributes of rational beings (ṣifāt man ya‘qil).
الذي (alladhī): A relative pronoun that, like 'man', allows the possibility of particularity (iḥtimāl al-khuṣūṣ).
من (man): The word for 'who', general for rational beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only non-rational beings.
B) Non-rational beings and attributes of rational beings.
C) Non-rational beings and rational beings.
D) Only attributes of rational beings.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ma' is a general word covering non-rational beings and attributes, and its scope must be carefully assessed in legal texts, as illustrated by the Hanafi rulings on conditional manumission and divorce.

Generality of the Indefinite Noun (Nakira) with Negation
Explaining the rule that nakira becomes general with evidence like negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an indefinite noun (nakira) becomes general only when connected to an evidence of generality, and identify negation as such evidence.

Core Idea
Negation makes an indefinite noun general; affirmation keeps it specific.

This passage explains a key principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the indefinite noun (nakira) is not inherently general. It only becomes general when paired with an external evidence (dalīl) of generality, such as negation. This rational distinction between negation and affirmation is fundamental to understanding how rulings are derived from texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indefinite noun
Locate the nakira in the given Arabic sentence. It is a noun without the definite article, indicating an unspecified individual.
The nakira itself is specific unless external evidence generalizes it.
2
Check for connection to an evidence of generality
Determine if the nakira is in a negated context (e.g., preceded by mā, lā, lam, etc.) or in an affirmative context. The passage specifies that negation is a strong evidence of generality.
Other evidences include the word kull, but this lesson focuses on negation.
3
Apply the logical necessity
If the nakira is in negation, recognize that negating one instance necessarily negates all instances, otherwise the negation would be incomplete. This rational proof (dalīl aqlī) is the basis of generality.
In affirmation, no such necessity exists; one instance does not imply all.
4
Derive the ruling
Based on step 3, the ruling from the text applies to all members of the category. For example, in 'mā jā’anī rajul' (no man came), the negation applies to every man.
This derivation is critical for istinbat (extraction of rulings) from texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم آخر النكرة إذا اتصل بها دليل العموم: تعمُّ؛ لأن النكرة تحتمل ذلك إذا اتصل بها دليله مثل ما قلنا في كلمة : كل. ودلائل عمومها ضروبٌ ، وبيان ذلك : أن النكرة في النفي تعم، وفي الإثبات: تخص؛ لأن النفي دليل العموم، وذلك ضروري لا لمعنى في صيغة الاسم، وذلك أنك إذا قلت: ما جاءني رجل: فقد نفيت مجيء رجل واحد نكرة، ومن ضرورة نفيه : نفي الجملة؛ ليصح عدمه، بخلاف الإثبات؛ لأن مجيء رجل واحد: لا يوجب مجيء غيره ضرورةً، فهذا ضرب من دلائل العموم.
And another section: the indefinite noun (nakira) when connected to an evidence of generality becomes general; because the indefinite noun can sustain that when its evidence is connected, like we said in the word 'kull'. And its evidences of generality are of various types. And its clarification is that the indefinite noun in negation is general, and in affirmation it is specific. Because negation is an evidence of generality, and that is necessary (ḍarūrī) not due to a meaning in the form of the noun. That is because when you say: 'No man came to me [mā jā’anī rajul],' you have negated the coming of a single indefinite man, and from the necessity of its negation is the negation of the entire class, so that its non-existence is valid. Contrary to affirmation, because the coming of a single man does not necessitate the coming of another necessarily. So this is one type of the evidences of generality.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage discusses the indefinite noun (nakira) in Hanafi usul. Although an indefinite noun is inherently specific, when connected to an evidence of generality (dalīl al-‘umūm) it becomes general. The prime example is negation: when you say 'mā jā’anī rajul' (no man came to me), the word 'rajul' (a man) is indefinite, but due to the negation, it applies to all men. This is rational: negating one implies negating all because otherwise the negation would not be complete. In affirmation 'jā’anī rajul' (a man came to me) does not imply all men. Thus negation is a sign of generality, not from the word itself but from the logical necessity.

Key Terms
النكرة (al-nakira): An indefinite noun; it becomes general when connected to a sign of generality such as negation.
دليل العموم (dalīl al-‘umūm): An evidence of generality; a sign that makes a word apply to all its instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When it is used in an affirmative sentence.
C) When it is connected to an evidence of generality, such as negation.
D) Only when used with the word 'kull'.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite noun becomes general only when connected to an evidence of generality such as negation, not by its own form.

Nakirah in Negation Indicates Generality
States the rule that indefinite nouns in negation are general, in affirmation specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that an indefinite noun in a negative context indicates generality to interpret legal texts.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in negation implies all instances, not just one.

This lesson introduces a foundational rule of Hanafi usul: indefinite nouns (nakirah) become general when placed in a negative context. This is not due to the word form but the logical necessity of negation. Understanding this rule is crucial for interpreting Quranic verses like 'Lā ikrāha fī d-dīn' (no compulsion in religion) and for deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the principle: the indefinite noun in negation indicates generality, while in affirmation it indicates specificity.
This is a key Hanafi usul rule, considered definitive (qat'i).
2
Understand the rationale
Why does negation cause generality? Because negating a single indefinite instance logically requires negating all instances; otherwise the negation would be false.
The author calls this 'ضروري' (necessary) and not due to the word's form.
3
Contrast with affirmation
In affirmation, the indefinite remains specific because affirming one does not imply the existence of others.
Example: 'A man came' does not mean all men came.
4
Apply to a sample text
Use the example 'No man came to me' (ما جاءني رجل). The indefinite 'رجل' in negation indicates that no man at all came.
This demonstrates the practical application of the rule.
5
Recognize the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school treats this generality as qat'i (definitive) due to logical necessity, unlike some other schools that consider it zanni (probable) without a contextual indicator.
This difference affects the strength of rulings derived from similar texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسم آخر النكرة إذا اتصل بها دليل العموم: تعمُّ؛ لأن النكرة تحتمل ذلك إذا اتصل بها دليله مثل ما قلنا في كلمة : كل. ودلائل عمومها ضروبٌ ، وبيان ذلك : أن النكرة في النفي تعم، وفي الإثبات: تخص؛ لأن النفي دليل العموم، وذلك ضروري لا لمعنى في صيغة الاسم، وذلك أنك إذا قلت: ما جاءني رجل: فقد نفيت مجيء رجل واحد نكرة، ومن ضرورة نفيه : نفي الجملة؛ ليصح عدمه، بخلاف الإثبات؛ لأن مجيء رجل واحد: لا يوجب مجيء غيره ضرورةً، فهذا ضرب من دلائل العموم.
Another type: The indefinite [noun] when connected with evidence of generality becomes general; because the indefinite can bear that when connected with its evidence, like we said regarding the word 'kull'. And the evidence of its generality are of types. And the explanation of that is that the indefinite in negation is general, and in affirmation it is specific, because negation is evidence of generality. And that is necessary, not due to a meaning in the form of the noun. That is, if you say: 'No man came to me,' you have negated the coming of one indefinite man, and from the necessity of that negation is the negation of the entire class, in order for its non-existence to be valid. Contrary to affirmation, because the coming of one man does not necessarily require the coming of another. So this is one type of the evidence of generality.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi discusses how indefinite nouns (nakirah) can indicate generality ('umum) when associated with certain indicators. The author identifies a key principle: an indefinite noun in a negative context becomes general, while in an affirmative context it remains specific. This is not due to the form of the word itself but arises from the logical necessity of negation. For example, saying 'No man came to me' (ما جاءني رجل) negates the coming of a single man, but for the negation to be true, it must negate all men; otherwise, if one man came, the statement would be false. In contrast, saying 'A man came to me' (جاءني رجل) only affirms the coming of one man and does not imply that others came. This principle is used in Hanafi usul to interpret legal texts—for instance, in the Quranic verse 'There is no compulsion in religion' (لا إكراه في الدين), the indefinite 'compulsion' is negated, thus indicating the negation of all compulsion. This is one type of evidence for generality.

Key Terms
النكرة (al-nakirah): An indefinite noun that can become general when connected with a proof of generality, especially in negation.
دليل العموم (dalil al-'umum): Evidence that makes an indefinite noun general; here specifically negation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because of a consensus among scholars.
D) Because the indefinite noun is always general.

Key Takeaway
The indefinite noun in negation becomes general due to the logical necessity that negating one requires negating all; this is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul.

Lam al-Ta'rif for Generic Meaning and 'Ahd
Explains lam al-ta'rif for generic meaning and 'ahd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lam al-ta'rif for jins (genus) and for 'ahd (prior mention), and apply the Hanafi rule that indefinite nouns require same session for 'ahd.

Core Idea
The definite article 'al' indicates either the entire species or a prior mention—knowing which reveals the ruling on generality.

This passage is a usul rule that explains one of the indicators of generality (sighah al-umum) in Hanafi usul: the definite article (lam al-ta'rif) when it cannot refer to a specific entity. It clarifies that 'al' then indicates either the genus (jins) or a previously known entity ('ahd). The author provides Quranic and fiqhi examples to illustrate, and also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about when indefinite nouns can indicate 'ahd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two functions of lam al-ta'rif
The definite article can indicate either the genus (jins) or a previously mentioned entity ('ahd).
2
Recognize when lam indicates jins
When the noun cannot logically refer to a specific entity (e.g., 'al-insan' = the human species).
Examples: 'al-insan' in Surah al-Asr, 'al-sariq' and 'al-zani'.
3
Recognize when lam indicates 'ahd
When the noun refers back to something already mentioned (e.g., 'al-rasul' after 'rasulan').
Example: Pharaoh verse: 'rasulan' then 'al-rasul'.
4
Distinguish definite vs. indefinite for 'ahd
If both mentions are definite, the second refers to the first. If both are indefinite, Abu Hanifa requires same session; the Sahibayn accept custom.
Intra-Hanafi dispute with practical implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضرب آخر: إذا دخلت لام التعريف فيما لا يحتمل التعريف بعينه لمعنى العهد، وذلك مثل قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَالْعَصْرِ إِنَّ الْإِنسَانَ لَفِي خُسْرٍ [سورة العصر]، أي هذا الجنس. وكذلك قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ [المائدة/38]، و﴿الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي [النور/2]. ومثاله : قول علمائنا رحمهم الله : المرأة التي أتزوجها : طالق. وأصل ذلك : أن لام المعرفة للعهد، وهو أن تَذكُر شيئاً، ثم تعاوده، فيكون ذلك معهوداً، قال الله تعالى: ﴿مَا أَرْسَلْنَا إِلَى فِرْعَوْنَ رَسُولًا فَعَصَى فِرْعَوْنُ الرَّسُولَ [المزمل/16]: أي هذا الذي ذكرناه فيكون الثاني هو الأول. ومثاله: قول علمائنا رحمهم الله فيمن أقرَّ بألف مقيداً بصك، ثم أقرَّ به كذلك: إن الثاني هو الأول. وإذا كان كل واحد منهما نكرة : كان الثاني غير الأول عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ، إلا أن يتحد المجلس، فيصير دلالةً على معنى العهد. وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله : يُحمَلُ الثاني على الأول وإن اختلف المجلس ؛ لدلالة العادة على معنى العهد. وذلك معنى قول ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما في قول الله تعالى: مع العسر يسرا إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا [الشرح:5-6] لن يغلب عسر واحد يسرين (١) الْعُسْرِ. (١) قوله : قول ابن عباس في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا له
Another type: When the definite article 'al' enters something that does not admit definition by itself for the purpose of 'ahd (prior knowledge). For example, Allah's statement: 'By the time, indeed mankind is in loss' [Surah al-'Asr], meaning this genus. And likewise Allah's statement: 'The male thief and the female thief' [5:38], and 'The female adulterer and the male adulterer' [24:2]. And an example: the statement of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them): 'The woman whom I marry is divorced.' And the basis of that is that the definite article is for 'ahd, which is that you mention something and then return to it, so that becomes something known. Allah said: 'We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, and Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' [73:16], meaning this one we mentioned, so the second is the first. And an example: the statement of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) regarding one who acknowledges a thousand tied to a document, then acknowledges it similarly: the second is the first. And when each of them is indefinite, the second is not the first according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), unless the session is the same, then it becomes an indication of the meaning of 'ahd. And according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), the second is considered the first even if the session differs, due to custom indicating 'ahd. And that is the meaning of the statement of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding Allah's saying: 'For indeed, with hardship comes ease. Indeed, with hardship comes ease' [94:5-6]: one hardship will never overcome two eases. [Footnote: His statement: The statement of Ibn Abbas regarding Allah's saying: 'فَإِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا' [...] ]
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage discusses another indicator of generality: the definite article 'al' when used in contexts where it cannot refer to a specific entity previously known but rather indicates the entire genus (jins) or a previously mentioned entity ('ahd). The author gives Quranic examples: 'man' (الإنسان) in Surah al-'Asr refers to the entire human species; 'the thief' (السارق) and 'the adulterer' (الزاني) refer to the class of such people. From Hanafi jurisprudence, the statement 'The woman whom I marry is divorced' means every such woman, because 'المرأة' with 'al' indicates the genus. The principle is that 'al' is primarily for 'ahd (prior reference). If a noun is defined by 'ahd, the second mention refers back to the first, as in the verse about Pharaoh: first 'a messenger' (رسولاً) indefinite, then 'the messenger' (الرسول) definite, referring to the same one. Similarly, if someone acknowledges a debt of a thousand with a document, then acknowledges it again, the second acknowledgment refers to the same debt if both mentions are definite. However, if both mentions are indefinite, Abu Hanifa says they are different unless they occur in the same session (majlis) which creates 'ahd. The Sahibayn say they are the same even in different sessions because custom ('adah) indicates that the second is intended to be the first. This is supported by Ibn Abbas's interpretation of the two 'عسر' (hardship) verses in Surah al-Sharh: the repetition indicates one hardship with two eases, using the definite article to show 'ahd.

Key Terms
لام التعريف (lam al-ta'rif): The definite article 'al' that enters nouns; it indicates either the genus (jins) or a previously mentioned entity ('ahd).
العهد (al-'ahd): Prior knowledge or mention; a definite noun refers back to something previously known or mentioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone says, 'I owe a man [indefinite] a thousand,' and then later says again, 'I owe a man [indefinite] a thousand,' when are these considered the same debt?
A) Always, because custom requires it.
B) Only if the second statement is in the same session (majlis).
C) Never, because indefinite nouns cannot refer to the same thing.
D) Only if the creditor is specified.

Key Takeaway
The definite article 'al' is a sighah al-umum when it indicates the genus, and it signals 'ahd (prior reference) when following a prior mention; the classification determines the ruling's scope.

Al-‘Ahd: The Definite Article for Previous Reference in Hanafi Usul
Defining al-‘ahd with examples from Quran and Hanafi rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-‘ahd, identify it in Arabic texts, and apply its legal implications in Hanafi rulings.

Core Idea
Al-‘ahd makes the second mention identical to the first – a single entity, not a class.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the definite article (alif lam) can function in several ways beyond simple definiteness. One critical function is al-‘ahd (previous reference), where it indicates that a noun refers back to a specific entity already mentioned in the discourse. This lesson unpacks al-‘ahd using Quranic examples and Hanafi legal maxims, showing how this linguistic tool affects rulings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of al-‘ahd
Al-‘ahd is when you mention something, then refer back to it with the definite article, making the second mention the same individual. The text defines it clearly.
2
Distinguish from al-jins
Al-‘ahd refers to a specific previously mentioned entity, while al-jins (genus) refers to the entire class. The author contrasts examples.
3
Analyze the Quranic examples
Look at the verses: al-‘Asr is given as meaning 'this genus' (jins), but the example of 'al-rasul' (the messenger) after mentioning 'rasulan' (a messenger) shows ‘ahd. Identify which is which.
4
Apply to Hanafi legal scenarios
The example of divorce: 'The woman whom I marry is divorced' uses ‘ahd to refer to a specific woman known from context. The acknowledgment example also shows that the second mention refers to the same debt.
Without prior mention, alif lam usually indicates genus; with prior mention, it indicates ‘ahd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضرب آخر: إذا دخلت لام التعريف فيما لا يحتمل التعريف بعينه لمعنى العهد، وذلك مثل قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَالْعَصْرِ إِنَّ الْإِنسَانَ لَفِي خسر سورة العصر، أي هذا الجنس.
وكذلك قول الله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ المائدة/۳۸، و الزَّانِيَةُ والزانى ( النور / ٢ .
ومثاله : قول علمائنا رحمهم الله : المرأة التي أتزوجها : طالق. وأصل ذلك : أن لام المعرفة للعهد، وهو أن تَذكُر شيئاً، ثم تعاوده،
فيكون ذلك معهوداً، قال الله تعالى: مَا أَرْسَلْنَا إِلَى فِرْعَوْنَ رَسُولًا فَعَصَى فِرْعَوْنُ الرَّسُولَ . المزمل: أي هذا الذي ذكرناه فيكون الثاني هو الأول. ومثاله: قول علمائنا رحمهم الله فيمن أقرَّ بألف مقيداً بصك، ثم أقرَّ به كذلك: إن الثاني هو الأول.
Another type: When the definite article enters into something that does not accept definition by itself for the meaning of al-ahd (previous reference). That is like the statement of Allah Most High: 'By the time, indeed man is in loss' — Surah al-Asr, meaning this genus. And likewise the statement of Allah Most High: 'The male thief and the female thief' (al-Ma'idah: 38), and 'The female fornicator and the male fornicator' (al-Nur: 2). And an example of it is the statement of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them): 'The woman whom I marry is divorced.' And the basis of that is that the definite article is for al-ahd, which is that you mention something, then you return to it, so it becomes previously referred to. Allah Most High said: 'We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, so Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' (al-Muzzammil): meaning this one whom we mentioned, so the second is the first. And an example of it is the statement of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) regarding one who acknowledged a thousand restricted with a document, then acknowledged it similarly: the second is the first.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of one function of the definite article (alif lam) in Arabic: al-ahd (reference to a previously mentioned entity). The author, al-Bazdawi, first states the type: when alif lam enters a noun that does not accept definition by itself (like abstract nouns or generic names), and is used for the meaning of 'ahd. He provides Quranic examples: 'al-'Asr' in Surah al-Asr means the genus of time, not a specific time; similarly 'al-sariq' and 'al-sariqah' in the verse on theft are generic, but in context they indicate the specific thief previously mentioned? Actually the author says these are for 'ahd? The example of 'al-'Asr' is given as meaning 'this genus' (jins), which contradicts the claim that it is for 'ahd. This may be a confusion in the text. Then he gives a Hanafi legal example: if a man says 'The woman whom I marry is divorced', the definite article indicates a specific woman previously referred to (the one he intends to marry). He explains the basis: lam al-ta'rif is for 'ahd, which means you mention something then refer back to it, so the second mention is the same as the first. He supports this with the Quranic verse about Pharaoh: first 'a messenger' (indefinite), then 'the messenger' (definite, referring back to the same messenger). Finally, he gives a legal example: if someone acknowledges a debt of a thousand restricted by a document, then acknowledges it again with the same restriction, the second acknowledgment refers to the same debt. This illustrates the principle of 'ahd in legal context.

Key Terms
لام التعريف (lām al-ta‘rīf): The definite article 'al' which can indicate either a previously mentioned entity (al-‘ahd) or the genus (al-jins).
العهد (al-‘ahd): The function of the definite article that refers back to a previously mentioned entity, making the second mention identical to the first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It indicates the genus of women.
B) It indicates a specific woman previously mentioned in the context.
C) It indicates an indefinite woman.
D) It indicates all women universally.

Key Takeaway
Al-‘ahd is a function of the definite article that identifies a second mention as the same specific entity as the first, crucial for accurate interpretation of legal texts.

Alif Lam for Genus and General Descriptions
Explaining the genus use of the definite article and another sign of generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the definite article indicates genus (jins) and when a general description (wasf 'amm) indicates generality, and apply these rules to interpret Arabic legal statements.

Core Idea
When 'ahd is impossible, alif lam indicates genus; a general description attached signals generality.

The definite article in Arabic can serve different functions. One common use is to refer to a previously mentioned entity (al-ahd). However, when such prior reference is missing, the definite article indicates the entire genus or class. This distinction is crucial for interpreting legal texts. Additionally, a general description attached to an oath or statement can also indicate that the ruling applies broadly. In this lesson, we analyze two types of indications of generality from the Hanafi usul tradition.

Guided Path
1
Determine the presence of alif lam
Identify whether the noun has the definite article.
Alif lam changes the meaning from indefinite to definite.
2
Check for 'ahd (prior reference)
Is there a specific entity previously mentioned? If yes, alif lam indicates that specific entity.
'Ahd can be of three types: dhihni, dhikri, or hadari.
3
If no 'ahd, interpret as genus
The definite article now indicates the entire class of that noun.
This is the default when no prior reference exists.
4
Identify a general description (wasf 'amm)
Look for a descriptive phrase that qualifies a noun in a general way.
The description must be general, not specific.
5
Apply the rule of generality
Such a description indicates that the ruling applies to all individuals meeting that description.
This is often seen in oaths with exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا تعذر معنى العهد: حمل على الجنس؛ ليكون تعريفاً له، مثل قولك : فلانٌ يُحبُّ الدينار أي هذا الجنس، إذ ليس فيه عين معهودة، وذلك مثل قول الرجل لامرأته : أنت طالق الطلاق.

وضرب آخر من دلائل العموم إذا اتصل بها وصف عام، مثل قول الرجل : والله لا أكلم أحداً إلا رجلاً كوفياً، ولا أتزوج امرأة إلا امرأةً
And when the meaning of al-ahd is impossible, it is taken to indicate the genus, to be a definition for it, like your statement: 'So-and-so loves the dinar,' meaning this genus, since there is no specific entity previously referred to. And that is like the statement of a man to his wife: 'You are divorced with the divorce' (i.e., the genus of divorce).

Another type of indications of generality: when a general description is attached to it, like the statement of a man: 'By Allah, I will not speak to anyone except a Kufi man, and I will not marry any woman except a woman...'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents two additional rules regarding the definite article and generality. First, when the meaning of 'ahd (reference to a previous entity) is impossible because no prior mention exists, the definite article must be interpreted as indicating the genus (jins). The author gives examples: 'He loves the dinar' means he loves the genus of dinars, not a specific coin; similarly, 'You are divorced with the divorce' means the genus of divorce (i.e., a complete divorce), not a specific type. Second, the author introduces another type of indication of generality ('umum): when a general description (wasf 'amm) is attached to a phrase, it indicates generality. The example given is an oath: 'I will not speak to anyone except a Kufi man' — the exception specifies a general class; similarly, 'I will not marry any woman except a woman...' (the text cuts off, but the implication is that the exception shows the ruling applies to all women except those meeting the condition). This relates to the usul principle that an exception (istithna) from a general statement clarifies the scope of generality.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): The genus or class; definiteness used to refer to the entire category rather than a specific member.
وصف عام (wasf 'āmm): A general description that, when attached to a statement, indicates that the ruling applies to all individuals meeting that description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It indicates the genus of divorce, i.e., a complete divorce.
C) It is an indefinite noun.
D) It indicates a particular divorce known to the speaker.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definite article indicates the genus when 'ahd is impossible, and a general description attached to a statement signals generality over its scope.
