The Word 'Ayy' and Its Generality When Described
Analyzing the word 'ayy' and its generality when described with a general attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which 'ayy' becomes general and apply it to Hanafi legal examples.

Core Idea
The word 'ayy' is indefinite but becomes general only when qualified by a general description.

The word 'ayy' is commonly misunderstood as always indicating generality. In fact, its default meaning is a single individual. This lesson clarifies that it becomes general only when described by a general attribute. We will examine linguistic consensus, Quranic evidence, and practical examples from Imam Muhammad to understand this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base meaning of 'ayy'
Recognize that 'ayy' is an indefinite (nakira) used to refer to a part of the whole to which it is added. Linguists agree on this.
The Quranic verse (27:38) uses the singular verb to show it points to one person.
2
Recognize the condition for generality
When 'ayy' is described by a general attribute (e.g., 'struck you'), it becomes general, covering all individuals who meet that description.
Contrast with a severed description where it remains specific.
3
Examine the example of Imam Muhammad
If a master says 'Any of my slaves who struck you is free' and all struck, all become free. But if he says 'Any of my slaves I struck is free' and strikes all, only one is freed.
The first example attaches a general description; the second severs it.
4
Extend to the heavy wood example
If a task cannot be done by one, and all do it together, all qualify. If one can do it alone but all cooperate, none qualify because the intent is individual action.
Context and custom (urf) affect the application of generality.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Given a statement using 'ayy', determine whether a general description is present and then derive the ruling.
Always check if the description is attached or severed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا الضرب كلمة أي وهي نكرة يُراد بها جزء مما تُضاف إليه، على هذا إجماع أهل اللغة. قال الله تعالى: أَيُّكُمْ يَأْتِينِي بِعَرشِهَا ﴾ النمل/٣٨: ولم يقل: يأتونني. ويقال: أي الرجال أتاك ؟ وقال محمد رحمه الله : أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر، فضربوه : فإنهم يعتقون، ولم يقل : ضربوك. فثبت أنها كلمة فرد لكنها متى وُصفت بصفة عامة : عمت بعمومها، كسائر النكرات في موضع الإثبات. وإذا قال: أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر فقد وصفها بالضرب، وصارت عامة. وإذا قال : أي عبيدي ضربته فهو حر فقد قطع الوصف عنها، فلم يعتق إلا واحد. وعلى ذلك مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله. وكذلك إذا قال: أيكم حَمَلَ هذه الخشبة فهو حر، وهي لا يحملها واحد، فحملوها : عَتَقوا. وإن كان يحملها واحد، فحملوا كلّهم فرادى عَتَقوا وإذا اجتمعوا على ذلك: لم يعتقوا ؛ لأن المراد به فيما يَحْفُ حَمْلُه : انفرادُ كلِّ واحدٍ منهم في العادة؛ لإظهار الجلادة.
And among this type is the word 'ayy', which is an indefinite (nakira) intended to indicate a part of what it is added to. On this there is consensus of the linguists. Allah Almighty said: 'Which of you will bring me her throne?' (al-Naml 27:38) and He did not say: 'You will bring me.' And it is said: 'Which of the men came to you?' And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'Whichever of my slaves struck you is free,' and they struck him: then all of them become free, and he did not say: 'They struck you.' So it is established that it is an indefinite word, but whenever it is described by a general description, it becomes general by virtue of that generality, like other indefinites in affirmative contexts. And if he says: 'Whichever of my slaves struck you is free,' he has described it with striking, and it becomes general. And if he says: 'Whichever of my slaves I struck is free,' he has severed the description from it, so only one is freed. And upon this are the issues of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them). Similarly, if he says: 'Whoever of you carries this piece of wood is free,' and it cannot be carried by one person, and they carry it: all become free. And if it can be carried by one person, and they each carry it individually, they become free. And if they cooperate in carrying it, they do not become free, because what is intended in something that is light to carry is the individual action of each one normally, to show strength.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage discusses the word 'ayy' (أي), a type of indefinite noun used to refer to a part of the whole to which it is added. The author begins by stating the consensus of linguists that 'ayy' is an indefinite intended for a part. He provides evidence from the Quran: 'أيكم يأتيني بعرشها' (Which of you will bring me her throne?) using the singular verb rather than plural, confirming it refers to a single individual. Similarly, in Arabic speech: 'أي الرجال أتاك' (Which of the men came to you?) uses singular. Then a legal example from Imam Muhammad: if a master says 'أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر' (Whichever of my slaves struck you is free), and all slaves struck him, then all are freed because the description 'striking' is general. But if he says 'أي عبيدي ضربته فهو حر' (Whichever of my slaves I struck is free), the description is cut off, so only one is freed. The rule is: when 'ayy' is qualified by a general description, it becomes general like other indefinites in affirmative contexts. The passage then gives an example of carrying a heavy piece of wood: if it cannot be carried by one, and all carry it together, they all become free because each contributed; but if one could carry it alone, and they all carry individually, they are free; however, if they cooperate when one could do it, none is free because the intent is individual action. This shows the nuance: generality or specificity depends on context and the nature of the action.

Key Terms
أي (ayy): An indefinite word used to ask about or indicate a part of a set.
نكرة (nakira): An indefinite noun, opposed to definite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) None become free.
D) The one who was beaten first becomes free.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ayy' is inherently indefinite and specific; it only becomes general when qualified by a general description.

The Singular Indefinite Noun: Hanafi 'Mutlaq' vs Shafi'i 'Amm'
Dispute on singular indefinite nouns: mutlaq vs 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school classifies singular indefinite nouns in affirmative contexts as mutlaq rather than 'amm, and apply this principle to derive the ruling on the expiation for zihar.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a singular indefinite noun is absolute (mutlaq), not general ('amm)—it can be restricted and its repetition follows the cause.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the nature of the singular indefinite noun (nakira mufrada) in affirmative contexts. The Hanafi school, following Imam Abu Hanifa, holds that such nouns are mutlaq (absolute) and do not indicate generality, while Imam al-Shafi'i argues that they do. The practical consequence is seen in the expiation for zihar: does 'raqaba' (slave) include non-Muslim slaves? Understanding this methodological difference will sharpen your ability to trace how usul principles yield divergent fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the contested ruling
Identify the exact point of dispute: the linguistic category of singular indefinite nouns (nakira mufrada) in affirmative contexts—are they 'amm (general) or mutlaq (absolute)?
The verse فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ (al-Mujadilah 58:3) is the key evidence.
2
Identify each school's position and proof
State the Hanafi position: it is mutlaq, not 'amm. State the Shafi'i position: it is 'amm and obligates generality. Note each school's dalil from the same verse.
Shafi'i claims consensus on excluding the chronically ill supports generality.
3
Trace the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument
Explain how the Hanafis rebut: the term 'raqaba' is a name for a complete body, so it does not include the chronically ill; the absolute can be restricted; the term is an individual (fard), not general.
The response uses linguistic analysis and the principle of mutlaq.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical divergence)
Identify the practical fiqh difference: Hanafis require a Muslim slave for expiation; Shafi'is allow any slave except the chronically ill, based on generality and qiyas on expiation for killing.
5
Reflect on the usul method
Consider how this dispute illustrates different hermeneutical approaches: Hanafi prioritizes the inherent nature of the word as absolute; Shafi'i prioritizes apparent generality in affirmative contexts.
This shapes how other texts with singular indefinites are interpreted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النكرة المفردة في موضع الإثبات فإنها تخص عندنا، ولا تعم، إلا أنها مطلقة. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : هي توجب العموم أيضاً، حتى قال في قول الله تعالى: فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ الله المجادلة / ۳ : إنها عامة، تتناول الصغيرة والكبيرة، والبيضاء والسوداء، والكافرة والمؤمنة، والصحيحة والزَّمنة، وقد خُص منها الزمنةُ بالإجماع، فصح تخصيص الكافرة منها بالقياس على كفارة القتل. قلنا نحن هذه مطلَقةٌ، لا عامة؛ لأنها فرد، فيتناول واحداً على احتمال وصف دون وصف والمطلق يحتمل التقييد، وذلك مانع من العمل بالمطلق، فصار نسخاً، وقد جعل وجوب التحرير جزاءً لأمر. فصار ذلك سبباً له فيتكرر مطلقاً بتكرر سببه، وصار مقيداً بالملك؛ لاقتضاء التحرير الملك لا على جهة الخصوص. ولم يتناول الزَّمِنَةَ ؛ لأن الرقبة اسمٌ للبنية مطلقاً، فوقعت على هالك من وجه. الكامل من الذي هو موجود مطلق، فلم يتناول ما هو وكذلك التحرير المطلق لا يخلص فيما هو يدخل الزَّمن، فأما أن يكون مخصوصاً: فلا، والله أعلم. هالك من وجه، فلم وصار ما ينتهي إليه الخصوص نوعين الواحد منهما فيما هو فرد بصيغته، أو ملحق بالفرد.
As for the singular indefinite (nakira mufrada) in an affirmative context, according to us (Hanafis) it is specific (takhassus), not general, but it is absolute (mutlaq). Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It also obligates generality, to the extent that he said regarding the saying of Allah Almighty: 'So freeing a slave' (al-Mujadilah 58:3) that it is general, including the small and the big, the white and the black, the disbelieving and the believing, the healthy and the chronically ill, and the chronically ill has been excluded by consensus, so the exclusion of the disbelieving slave is valid by analogy to the expiation for killing. We (Hanafis) say: This is absolute (mutlaq), not general, because it is an individual (fard), so it applies to one with the probability of some description over another, and the absolute can be restricted, and that prevents acting upon the absolute; so it becomes like abrogation (naskh). And He made the obligation of freeing a recompense for a matter, so that becomes a cause for it, and it repeats absolutely with the repetition of its cause, and it is restricted by ownership (milk), because freeing requires ownership not as a specification. And it does not include the chronically ill, because 'raqaba' is a name for the body absolutely, and it applies to what is perishing in one aspect. The perfect one which exists absolutely, so it does not apply to what is perishing. Similarly, absolute freeing does not benefit what is in a state of being perishing from one aspect, so if it is not specific: no, and Allah knows best. And what the specificity reaches are two types: one is what is an individual by its form, or what is attached to an individual.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental dispute in usul al-fiqh between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the singular indefinite noun (nakira mufrada) in affirmative contexts. The Hanafi position, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and his school, is that such a noun is not general ('amm) but is absolute (mutlaq). That is, it indicates a single individual without extending to all individuals of its kind. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i holds that it indicates generality ('umum) unless there is evidence to specify it. The Shafi'i proof is from the Quranic verse 'فتحرير رقبة' (so freeing a slave) in Surah al-Mujadilah. They argue that the word 'raqaba' (slave) is general, covering all types of slaves, and therefore includes believers and disbelievers, healthy and sick, etc. They acknowledge that the chronically ill (zamina) is excluded by consensus, and they extend that to exclude the disbelieving slave by analogy to the expiation for killing. The Hanafi response is that 'raqaba' is not general but absolute. It is a singular indefinite (fard), which can be restricted. They argue that the absolute nature means it applies to one individual with the possibility of any description, and it can be qualified by conditions. In the case of expiation, the cause (sabab) repeats with each occurrence, and the freeing is restricted by the requirement of ownership (milk). Furthermore, they argue that a chronic illness prevents the slave from being considered a perfect 'raqaba' because the term applies to a complete body existing absolutely, not a partially defective one. The passage concludes by stating that specificity (khushus) is of two types: one that is individual by its form (fard bi-sighati) and one attached to the individual (mulhaq bi-l-fard). This dispute has practical implications: in Hanafi fiqh, the word 'raqaba' in expiation requires a Muslim slave, while Shafi'is allow a non-Muslim slave based on the generality.

Key Terms
نكرة مفردة (nakira mufrada): A singular indefinite noun (e.g., رقبة, رجل, etc.)
مطلق (mutlaq): An absolute term that is unrestricted and can be qualified by conditions.
عام (aamm): A general term that applies to all individuals of its kind without exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the status of the word 'رقبة' (slave) in the Quranic verse 'فتحرير رقبة'?
A) It is a general term (عام) that includes all types of slaves.
B) It is an absolute term (مطلق) that can be restricted.
C) It is a specific term (خاص) that refers only to Muslim slaves.
D) It is a metaphorical term (مجاز).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a singular indefinite noun in an affirmative context is mutlaq, not 'amm, and its meaning can be restricted by evidence.

Minimum of Plural: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on Three or Two
Determining that the minimum plural is three according to Hanafis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi ruling on the minimum of a plural (three) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school defines the plural minimum as three, not two, based on linguistic precision and the authority of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

This lesson examines the foundational usul dispute over the minimum number of a plural (جمع). While some Shafi'i scholars argue that two is sufficient, the Hanafi school insists on three. This difference affects how conditional statements and Quranic texts are interpreted, making it a key point of ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Hanafi definition of plural
Identify that the Hanafi school defines a true plural as three or more, based on the explicit statement of Imam Muhammad in al-Siyar al-Kabir.
This applies to words plural in both form and meaning (e.g., 'عبيد', 'نساء'), not to words like 'طائفة' which can be singular.
2
Identify the Shafi'i proofs
Examine the hadith 'Two and above are a congregation' and the Quranic verse using 'ikhwa' (brothers) for two, and the consensus on plural applying to dual in inheritance.
These proofs are presented as evidence that the minimum plural is two.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafi school rebuts: the hadith uses 'جماعة' which is not synonymous with 'جمع', and the Quranic usage is contextual, not a minimum rule.
The response is based on linguistic differentiation and the authority of Hanafi imams.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply the dispute to a practical example: in a conditional statement like 'if I buy slaves', the condition is fulfilled with three slaves in Hanafi fiqh, but with two in the Shafi'i view.
This shows the real-world impact of the usul difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما كان جمعاً صيغةً ومعنى: مثل قوله: إن اشتريت عبيداً، أو: إن تزوجتُ نساءً، أو: إن اشتريتُ ثياباً فإن ذلك يحتمل الخصوص إلى الثلاثة. والطائفة تحتمل الخصوص إلى الواحد. بخلاف الرَّهْط، والقوم، وهذا لأن أدنى الجمع: ثلاثة، نص محمد رحمه الله على ذلك في السير الكبير، وعلى هذا عامة مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله.
وقال بعض أصحاب الشافعي: إن أدنى الجمع: اثنان؛ لما روي عن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه قال: الاثنان فما فوقهما: جماعة. ولأن اسم الإخوة ينطلق على الاثنين في قوله تعالى: فَإِن كَانَ لَهُ إِخْوَةٌ فَلِأُمِّهِ السُّدُسُ النساء/11. وفي المواريث والوصايا يُصرف الجمع إلى المُثنّى، بالإجماع.
As for what is plural in form and meaning: like his saying: 'If I buy slaves', or 'if I marry women', or 'if I buy clothes' – that can be specified down to three. And 'طائفة' can be specified down to one. Unlike 'رهط' and 'قوم', and this is because the minimum of plural is three. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) explicitly stated that in al-Siyar al-Kabir, and upon this are the majority of the cases of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them). Some of the companions of al-Shafi'i said: The minimum of plural is two, because it is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Two and above are a congregation'. And because the term 'ikhwa' (brothers) applies to two in the saying of Allah Almighty: 'If he has brothers, then for his mother the sixth' (4:11). And in inheritance and bequests, the plural is applied to the dual by consensus.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage addresses the minimum number required for a plural (جمع) in Arabic usul al-fiqh. The Hanafi position, as articulated by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Siyar al-Kabir, is that the minimum plural is three. This applies to words that are plural in both form and meaning, such as 'عبيد', 'نساء', 'ثياب'. These can be specified (تخصيص) down to a minimum of three individuals. However, words like 'طائفة' (a group) can be specified down to one, as they are not true plurals. Words like 'رهط' and 'قوم' are treated differently because they refer to a group of unspecified number. The Hanafi ruling is supported by general principles and is the basis of most Hanafi cases. The opposing view from some Shafi'i scholars claims that the minimum plural is two, citing a hadith that 'two and above are a congregation' and the Quranic use of 'ikhwa' (brothers) for two, as well as the consensus in inheritance and bequests that plural can refer to the dual. The Hanafi response would hold that the hadith is weak or that 'جماعة' is not synonymous with 'جمع' linguistically, and that the Quranic usage does not prove the minimum, as 'ikhwa' can apply to more than two. The Hanafi school maintains three as the minimum for true plurals.

Key Terms
أدنى الجمع (adnā al-jamʿ): The minimum number of individuals covered by a plural word.
طائفة (ṭāʾifa): A group, can be specified down to one person.
جمع (jamʿ): Plural; a word indicating more than one in form and meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum number for a true plural (جمع) in conditional statements?
A) Two
B) Three
C) One
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the minimum of a true plural is three, a ruling derived from linguistic principles and the authority of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

The Minimum Number for Plural: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute on whether plural minimum is three or two

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the minimum number for a plural (jama') and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-arguments using linguistic consensus and rational proof.

Core Idea
The Hanafi plural begins at three; two is a distinct category.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute: what is the minimum number that a plural (جمع) must cover when used in legal texts? The Hanafi school insists on three, while some Shafi'is argue for two. The difference affects how general commands are interpreted and specified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Position and Its Basis
State the Hanafi ruling that the minimum for a true plural (جمع صيغةً ومعنى) is three. Note that this is based on explicit statements of Imam Muhammad and the general practice of the Hanafi school.
The term 'جمع' here refers to the linguistic plural that can be specified (تخصيص) down to three.
2
Present the Opposing Shafi'i Position and Its Proofs
Outline the Shafi'i claim that the minimum is two. List their six proofs: hadith about 'two and above are a congregation', Quranic usage of 'ikhwah' for two, consensus in inheritance, linguistic usage of dual as plural, imamate with two followers, and the concept of assembly in dual.
Pay attention to the distinction between linguistic categories and practical rulings.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response to Each Shafi'i Proof
For each Shafi'i proof, explain how the Hanafi school responds. Focus on the key arguments: the hadith about congregation is about prayer, not linguistics; linguistic consensus clearly separates dual from plural; rational proof that true assembly requires a third.
The Hanafi response relies on the precise meaning of 'جماعة' in the hadith.
4
Note the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Describe the real-world impact: if someone says 'I sell my slaves', a Hanafi interprets it as three or more, while a Shafi'i might consider two or more. This affects legal contracts and oaths.
This difference illustrates how usul disputes translate into fiqh rulings.
5
Understand the Tarjih (Preferred View) According to al-Bazdawi
Explain that al-Bazdawi favors the Hanafi position, supported by linguistic consensus and rational proof. The Shafi'i arguments are either reinterpreted or weaker in terms of dalala.
The tarjih is based on strength of evidence, not mere school affiliation.

أما الفرد فمثلُ : الرجل والمرأة والإنسان والطعام والشراب وما أشبه ذلك : أن الخصوص يصح إلى أن يبقى الواحد.
وأما الفرد بمعناه : فمثل قوله : لا يتزوج النساء، ولا يشتري العبيد: أنه يصح الخصوص حتى يبقى الواحد.
وأما ما كان جمعاً صيغةً ومعنى: مثل قوله : إن اشتريت عبيداً، أو: إن تزوجتُ نساءً، أو : إن اشتريتُ ثياباً فإن ذلك يحتمل الخصوص إلى الثلاثة.
والطائفة تحتمل الخصوص إلى الواحد.

وفي المواريث والوصايا يُصرف الجمع إلى المُثنّى، بالإجماع.
ويستعمل المثنى استعمال الجمع اللغة، يقال: نحن فعلنا الاثنين.
وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿فَقَدْ صَغَتْ قُلُوبُكُمَا﴾ التحريم / ٤ .
ولا خلاف أن الإمام يتقدم إذا كان خلفه اثنان.
وفي المثنى اجتماع، كما في الثلاثة.
ولنا قول النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام : «الواحد شيطان، والاثنان شیطانان، والثلاثة ركب».
ولنا أيضاً دليل من قبل الإجماع، ودليل من قبل المعقول.
أما من قبل الإجماع : فإن أهل اللغة مُجمعون على أن الكلام ثلاثة أقسام : آحاد ،ومثنى وجمع، وعلى ذلك بنيت أحكام اللغة.
فللمثنى صيغة خاصةً لا تختلف. وللوحدان أبنية مختلفة.
وكذلك الجمع أيضاً تختلف أبنيته ، وليس للمثنى إلا مثال واحد، وله علامات على الخصوص.
وأجمع الفقهاء أن الإمام لا يتقدم على الواحد، فثبت أنه قسم منفرد.
وأما المعقول : فإن الواحد إذا أُضيف إليه الواحد : تعارض الفردان، فلم يحصل الاجتماع إلا في الثالث.
As for the singular, such as: man, woman, human, food, drink, and the like: that specification is valid until one remains. And as for the singular in its meaning: such as his statement: 'He does not marry women, nor buy slaves': that specification is valid until one remains. And as for what is a plural in form and meaning: such as his statement: 'If I buy slaves', or 'If I marry women', or 'If I buy garments' – then that admits specification down to three. And 'ṭā'ifah' admits specification down to one. Unlike 'raht' and 'qawm'. And this is because the minimum of the plural is three; Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) explicitly stated that in al-Siyar al-Kabir, and upon this are the general issues of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them). And some followers of al-Shafi'i said: the minimum of the plural is two; due to what was narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Two and above are a congregation'. And because the term 'ikhwah' applies to two in His statement: 'But if he has brothers, then for his mother the sixth' (Quran 4:11). And in inheritance and bequests, the plural is referred to the dual by consensus. And the dual is used in the usage of the plural in the language; it is said: 'We both did'. And Allah said: 'Your hearts have inclined' (Quran 66:4). And there is no dispute that the imam leads when there are two behind him. And in the dual there is assembly, just as in three. And we have the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'One is a devil, two are devils, and three are a riding party'. And we also have proof from consensus, and proof from reason. As for consensus: the linguists are unanimous that speech has three categories: singular, dual, and plural, and upon that the rulings of language are built. For the dual has a specific form that does not vary. For singulars their constructions vary. Likewise, the plural also varies in its constructions, while the dual has only one pattern and has specific markers. And the jurists are unanimous that the imam does not lead one person, so it is established that it (the dual) is a separate category. As for reason: when one is added to one, the two individuals oppose each other, so assembly is not achieved except in the third.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute concerning the minimum number required for a 'plural' (جمع) in Arabic. The Hanafi position, as articulated by al-Bazdawi, is that the minimum is three. This is based on explicit statements from Imam Muhammad in al-Siyar al-Kabir and is the foundation of many Hanafi rulings. The opposing view, attributed to some Shafi'i scholars, holds that the minimum is two. They cite a hadith that 'two and above are a congregation' (جماعة) and that the Quranic term 'ikhwah' (brothers) applies to two in the verse of inheritance. Additionally, they argue that in inheritance rulings the plural is interpreted as dual by consensus, and that the dual is used like a plural in language. They also note that the imam can lead with two followers, and that the dual implies 'assembly' just as three does. Al-Bazdawi then presents the Hanafi counterarguments: First, the hadith about 'two are devils' supports that real assembly begins with three. Second, there is consensus among linguists that speech is divided into singular, dual, and plural; the dual has a unique form, while plurals have varied patterns – thus the dual is a distinct category. Third, by rational reasoning, adding one to one results in two opposing individuals, not a unified group; the 'coming together' (اجتماع) only occurs with a third. Thus, the Hanafi school maintains that the minimum for جمع is three.

Key Terms
جمع (jama‘): A linguistic category indicating three or more individuals (Hanafi view).
مثنى (muthannā): A linguistic category for exactly two, with a specific form and markers distinct from plural.
أدنى الجمع (adnā al-jam‘): The smallest number that constitutes a plural; according to Hanafis, three.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines plural as three or more, distinguishing it from the dual, affecting how general commands are specified.

Rulings of Mushtarak and Mu'awwal in Hanafi Usul
Stating the usul rulings for mushtarak and mu'awwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the usul rulings for mushtarak and mu'awwal, and apply the condition of tawaqquf to mushtarak terms.

Core Idea
Mushtarak requires suspension until tarjih; Mu'awwal is acted upon despite error possibility.

In Hanafi usul, not all texts are immediately actionable. Some words carry multiple meanings (mushtarak), requiring investigation before use. Others (mu'awwal) have been interpreted from their apparent meaning and are actioned despite potential error. This lesson anchors these two foundational rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term type
Determine whether you are dealing with a mushtarak (a word with multiple possible meanings) or a mu'awwal (a text interpreted away from its apparent meaning).
This classification is the first step in applying the correct ruling.
2
Apply the ruling for mushtarak
If the term is mushtarak, you must suspend judgment (tawaqquf) and engage in reflection (ta'mmul) to find a preponderant meaning before acting.
Suspension is mandatory; immediate action on any meaning is invalid.
3
Apply the ruling for mu'awwal
If the text is mu'awwal, you act upon its interpreted meaning, while acknowledging the possibility of oversight or error.
Action is obligatory in practice, but the ruling remains zanni (probable).
4
Understand the epistemological status
Both rulings produce probable knowledge (zann), not certainty. Mushtarak leads to zann after tarjih; mu'awwal is acted upon despite the chance of being wrong.
This differentiates them from definitive texts (qat'i).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشترك فحكمه : التوقف بشرط التأمل؛ ليترجح بعض وجوهه للعمل به. وأما المؤول فحكمه العمل به على احتمال السهو والغلط، والله أعلم بالصواب.
As for the homonym (mushtarak), its ruling is suspension (tawaqquf) with the condition of reflection, so that one of its meanings may become preponderant for acting upon it. As for the interpreted (mu'awwal), its ruling is acting upon it with the possibility of oversight and error. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental usul rule regarding ambiguous terms. The mushtarak (word with multiple meanings) cannot be acted upon until after investigation (tawqif) to determine the likely meaning. In contrast, the mu'awwal (a text that has been interpreted from its apparent meaning based on evidence) is acted upon, even though there remains a possibility of error. This reflects the Hanafi approach to textual interpretation, emphasizing careful analysis before application. The ruling on mushtarak is that one must suspend judgment and reflect to favor one meaning. For mu'awwal, one proceeds with action despite the potential for mistake, relying on the preponderance of evidence.

Key Terms
المشترك (al-mushtarak): A linguistic term with multiple possible meanings that requires suspension before application.
المؤول (al-mu'awwal): A text that has been interpreted away from its apparent meaning based on a valid evidence, and is acted upon despite possibility of error.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Suspend judgment until investigation
C) Reject it entirely
D) Interpret it figuratively

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak requires suspension until preponderance, while mu'awwal is acted upon despite error.

Definitions of Haqiqah and Majaz and the Conflict Rule
Defines haqiqah and majaz, and discusses preference in conflict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of haqiqah and majaz according to Hanafi usul and apply the rule that haqiqah takes precedence over majaz when they conflict.

Core Idea
Haqiqah is literal use; majaz is borrowed. When they conflict, the literal meaning prevails.

This passage introduces the foundational concepts of haqiqah (literal truth) and majaz (metaphor) in Hanafi legal theory. Understanding these terms is crucial for interpreting the Qur'an and Sunnah correctly. The author defines both, explains how they are known, and establishes a key rule: when a text can be read both literally and metaphorically, the literal meaning is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the definitions of haqiqah and majaz in the text. Note that haqiqah is defined as 'existence of what it was coined for' and majaz as 'existence of what it was borrowed for'.
2
Understand the methods of knowing
The text says haqiqah is known through 'tawqif' (divine determination) and 'sama'' (hearing), like the nusus. Majaz is known through 'ta'ammul' (reflection) on contexts where haqiqah is impossible.
3
Grasp the equality in ruling
Note that in terms of legal effect, haqiqah and majaz are equal: both can be used to derive rulings.
4
Apply the conflict rule
When they conflict, haqiqah is preferred. This means if a word has both a literal and metaphorical meaning, the literal meaning is taken unless there is a clear indication (qarinah) to the contrary.
5
Note the practical implication
This rule is applied throughout fiqh, e.g., in interpreting commands and prohibitions. For example, the command 'Aqimu al-salah' is taken literally as ritual prayer because no qarinah suggests otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب أحكام الحقيقة والمجاز والصريح والكناية. قال: حكم الحقيقة وجود ما وضع له أمراً كان أو نهياً، خاصاً كان أو عاماً. وحكم المجاز وجود ما استُعير له، خاصاً كان أو عاماً. وطريق معرفة الحقيقة : التوقيف والسماع، بمنزلة النصوص. وطريق معرفة المجاز : التأمل في مواضع الحقائق. وأما في الحكم فهما سواء، إلا عند التعارض : فإن الحقيقة أولى منه. ومن أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله من قال : لا عموم للمجاز. وبيان ذلك أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء» (١) ، فاحتج الشافعي رحمه الله (١) بعمومه، وأبى أن (١) باب أحكام الحقيقة والمجاز حديث لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء: أخرجه الشافعي في مسنده بلفظ : «لا تبيعوا الذهب بالذهب، ولا الورق بالورق، ولا البُرَّ بالبرّ، ولا الشعير بالشعير، ولا التمر بالتمر، ولا الملح بالملح، إلا سواء بسواء». هذا لفظه في حديث عبادة، وللشيخين من حديث أبي بكرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه نهى عن الفضة بالفضة والذهب بالذهب، إلا سواء بسواء». (ملاحظة: قدمت النص كما هو مع الحاشية)
Chapter on the rulings of truth and metaphor, and explicit and implicit. He said: The ruling of truth (haqiqah) is the existence of what it was coined for, whether it be a command or a prohibition, whether particular or general. And the ruling of metaphor (majaz) is the existence of what it was borrowed for, whether particular or general. And the method of knowing truth is through divine determination and hearing, like the texts. And the method of knowing metaphor is through reflection on the places of truths. As for the ruling, they are equal, except when they conflict, then truth is more worthy than it. And among the followers of al-Shafi‘i - may Allah have mercy on him - some said: There is no generality (umum) for metaphor. And the clarification of that is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' So al-Shafi‘i argued by its generality, and he refused that... (Footnote: Chapter on rulings of truth and metaphor, hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal': Al-Shafi‘i narrated in his Musnad with the wording: 'Do not sell gold for gold, nor silver for silver, nor wheat for wheat, nor barley for barley, nor dates for dates, nor salt for salt, except equal for equal.' This is his wording in the hadith of ‘Ubadah, and the two Shaykhs [have] from the hadith of Abu Bakra from the Prophet that he forbade silver for silver and gold for gold, except equal for equal.')
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter on the concepts of haqiqah (truth/literal meaning) and majaz (metaphor) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first defines haqiqah as a word used for its original linguistic meaning, whether in commands or prohibitions, general or specific. Majaz is defined as a word used for a borrowed meaning. The method to know haqiqah is through divine revelation (tawqif) and hearing (sama'), akin to definitive texts (nusus). The method to know majaz is through reflection on the contexts where haqiqah is impossible. In terms of legal ruling, haqiqah and majaz are equal, meaning both can be used to derive rulings. However, when they conflict, haqiqah takes precedence (haqiqah is preferred). The text then presents a dispute: some Shafi'i scholars claim that majaz does not have 'umum' (general applicability). To illustrate, the author cites the Prophet's hadith: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' Al-Shafi'i used the generality of this hadith (i.e., it includes all types of food), but the Hanafi school (indicated by 'أبى أن' - he refused) objected to that generality, because the word 'food' (ta'am) is haqiqah only in certain types (like wheat and barley) and majaz in others; thus it is not general. This shows the practical implication: for Hanafis, the prohibition of riba in food applies only to specific items specified in other hadith, not all foods generically.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): استعمال اللفظ فيما وضع له في أصل اللغة أو الشرع
المجاز (al-majaz): استعمال اللفظ في غير ما وضع له لعلاقة بينهما مع قرينة مانعة من إرادة المعنى الأصلي
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): هل يحمل لفظ المجاز على الاستغراق أم لا؟

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When haqiqah and majaz conflict in a legal text, which does the Hanafi school prefer?
A) Majaz
B) Haqiqah
C) Neither (suspend judgment)
D) The more commonly used meaning

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, haqiqah (literal meaning) is the default and is preferred over majaz (metaphor) when both are possible and no qarinah excludes the literal.

The Dispute over Umum al-Majaz: The Hanafi Position
Discusses preference in conflict and introduces dispute on umum al-majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that majaz has no generality (umum) and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument using the hadith on selling food.

Core Idea
Majaz lacks generality; thus a metaphorical term does not cover all its possible referents.

This passage addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether metaphorical expressions can have general applicability (umum). The Hanafi position is that majaz has no umum, meaning that if a word is used metaphorically, it does not automatically encompass all individuals or types that fall under that metaphor. The proof is the hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal,' which Shafi'i used to prohibit all food exchanges with inequality, but Hanafis argue that 'food' is only a metaphor for certain specific grains, not a general term.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that majaz has no umum (generality).
2
Identify the Shafi'i challenge
Some Shafi'is claimed that majaz can have umum, using the hadith as evidence.
3
Examine the hadith
The Prophet said, 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' The word 'ta'am' (food) was argued to be general.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The word 'ta'am' is haqiqah only for specific items (wheat, barley) and majaz for others. Since it's majaz, it has no generality, so the prohibition does not extend to all foods.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis allow selling dates for wheat with inequality (subject to other evidence), while Shafi'is prohibit it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب أحكام الحقيقة والمجاز والصريح والكناية. قال: حكم الحقيقة وجود ما وضع له أمراً كان أو نهياً، خاصاً كان أو عاماً. وحكم المجاز وجود ما استُعير له، خاصاً كان أو عاماً. وطريق معرفة الحقيقة : التوقيف والسماع، بمنزلة النصوص. وطريق معرفة المجاز : التأمل في مواضع الحقائق. وأما في الحكم فهما سواء، إلا عند التعارض : فإن الحقيقة أولى منه. ومن أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله من قال : لا عموم للمجاز. وبيان ذلك أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء» (١) ، فاحتج الشافعي رحمه الله (١) بعمومه، وأبى أن (١) باب أحكام الحقيقة والمجاز حديث لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء: أخرجه الشافعي في مسنده بلفظ : «لا تبيعوا الذهب بالذهب، ولا الورق بالورق، ولا البُرَّ بالبرّ، ولا الشعير بالشعير، ولا التمر بالتمر، ولا الملح بالملح، إلا سواء بسواء». هذا لفظه في حديث عبادة، وللشيخين من حديث أبي بكرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه نهى عن الفضة بالفضة والذهب بالذهب، إلا سواء بسواء». (ملاحظة: قدمت النص كما هو مع الحاشية)
Chapter on the rulings of truth and metaphor, and explicit and implicit. He said: The ruling of truth (haqiqah) is the existence of what it was coined for, whether it be a command or a prohibition, whether particular or general. And the ruling of metaphor (majaz) is the existence of what it was borrowed for, whether particular or general. And the method of knowing truth is through divine determination and hearing, like the texts. And the method of knowing metaphor is through reflection on the places of truths. As for the ruling, they are equal, except when they conflict, then truth is more worthy than it. And among the followers of al-Shafi‘i - may Allah have mercy on him - some said: There is no generality (umum) for metaphor. And the clarification of that is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' So al-Shafi‘i argued by its generality, and he refused that... (Footnote: Chapter on rulings of truth and metaphor, hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal': Al-Shafi‘i narrated in his Musnad with the wording: 'Do not sell gold for gold, nor silver for silver, nor wheat for wheat, nor barley for barley, nor dates for dates, nor salt for salt, except equal for equal.' This is his wording in the hadith of ‘Ubadah, and the two Shaykhs [have] from the hadith of Abu Bakra from the Prophet that he forbade silver for silver and gold for gold, except equal for equal.')
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter on the concepts of haqiqah (truth/literal meaning) and majaz (metaphor) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author first defines haqiqah as a word used for its original linguistic meaning, whether in commands or prohibitions, general or specific. Majaz is defined as a word used for a borrowed meaning. The method to know haqiqah is through divine revelation (tawqif) and hearing (sama'), akin to definitive texts (nusus). The method to know majaz is through reflection on the contexts where haqiqah is impossible. In terms of legal ruling, haqiqah and majaz are equal, meaning both can be used to derive rulings. However, when they conflict, haqiqah takes precedence (haqiqah is preferred). The text then presents a dispute: some Shafi'i scholars claim that majaz does not have 'umum' (general applicability). To illustrate, the author cites the Prophet's hadith: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' Al-Shafi'i used the generality of this hadith (i.e., it includes all types of food), but the Hanafi school (indicated by 'أبى أن' - he refused) objected to that generality, because the word 'food' (ta'am) is haqiqah only in certain types (like wheat and barley) and majaz in others; thus it is not general. This shows the practical implication: for Hanafis, the prohibition of riba in food applies only to specific items specified in other hadith, not all foods generically.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): استعمال اللفظ فيما وضع له في أصل اللغة أو الشرع
المجاز (al-majaz): استعمال اللفظ في غير ما وضع له لعلاقة بينهما مع قرينة مانعة من إرادة المعنى الأصلي
عموم المجاز (umum al-majaz): هل يحمل لفظ المجاز على الاستغراق أم لا؟

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the hadith 'لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء' not prohibit the exchange of dates for wheat in unequal quantities based on this hadith alone?
A) Because the hadith is weak
B) Because 'food' (ta'am) in this hadith is a metaphor for dates, and metaphors have no generality
C) Because the hadith only applies to gold and silver
D) Because the Hanafi school rejects this hadith

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that metaphorical expressions (majaz) do not carry general applicability, a principle that significantly impacts the scope of prohibitions like riba al-fadl.

Definitions and Rulings of Haqiqah and Majaz
Definitions and rulings of literal and figurative speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define haqiqah and majaz, state their rulings, and identify the method of knowing each.

Core Idea
Haqiqah and majaz are equally valid for rulings except when they conflict, where haqiqah takes precedence.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces the foundational categories of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative) speech. Understanding their definitions and how they are identified is crucial for interpreting legal texts. The passage also establishes that in terms of legal effect, both are on par unless they directly conflict.

Guided Path
1
Define haqiqah and majaz
Haqiqah is a word used for its original meaning, majaz is used for a meaning borrowed from the original due to a connection.
Both can be specific or general.
2
Identify the method of knowing each
Haqiqah is known through tawqif (revelation) and sam'a (transmission); majaz through reflection (ta'ammul) on contexts of literal meanings.
These are the primary sources for determining usage.
3
Understand the ruling of each
The ruling of haqiqah is the existence of what it was coined for; the ruling of majaz is the existence of what it was borrowed for.
Both produce legal effects.
4
Apply the precedence rule
When haqiqah and majaz conflict, haqiqah takes precedence because it is the origin of speech.
This is the default rule unless evidence overrides.
5
Note the scope for generality
The text explicitly states that both haqiqah and majaz can be specific or general, setting up the subsequent dispute.
This claim is contested by some Shafi'is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب أحكام الحقيقة والمجاز والصريح والكناية
قال: حكم الحقيقة وجود ما وضع له أمراً كان أو نهياً، خاصاً كان أو عاماً.
وحكم المجاز وجود ما استُعير له، خاصاً كان أو عاماً.
وطريق معرفة الحقيقة : التوقيف والسماع، بمنزلة النصوص.
وطريق معرفة المجاز : التأمل في مواضع الحقائق.
وأما في الحكم فهما سواء، إلا عند التعارض : فإن الحقيقة أولى منه.
ومن أصحاب الشافعي رحمه الله من قال : لا عموم للمجاز.
وبيان ذلك أن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: «لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء» (١) ، فاحتج الشافعي رحمه الله
بعمومه، وأبى أن
يعارضه حديث ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما في النهي عن بيع الدرهم بالدرهمين والصاع بالصاعين ؛ لأن الصاعَ مجاز عما يحويه، ولا عموم
له، فإذا ثبت المطعوم به مراداً : سقط غيره.
قال: لأن الحقيقة أصل الكلام، والمجاز ضروري يُصار إليه توسعةً، ولا عموم لما ثبت ضرورة تكلّم البشر.
والصحيح ما قلنا ؛ لأن المجاز أحد نوعي الكلام، فكان مثل صاحبه ؛ لأن عموم الحقيقة لم يكن لكونه حقيقةً ، بل لدلالة زائدة على ذلك. ألا يُرى أن رجلاً : اسم خاص، فإذا زدت عليه : لام التعريف من غير
معهود ذكرته : انصرف إلى تعريف الجنس، فصار عاماً بهذه الدلالة. والصاع نكرةٌ زيد عليها: لام التعريف، وليس في ذلك معهود ينصرف إليه، فانصرف إلى جنس ما أُريد به، ولو أريد به عينه لصار عاماً. فإذا أُريد به ما يَحُلُّه ويجاوره مجازاً: كان كذلك؛ لوجود دلالته، ألا يُرى أنه استعير له ذلك بعينه ؛ ليعمل في ذلك عمله في موضعه، كالثوب يلبسه المستعير كان أثره في دفع الحر والبرد مثل عمله إذا لَبِسَ بحقِّ الملك ، إلا أنهما يتفاوتان لزوماً وبقاء.
والمجاز طريق مطلق، لا ضروري ، حتى كَثُر ذلك في كتاب الله
(١) باب أحكام الحقيقة والمجاز حديث لا تبيعوا الطعام بالطعام إلا سواء بسواء: أخرجه الشافعي في مسنده بلفظ : «لا تبيعوا الذهب بالذهب، ولا الورق بالورق، ولا البُرَّ بالبرّ، ولا الشعير بالشعير، ولا التمر بالتمر، ولا الملح بالملح، إلا سواء بسواء». هذا لفظه في حديث عبادة، وللشيخين من حديث أبي بكرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه نهى عن الفضة بالفضة والذهب بالذهب، إلا سواء بسواء».
(١) حديث ابن عمر لا تبيعوا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين: عن : أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : لا تبيعوا الدينار بالدينارين، ولا الدرهم بالدرهمين ولا الصاع بالصاعين، إني أخاف عليكم الرماء، والرماء: هو الربا». أخرجه أحمد والطبراني في الكبير.
Chapter on the rulings of literal speech (haqiqah), figurative speech (majaz), explicit (sarih), and implicit (kinayah). He said: The ruling of literal speech is the existence of that for which it was coined, whether command or prohibition, specific or general. The ruling of figurative speech is the existence of that for which it was borrowed, specific or general. The way of knowing literal speech is through authoritative transmission and hearing, like the revealed texts. The way of knowing figurative speech is through reflection on the contexts of literal meanings. As for the ruling, they are equal, except in case of conflict: then literal speech takes precedence. Among the followers of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, is one who said: There is no generality in figurative speech. Explanation of that is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' Al-Shafi'i argued by its generality, but he rejected that it is contradicted by the hadith of Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, prohibiting selling a dirham for two dirhams and a sa' for two sa's, because the sa' is figurative for what it contains, and there is no generality for it. So if the food is established as intended, other than it falls away. He said: Because literal speech is the origin of speech, and figurative speech is necessary, resorted to for expansiveness, and there is no generality for what is established by necessity of human speech. The correct is what we said: because figurative speech is one of the two types of speech, so it is like its counterpart; because the generality of literal speech was not due to its being literal, but due to a additional indication. Do you not see that 'rajul' (man) is a specific noun, but when you add to it the definite article without a previously mentioned referent, it turns to definition of the genus, and becomes general by this indication. And 'al-sa'' is an indefinite noun with the definite article added, and there is no previously mentioned referent to refer to, so it turns to the genus of what is intended; and if it were intended as a specific entity, it would become general. So if it is intended for what it contains and its adjacent (figuratively), it is likewise, due to the existence of its indication. Do you not see that it was borrowed for that specific thing, so that it works in that regard as it works in its original place, like a garment borrowed by the borrower; its effect in repelling heat and cold is like its effect when worn by right of ownership, except that they differ in permanence and durability. And figurative speech is an absolute mode, not necessary, to the extent that it is abundant in the Book of Allah. (Footnote 1) Chapter on rulings of literal and figurative speech: Hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal'... (Footnote 1) Hadith of Ibn Umar: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor a sa' for two sa's'...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh, chapter on the rulings of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative), and also sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) though the latter two are not elaborated here. The author first defines haqiqah: it is a word used for its original meaning, be it a command or prohibition, specific or general. Then defines majaz: a word borrowed for a meaning other than its original due to a connection. The way to know haqiqah is through tawqif (revelation) and sam'a (traditional transmission), like the primary texts (nusus). The way to know majaz is through reflection (ta'ammul) on the contexts where literal meanings are used. In terms of legal effect, haqiqah and majaz are equal—both are valid—except when they conflict, then haqiqah takes precedence. Then a dispute is presented: some Shafi'is claim that majaz cannot be general (la 'umum li'l-majaz). They provide evidence: the Prophet's hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal'—Shafi'i argued by its generality that all food items are included, but this is opposed by Ibn Umar's hadith prohibiting selling a dirham for two dirhams and a sa' for two sa's, because 'al-sa'' (a measure) is used figuratively for what it contains, and according to them, figurative expressions lack generality. Therefore, if the intended food is established, other foods are excluded. The author then presents the Hanafi response: haqiqah is the foundation of speech, and majaz is a necessity resorted to for expansion, but it is not that what is established by necessity cannot be general. In fact, the correct position is that majaz, being one of the two types of speech, is like haqiqah in its capacity for generality. The generality of a literal expression does not arise from its being literal, but from an additional indicator (e.g., the definite article 'al-' added to a generic noun). The example is given: 'rajul' (man) is specific, but when you add the definite article without a previously known referent, it becomes general (indicating the genus). Similarly, 'al-sa'' is an indefinite noun with the definite article; since there is no specific referent, it refers to the genus of what is intended (the measure used for commodities). So even if used figuratively for the commodity itself, it can be general if the indicator exists. The analogy: a borrowed garment still protects from heat and cold like an owned garment, though they differ in permanence. Thus, majaz is an absolute mode, not merely a necessity, and it is abundant in the Quran.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): A word used for its original meaning for which it was coined, whether command or prohibition, specific or general.
المجاز (al-majaz): A word used in a meaning other than its original due to a connection (isti'ara), whether specific or general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, what is the ruling regarding haqiqah and majaz when they do not conflict?
A) Haqiqah always takes precedence.
B) Majaz always takes precedence.
C) They are equal in ruling.
D) Only haqiqah can produce rulings.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqah and majaz are both valid sources of legal rulings, but haqiqah takes precedence when they conflict.

The Generality of Majaz: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Dispute on generality of majaz between Hanafis and Shafi'is

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that majaz can be general and critique the Shafi'i objection using linguistic indicators.

Core Idea
Majaz can be general because generality arises from linguistic indicators, not from literalness.

Some Shafi'i scholars argue that figurative speech cannot be general. The Hanafis counter that generality is a function of indicators like the definite article, not of literalness. Through the example of 'al-sa'' (the measure) used figuratively, this lesson demonstrates the Hanafi reasoning and its implications for interpreting hadith.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i claim
Some Shafi'is assert that majaz cannot be general ('amm).
They consider majaz a secondary usage lacking the clarity needed for generality.
2
Present the Shafi'i evidence
They cite the hadith of Ibn Umar forbidding selling a sa' for two sa's, arguing that 'al-sa'' is figurative for its contents and thus not general.
If it were general, it would include all commodities measured by sa', but they say it is specific.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Hanafis argue that generality comes from added indicators like the definite article. The word 'al-sa'' has the definite article without a specific referent, making it general for the genus of what the sa' contains.
The same applies to haqiqah: e.g., 'al-rajul' becomes general when definite article is added without prior referent.
4
Examine the analogy of the borrowed garment
Hanafis compare majaz to a borrowed garment: its effect (e.g., repelling heat) is the same as when owned. Similarly, majaz works like haqiqah in ruling.
The difference is only in permanence, not in effect.
5
Conclude with the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is in interpreting 'al-ta'am' (food) in the riba hadith. Hanafis consider it general (all food items), while Shafi'is restrict it based on other evidence.
This affects which transactions are considered ribawi.

Chapter on the rulings of literal speech (haqiqah), figurative speech (majaz), explicit (sarih), and implicit (kinayah). He said: The ruling of literal speech is the existence of that for which it was coined, whether command or prohibition, specific or general. The ruling of figurative speech is the existence of that for which it was borrowed, specific or general. The way of knowing literal speech is through authoritative transmission and hearing, like the revealed texts. The way of knowing figurative speech is through reflection on the contexts of literal meanings. As for the ruling, they are equal, except in case of conflict: then literal speech takes precedence. Among the followers of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, is one who said: There is no generality in figurative speech. Explanation of that is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal.' Al-Shafi'i argued by its generality, but he rejected that it is contradicted by the hadith of Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, prohibiting selling a dirham for two dirhams and a sa' for two sa's, because the sa' is figurative for what it contains, and there is no generality for it. So if the food is established as intended, other than it falls away. He said: Because literal speech is the origin of speech, and figurative speech is necessary, resorted to for expansiveness, and there is no generality for what is established by necessity of human speech. The correct is what we said: because figurative speech is one of the two types of speech, so it is like its counterpart; because the generality of literal speech was not due to its being literal, but due to a additional indication. Do you not see that 'rajul' (man) is a specific noun, but when you add to it the definite article without a previously mentioned referent, it turns to definition of the genus, and becomes general by this indication. And 'al-sa'' is an indefinite noun with the definite article added, and there is no previously mentioned referent to refer to, so it turns to the genus of what is intended; and if it were intended as a specific entity, it would become general. So if it is intended for what it contains and its adjacent (figuratively), it is likewise, due to the existence of its indication. Do you not see that it was borrowed for that specific thing, so that it works in that regard as it works in its original place, like a garment borrowed by the borrower; its effect in repelling heat and cold is like its effect when worn by right of ownership, except that they differ in permanence and durability. And figurative speech is an absolute mode, not necessary, to the extent that it is abundant in the Book of Allah. (Footnote 1) Chapter on rulings of literal and figurative speech: Hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal'... (Footnote 1) Hadith of Ibn Umar: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams nor a sa' for two sa's'...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh, chapter on the rulings of haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative), and also sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) though the latter two are not elaborated here. The author first defines haqiqah: it is a word used for its original meaning, be it a command or prohibition, specific or general. Then defines majaz: a word borrowed for a meaning other than its original due to a connection. The way to know haqiqah is through tawqif (revelation) and sam'a (traditional transmission), like the primary texts (nusus). The way to know majaz is through reflection (ta'ammul) on the contexts where literal meanings are used. In terms of legal effect, haqiqah and majaz are equal—both are valid—except when they conflict, then haqiqah takes precedence. Then a dispute is presented: some Shafi'is claim that majaz cannot be general (la 'umum li'l-majaz). They provide evidence: the Prophet's hadith 'Do not sell food for food except equal for equal'—Shafi'i argued by its generality that all food items are included, but this is opposed by Ibn Umar's hadith prohibiting selling a dirham for two dirhams and a sa' for two sa's, because 'al-sa'' (a measure) is used figuratively for what it contains, and according to them, figurative expressions lack generality. Therefore, if the intended food is established, other foods are excluded. The author then presents the Hanafi response: haqiqah is the foundation of speech, and majaz is a necessity resorted to for expansion, but it is not that what is established by necessity cannot be general. In fact, the correct position is that majaz, being one of the two types of speech, is like haqiqah in its capacity for generality. The generality of a literal expression does not arise from its being literal, but from an additional indicator (e.g., the definite article 'al-' added to a generic noun). The example is given: 'rajul' (man) is specific, but when you add the definite article without a previously known referent, it becomes general (indicating the genus). Similarly, 'al-sa'' is an indefinite noun with the definite article; since there is no specific referent, it refers to the genus of what is intended (the measure used for commodities). So even if used figuratively for the commodity itself, it can be general if the indicator exists. The analogy: a borrowed garment still protects from heat and cold like an owned garment, though they differ in permanence. Thus, majaz is an absolute mode, not merely a necessity, and it is abundant in the Quran.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): Literal speech, the original meaning.
المجاز (al-majaz): Figurative speech, borrowed meaning.
العموم (al-'umum): Generality, referring to all individuals of a kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what makes 'al-sa'' general even when used figuratively?
A) Because it is a command.
B) Because it is a prohibition.
C) Because it has the definite article and no specific referent.
D) Because it is a noun.

Key Takeaway
Majaz can be general because generality is determined by linguistic indicators like the definite article, not by whether the word is literal or figurative.

Haqiqah and Majaz: The Independence of Metaphorical Speech
Debating the nature of haqiqah and majaz with applications to oaths and wills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position that majaz is an independent mode of speech and apply the haqiqah/majaz distinction to interpret oaths and bequests.

Core Idea
Majaz is not a mere necessity; it is a full-fledged linguistic mode abundant even in the Quran.

The relationship between literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings is foundational in usul al-fiqh. This passage from al-Bazdawi defends the Hanafi view that majaz is an independent and abundant mode of speech, not a forced retreat from haqiqah. Understanding this distinction directly impacts how we interpret legal texts, oaths, and bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
Some scholars argue that majaz is only used out of necessity (daruri), citing that haqiqah is the origin of speech.
This view is presented as the opponent's argument (qaala).
2
State the Hanafi counter-position
Al-Bazdawi holds that majaz is one of two equal types of speech, not a mere necessity. Generality in haqiqah comes from additional indicators, not from being haqiqah itself.
The Hanafi emphasis is on additional indicators (dalalah za'idah) that grant generality.
3
Analyze the dalil from the hadith
The hadith prohibiting selling a sa' for two sa's uses 'sa'' metaphorically for its contents. The Hanafi response: majaz functions with its own indications, just like haqiqah.
The borrowed word (sa') still carries meaning through context, similar to a borrowed garment still warding off cold.
4
Apply the rule to oaths and bequests
Haqiqah never drops from its original meaning; majaz can be denied. In oaths, only literal breach matters. In bequests, only haqiqah relatives (direct freed slaves) inherit, not metaphorical ones.
This concretely shows the legal effect of the haqiqah/majaz distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعارضه حديث ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما في النهي عن بيع بالدرهمين والصاع بالصاعين؛ لأن الصاع مجاز عما يحويه، ولا عموم له، فإذا ثبت المطعوم به مراداً: سقط غيره.

قال: لأن الحقيقة أصل الكلام، والمجاز ضروري يُصار إليه توسعةً، ولا عموم لما ثبت ضرورة تكلّم البشر.

والصحيح ما قلنا؛ لأن المجاز أحد نوعي الكلام، فكان مثل صاحبه؛ لأن عموم الحقيقة لم يكن لكونه حقيقةً، بل لدلالة زائدة على ذلك. ألا يُرى أن رجلاً: اسم خاص، فإذا زدت عليه: لام التعريف من غير معهود ذكرته: انصرف إلى تعريف الجنس، فصار عاماً بهذه الدلالة. والصاع نكرة زيد عليها: لام التعريف، وليس في ذلك معهود ينصرف إليه، فانصرف إلى جنس ما أُريد به، ولو أريد به عينه لصار عاماً. فإذا أُريد به ما يَحُلُّه ويجاوره مجازاً: كان كذلك؛ لوجود دلالته، ألا يُرى أنه استعير له ذلك بعينه؛ ليعمل في ذلك عمله في موضعه، كالثوب يلبسه المستعير كان أثره في دفع الحر والبرد مثل عمله إذا لَبِسَ بحقِّ الملك، إلا أنهما يتفاوتان لزوماً وبقاء.

والمجاز طريق مطلق، لا ضروري، حتى كَثُر ذلك في كتاب الله تعالى، وهو أفصح اللغات والله سبحانه وتعالى علي عن العجز والضرورات.

ومن حكم الحقيقة أن لا تسقط عن المسمى بحال، فإذا استعير لغيره: احتمل السقوط، يقال للوالد أب، ولا يُنفى عنه بحال. ويقال للجد: أب مجازاً، ويصح أن يُنفى عنه؛ لما بينا أن الحقيقة وضع، وهذا مستعار، فكانا كالملك والعارية، إلا أن يكون مهجوراً: فيصير ذلك دلالة الاستثناء، كما قلنا فيمن حلف لا يسكن هذه الدار، فانتقل من ساعته، وكمن حلف لا يقتل وقد كان جَرَحَ، ولا يطلق وقد كان حلف، وكمَن حلف لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق: لا يحنث بالأكل من عينه عند بعض مشايخنا.

وإذا حلف لا يأكلُ من هذه الشجرة فأكل من عين الشجرة: لم يحنث أيضاً.

ومن أحكام الحقيقة والمجاز استحالةُ اجتماعهما مرادَيْن بلفظ واحد؛ لما قلنا إن أحدهما موضوع، والآخر مستعار منه، فاستحال اجتماعهما، كما استحال أن يكون الثوب الواحد على رجل لبسه ملكاً وعاريةً معاً.

ولهذا قلنا فيمن أوصى لمواليه وله موال أعتقهم، ولمواليه موال أعتقوهم: إن الثلث للذين أعتقهم، وليس لموالي معتقيه شيء؛ لأن معتقيه مواليه حقيقة، بأن أنعم عليهم، فصار ذلك كولادهم؛ لإحيائهم بالإعتاق. فأما موالي الموالي فمواليه مجازاً؛ لأنه لما أعتق الأولين: فقد أثبت لهم مالكية الإعتاق، فصار بذلك مسبباً لإعتاقهم، فنسبوا إليه بحكم.
It is opposed by the hadith of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) prohibiting selling a dirham for two dirhams and a sa' for two sa's; because 'sa'' is a metaphor for what it contains, and it has no generality. So when the food intended by it is established, other than it drops. He said: Because haqiqah is the origin of speech, and majaz is a necessity resorted to for expansion, and there is no generality in what is established by the necessity of human speech. And the correct is what we said; because majaz is one of the two types of speech, so it is like its counterpart; because the generality of haqiqah was not because it is haqiqah, but due to an additional indication. Do you not see that 'rajul' is a specific noun, but when you add to it the definite article without a previously mentioned known referent, it turns to the definition of the genus, thus becoming general by this indication. And 'sa'' is an indefinite noun upon which the definite article was added, and there is no previously known referent for it, so it turns to the genus of what is intended by it. And if what was intended by it were the specific thing itself, it would become general. So when what is intended by it is what it contains and what is adjacent to it metaphorically, it is like that due to the existence of its indication. Do you not see that it was borrowed for that very thing, so that it might function in that regard as it functions in its original place, like a garment borrowed by the borrower: its effect in warding off heat and cold is like its effect when worn with rightful ownership, except that they differ in permissibility and permanence. And majaz is an absolute mode of speech, not a necessity, to the extent that it is abundant in the Book of Allah, which is the most eloquent of languages, and Allah is far above incapacity and necessities. And among the rulings of haqiqah is that it never drops from its designated meaning. So when it is borrowed for something else, dropping becomes possible. For example, one says 'father' for the parent and it is never denied from him. And one says for the grandfather 'father' metaphorically, and it is correct to deny it from him, because we have explained that haqiqah is a primary coinage, and this is borrowed, so they are like ownership and borrowed property. Unless it is abandoned (in usage), then that becomes an indication of exclusion, as we said regarding one who swears 'I will not reside in this house' and then moves out immediately, and like one who swears 'I will not kill' while he had already wounded, and 'I will not divorce' while he had already sworn, and like one who swears 'I will not eat from this flour' – he does not break his oath by eating from the flour itself according to some of our shaykhs. And if he swears 'I will not eat from this tree' and he eats from the tree itself, he also does not break his oath. And among the rulings of haqiqah and majaz is the impossibility of both being intended by a single word; because we said that one is primary and the other is borrowed from it, so their simultaneous intention is impossible, just as it is impossible for a single garment worn by a man to be both owned and borrowed simultaneously. And for this reason we said regarding one who bequeaths to his freed slaves and he has freed slaves and his freed slaves have (their own) freed slaves: the third is for those he freed, and nothing for the freed slaves of his freed slaves; because his freed slaves are his 'mawali' by haqiqah, since he bestowed favor upon them, so that becomes like their birth due to his reviving them through emancipation. As for the freed slaves of his freed slaves, they are his 'mawali' metaphorically; because when he freed the first set, he established for them the right of emancipation, thus becoming the cause of their freeing others, so they are attributed to him by that ruling.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the relationship between haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (metaphorical meaning) and how they function in legal interpretation. The author begins by addressing a hadith that prohibits selling a measure (sa') for two of the same measure. The opponent argues that the word 'sa'' is a metaphor for the food it contains, and thus has no general application; only the food intended is meant. The author then presents a counterargument: haqiqah is the foundation of speech, and majaz is only used out of necessity. However, the author refutes this, stating that majaz is one of two valid types of speech, just like haqiqah. Generality in haqiqah comes not from being haqiqah itself but from additional indicators, such as the definite article. Using examples like 'rajul' (man) becoming general with 'al-' (the definite article), and 'sa'' with 'al-', he shows that metaphorical usage still carries its own indication. He compares metaphorical borrowing to borrowing a garment: the borrowed garment still functions as a garment, just with different permanence. The author emphasizes that majaz is not merely a necessity; it is a full-fledged mode of speech, abundant even in the Quran. Next, he outlines key rulings: haqiqah never drops from its meaning; it is always attached to its original referent (e.g., 'father' always means biological father). Majaz, however, can be denied or dropped when convenient (e.g., calling a grandfather 'father' is metaphorical and can be denied). This is like ownership versus borrowing. Then he applies this to oaths: swearing not to reside in a house, to kill, or to eat from specific items—breach only occurs if the literal meaning applies, not metaphorical extensions. Finally, the passage discusses the impossibility of combining haqiqah and majaz in a single intended meaning of a word; they are mutually exclusive. This is applied to a case of a will: if a person bequeaths to his 'mawali' (freed slaves), the bequest goes only to those he directly freed (haqiqah), not to those freed by his freed slaves (majaz). The commentary follows the author's sequential reasoning, providing a Hanafi teacher's explanation of these foundational usul concepts.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The original, literal meaning of a word as coined in language; it never drops from its referent.
المجاز (al-majaz): The metaphorical use of a word, borrowed from its original meaning; it can be dropped or denied.
العموم (al-umum): Generality; a word covering all individuals of its kind. Achieved by indicators like the definite article.
الصاع (al-sa'): A unit of measure; in the hadith, debated whether it is used literally (for the measure) or metaphorically (for what it contains).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The testator's direct freed slaves (haqiqah).
B) Both the direct freed slaves and those freed by them.
C) Only those freed by the testator's freed slaves.
D) Neither group.

Key Takeaway
Majaz is an independent and legitimate mode of speech in Hanafi usul, with distinct legal implications for oaths, bequests, and textual interpretation.
