The Impossibility of Combining Haqiqa and Majaz in Umum
Defending the rule against counterexamples from oaths and wills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that haqiqa and majaz cannot combine for generality and refute counterexamples from oaths and wills by analyzing the scope of the metaphor.

Core Idea
Haqiqa and majaz cannot combine; alleged counterexamples operate on the generality of the metaphor itself.

This passage from Kanz al-Wusul defends a core Hanafi usul principle: a word cannot simultaneously bear literal and metaphorical meanings to produce generality. The author provides three supporting proofs and then engages with four counterexamples from oaths and wills, showing that in each case the ruling follows from the metaphor's own generality, not from combining meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State in your own words the Hanafi rule: haqiqa and majaz cannot combine to create 'umum. A word can only carry one intended meaning at a time for generality.
The rule is categorical: the two are fundamentally distinct and their indications are unequal.
2
Examine the Hanafi proofs
Look at the three examples: (1) non-khamr and hadd punishment, (2) Quran 4:43 'mass' as metaphor for intercourse, (3) bequest to 'awlad' excluding grandchildren. How does each illustrate that the intended meaning voids the other?
In each, the text's intended meaning (haqiqa or majaz) precludes the other.
3
Present the opponent's counterexamples
List the four cases opponents raise: (a) oath on 'placing foot', (b) oath on 'day', (c) safe conduct in al-Siyar, (d) oath on 'house'. What do opponents claim these show?
Opponents argue that rulings in these cases seem to consider both literal and metaphorical senses.
4
Analyse the Hanafi refutation
For each counterexample, explain the author's reinterpretation: 'placing foot' is metaphor for absolute entry; 'day' depends on verb type; 'house' is metaphor for dwelling; al-Siyar has a contrary narration. In all, only one meaning operates.
The key move: the metaphor itself is general, so its generality applies, not a combination.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis restrict rulings to one intended meaning, affecting punishments, oaths, and bequests. Opponents might allow broader applications. How does this difference appear in real fatwa?
For example, a vow 'I will not set foot in this house' is interpreted broadly under the metaphor of entry, not literally as placing a foot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السببية مجازاً، والحقيقة ثابتة، فلم يثبت المجاز. ألا يُرى أن الاسم المشترك لا عموم له مثل : الموالي: لا يعمُّ الأعلَيْن والأسفلين، حتى إن الوصية للموالي، وللموصي موال أعتقهم، وموال أعتقوه باطلة. وهذه معان يحتملها الاسم احتمالاً على السواء، إلا أنها لما اختلفت: سقط العموم. فالحقيقة والمجاز وهما مختلفان ودلالة الاسم عليهما متفاوتة: أولى أن لا يجتمعا . ولهذا قلنا في غير الخمر: إنه لا يُلحق بالخمر في الحد؛ لأن الحقيقة أُريدت بذلك النص، فبطل المجاز. ولهذا قلنا في قوله تعالى: ﴿أَوْ لَمَسْتُمُ النِّسَاء ﴾ النساء /٤٣ : إن المس باليد غير مراد ؛ لأن المجاز، مراد، بالإجماع، وهو الوطء، حتى حَلّ للجنب التيمم، فبطلت الحقيقة. ولهذا قلنا فيمن أوصى لأولاد فلان، أو لأبنائه، وله بنون وبنو بنين جميعاً : إن الوصية لأبنائه، دون بني بنيه ؛ لما قلنا. فإن قيل : قد قالوا فيمن حلف لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان: إنه يحنث إن دخلها حافياً، أو متنعلاً، أو راكباً. وفيمن قال : عبدي حرِّ يوم يقدَم فلانٌ: إنه إن قدم ليلاً أو نهاراً : عَتَقَ. وقال في السير الكبير في حربي استأمن على نفسه وأبنائه : إنه يدخل فيه البنون وبنو البنين. وقال فيمن حلف لا يسكن دار فلان إنه يقع على الملك والإجارة والعارية جميعاً. فقد جمع في هذه المسائل بين الحقيقة والمجاز. قيل له: وَضعُ القدم مجاز عن الدخول؛ لأنه موجبه، والدخولُ مطلق ، فوجب العمل بإطلاق المجاز وعمومه. وكذلك : اليوم : اسم للوقت، ولبياض النهار، ودلالة تعين أحد الوجهين : أن يُنظر إلى ما دخل عليه : فإن كان فعلاً يمتد : كان النهارُ أولى به ؛ لأنه يصلح معياراً له. وإذا كان لا يمتد : كان الظرف أولى به، وهو الوقت، ثم العمل بعموم الوقت واجب، فلذلك دخل الليل والنهار. بخلاف قوله : ليلةَ يَقدَم فلان فإنه لا يتناول النهار؛ لأنه اسم للسواد الخالص، لا يحتمل غيره. مثل : النهار : اسم للبياض الخالص، لا يحتمل غيره. وأما إضافة الدار فإنما يراد به نسبةُ السكنى إليه، فتُستعار الدار للسكنى، فوجب العمل بعموم نسبة السكنى. وفي نسبة الملك: نسبة السكنى موجودة، لا محالة، فيتناوله عموم المجاز. وأما مسألة «السِّير»: ففيها رواية أخرى بعد ذلك الباب: أنه لا يتناولهم.
Causality metaphorically, and the literal meaning is established, so the metaphorical is not established. Do you not see that the common noun has no generality like 'al-mawali': it does not include the higher and lower, so much so that a bequest to 'al-mawali' while the testator has clients he freed and clients who freed him is void. These are meanings that the noun can equally bear, but when they differ, generality falls. The literal and metaphorical are different and the indication of the noun to each is variant: it is more fitting that they do not combine. Hence we said regarding other than wine: it is not annexed to wine in punishment because the literal meaning was intended by that text, so the metaphorical becomes void. Hence we said in the verse 'or you have touched women' [4:43]: touching with the hand is not intended because the metaphorical is intended by consensus, which is intercourse, so much so that junub is permitted tayammum, so the literal meaning becomes void. Hence we said regarding one who bequeaths to 'the children of so-and-so' or 'his sons' and he has sons and grandsons: the bequest is for his sons, not his grandsons, for what we have said. If it is said: They have said regarding one who swears not to place his foot in so-and-so's house: he breaks his oath if he enters it barefoot, shod, or riding. And regarding one who says 'my slave is free on the day so-and-so arrives': if he arrives at night or during the day, he becomes free. And it is said in al-Siyar al-Kabir regarding a belligerent who sought safe conduct for himself and his sons: that it includes sons and grandsons. And regarding one who swears not to reside in so-and-so's house: that it applies to ownership, renting, and borrowing all together. So in these cases they combined literal and metaphorical. It is said to him: 'placing the foot' is a metaphor for entry because it is its cause, and entry is absolute, so it is necessary to act upon the absoluteness of the metaphor and its generality. Likewise, 'day': is a name for time and for the whiteness of the day, and the indication to determine one of the two aspects is to consider what it is attached to: if it is a verb that extends, then 'day' is more appropriate for it because it can serve as a measure for it. If it does not extend, then the adverbial is more appropriate for it, which is time, then acting upon the generality of time is obligatory, therefore night and day enter. Unlike his saying 'the night so-and-so arrives' for it does not include daytime because it is a name for pure blackness, which does not bear anything else. Like 'day': a name for pure whiteness, which does not bear anything else. As for the addition of 'house', it is intended as a relation of dwelling to it, so 'house' is used metaphorically for dwelling, so it is necessary to act upon the generality of the relation of dwelling. And in the relation of ownership, the relation of dwelling exists necessarily, so it is included by the generality of the metaphor. As for the case of 'al-Siyar', there is another narration after that chapter: that it does not include them.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on whether haqiqa (literal meaning) and majaz (metaphorical meaning) can combine to produce umum (generality) in a word. The author establishes the Hanafi rule: haqiqa and majaz are fundamentally different and their indications are unequal, so they cannot combine. He provides three examples to support this: (1) For non-khamr drinks, the hadd punishment does not apply because the text for khamr intended the literal meaning, voiding the metaphorical extension. (2) In Quran 4:43, 'aw lamastumu al-nisa'' is agreed to be metaphorical for intercourse, so the literal hand-touching is invalidated. (3) A bequest to 'awlad fulan' includes only direct children, not grandchildren, because the literal meaning (sons) is intended. An objection is then raised: there are cases where both haqiqa and majaz appear to be considered, such as in oaths of 'placing foot' (entry), 'day' (time vs. daylight), 'night', and 'house' (dwelling vs. ownership). The author refutes each: 'placing foot' is a metaphor for absolute entry, so its generality applies. For 'day', the choice depends on whether the verb is continuous (then literal daytime) or not (then time). 'Night' strictly means pure blackness. 'House' is a metaphor for dwelling, and ownership inherently includes dwelling, so the metaphor's generality applies. The case from al-Siyar al-Kabir has another narration excluding grandsons. Thus the counterexamples do not truly combine haqiqa and majaz.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqa): The literal, original meaning of a word as used in its coinage.
المجاز (al-majāz): The metaphorical meaning of a word derived from its literal sense by relationship.
العموم (al-ʿumūm): Generality; the inclusion of all individuals covered by a word's meaning.
الاسم المشترك (al-ism al-mushtarak): A homonym that bears multiple unrelated meanings, thus no generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the hadd punishment not apply to non-khamr drinks?
A) Because the text for khamr used metaphorical meaning.
B) Because the literal meaning for khamr was intended, voiding the metaphor.
C) Because non-khamr is not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because the hadd requires direct textual mention.

Key Takeaway
Haqiqa and majaz cannot combine for umum; alleged counterexamples are resolved by considering the generality of the metaphor alone.

Haqiqah and Majaz in Oaths: The Principle of General Metaphorical Meaning
Analyzing how haqiqah and majaz are applied in various legal examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when the literal meaning is prevented, the general metaphorical meaning (عُموم المجاز) governs, and derive the legal consequence for oath rulings.

Core Idea
When literal meaning is blocked, the general metaphorical meaning applies — not a combination.

In Hanafi usul, the interaction between literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings is critical for interpreting oaths and legal terms. This passage from Al-Bazdawi illustrates how multiple examples — from dwellings to wheat to vows — all follow the same principle: the metaphorical meaning has generality and overrides when the literal is inapplicable. Let's examine these cases step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example of 'sons' and 'grandsons'
Note that 'sons' literally means immediate sons, but through inheritance rules it includes grandsons, showing metaphorical extension. The literal meaning is blocked by the rule of inheritance, so the general metaphorical meaning of 'offspring' applies.
This is an early example in the passage.
2
Analyze the oath not to 'reside' in a house
The term 'reside' (سكنى) is applied to ownership, lease, and borrowing. The literal meaning of 'placing the foot' (وضع القدم) is metaphorical for entering/dwelling. Since the literal is not the intended meaning, the general metaphorical meaning of 'dwelling' applies to all tenures.
The passage explains that the relation of dwelling (نسبة السكنى) is general.
3
Compare the terms 'day' and 'night'
The word 'yawm' (day) can mean time or daylight. Context determines which: extended actions (like fasting) take daylight; non-extended actions take time. 'Layl' (night) is pure darkness with no metaphorical extension. This shows that when a term has both literal and metaphorical, the context selects one, not a combination.
Contrast 'layl' which is specific to darkness.
4
Examine the wheat and Euphrates cases
The objector claims that eating bread (metaphorical) and grain (literal) would be a combination. The Hanafi response is that 'wheat' in custom is a metaphor for the inner kernel, so both grain and bread are instances of the same general metaphorical meaning. Similarly, 'drinking from the Euphrates' is a metaphor for drinking water associated with the river, covering direct and indirect drinking.
This is the core of the madhhab dispute map.
5
Understand the vow of Rajab
The vow 'I must fast Rajab' is a single act with dual legal consequence: a vow (nadhr) by wording and an oath (yamin) by making a permissible act obligatory. This is not a combination of literal and metaphorical but two attributes of the same act.
The passage clarifies it is not a combination of haqiqah and majaz.

وقال فيمن حلف لا يسكن دار فلان إنه يقع على الملك والإجارة والعارية جميعاً.
فقد جمع في هذه المسائل بين الحقيقة والمجاز.

وكذلك: اليوم: اسم للوقت، ولبياض النهار، ودلالة تعين أحد الوجهين: أن يُنظر إلى ما دخل عليه:
فإن كان فعلاً يمتد: كان النهارُ أولى به؛ لأنه يصلح معياراً له. وإذا كان لا يمتد: كان الظرف أولى به، وهو الوقت، ثم العمل بعموم الوقت واجب، فلذلك دخل الليل والنهار.

وأما إضافة الدار فإنما يراد به نسبةُ السكنى إليه، فتُستعار الدار للسكنى، فوجب العمل بعموم نسبة السكنى.
وفي نسبة الملك: نسبة السكنى موجودة، لا محالة، فيتناوله عموم المجاز.
وأما مسألة «السِّير»: ففيها رواية أخرى بعد ذلك الباب: أنه لا يتناولهم.
ووجه الرواية الأولى: أن الأمان لحقن الدم، فيُبنى على الشبهات، وهذا الاسم بظاهره يتناولهم، لكن بطل العمل به؛ لتقدم الحقيقة عليه، فبقي ظاهر الاسم شبهة.
فإن قيل: قد قال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله فيمن حلف لا يأكل هذه الحنطة: إنه يحنث إن أكل من عينها أو مما يُتَّخذ منها، وفيه جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز.
وكذلك قالا فيمن حلف لا يشرب من الفرات: إنه يحنث إن كَرَع أو اغترف.
وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله فيمن قال: الله علي أن أصوم رجب: إنه إن نوى اليمين كان نذراً ويميناً، وهو جمع بينهما.
قيل له: أما أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله فقد عملا بإطلاق المجاز في العادة اسم لما في باطنها، ومَن أَكَلَها أو ما يُتَّخذ منها: فقد أكل ما فيها.

It includes sons and sons of sons. And he said regarding the one who swore 'I will not reside in so-and-so's house': it applies to ownership, lease, and borrowing all together. For he combined literal and metaphorical meaning in these issues. It was said to him: Placing the foot is metaphorical for entering, because it is its cause, and entering is absolute, so it is obligatory to act upon the absoluteness and generality of the metaphor. And similarly: 'al-yawm' is a name for time, and for the whiteness of the day, and the indication of which of the two meanings is intended: it is considered by what it enters upon: if it is an extended action, then 'al-nahar' is more appropriate for it because it serves as a measure for it. And if it is not extended, then the adverb is more appropriate, i.e., time, then acting upon the generality of time is obligatory, therefore both night and day are included. Contrary to his saying: 'the night so-and-so arrives' for it does not include the day, because it is a name for pure darkness, admitting no other. Like: 'al-nahar' is a name for pure whiteness, admitting no other. As for the addition of 'al-dar' (house), only the relation of dwelling is intended, so the house is used metaphorically for dwelling, so it is obligatory to act upon the generality of the relation of dwelling. And in the relation of ownership, the relation of dwelling exists necessarily, so the generality of the metaphor includes it. And as for the issue of 'al-seer' (travelers/co-passengers): in another narration after that chapter, it does not include them. And the justification for the first narration: that safe-conduct is for the protection of blood, so it is built on ambiguities, and this name in its apparent meaning includes them, but acting upon it is invalid due to the precedence of the literal meaning, so the apparent name remains an ambiguity. For if it is said: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding one who swore 'I will not eat this wheat': he breaks the oath if he eats from the actual wheat or from what is made from it, and in this there is combining literal and metaphorical. And likewise they said regarding one who swore 'I will not drink from the Euphrates': he breaks the oath if he drinks directly by lowering his mouth or by scooping. And Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding one who said 'Upon me is the obligation to fast Rajab': if he intended an oath, it is both a vow and an oath, and that is a combination of both. It was said to him: As for Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), they acted upon the absoluteness and generality of the metaphor for 'wheat' is customary as a name for what is inside it, and whoever eats it or what is made from it has eaten what is inside it. And drinking from the Euphrates is a metaphor for drinking from the water adjacent to the Euphrates and attributed to it, and this attribution is not cut off by vessels and scoops, due to what we mentioned in 'Al-Jami'', so that became acting upon its generality, not combining literal and metaphorical. As for the issue of the vow: it is not a combination of literal and metaphorical, rather it is a vow by its wording and an oath by its consequence, i.e., making obligatory, because making permissible things obligatory can serve as an oath.

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul addresses the interplay between haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (metaphorical meaning) in legal interpretation, particularly in oaths and legal definitions. The author first explains that the term 'banun' (sons) literally means sons but legally includes grandsons through metaphorical extension because the literal meaning is overridden by the rule of inheritance. In the case of an oath not to reside in a house, the ruling applies to ownership, lease, and borrowing because 'residing' metaphorically covers all forms of dwelling. The author then refutes an objection by clarifying that placing the foot is a metaphor for entering, and since entering is absolute, the general metaphorical meaning applies. The discussion on 'al-yawm' shows how context determines whether the literal (daylight) or metaphorical (time) meaning is used: with extended actions like fasting, 'day' means daylight; with non-extended actions, it means time including night. 'Layl' (night) is pure darkness and cannot metaphorically include day. The addition of 'dar' (house) is a metaphor for dwelling, so the general relation of dwelling applies, necessarily including ownership as it entails dwelling. The issue of 'al-seer' (safe-conduct for a group) illustrates a conflict: the apparent name includes the group, but the literal meaning (the person being safe-conducted) prevails due to its precedence, leaving the name as an ambiguity. The passage then addresses an objection regarding the rulings of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on wheat and the Euphrates. The objector claims they combine literal and metaphorical meanings (e.g., eating wheat literally vs. eating bread metaphorically). The author responds that 'wheat' is customarily a metaphor for the kernel inside, so eating the grain or its product constitutes eating that kernel. Similarly, 'drinking from the Euphrates' is a metaphor for drinking the water associated with the river, which remains even when using a vessel. Thus, these are not combinations but applications of the general metaphorical meaning. Finally, the case of a vow to fast Rajab: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad ruled that it is both a vow (by wording) and an oath (by making something obligatory). This is not combining literal and metaphorical, but rather a single act with two legal consequences: the wording makes it a nadhr, and the effect (obligation of a permissible act) makes it a yamin.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as used in its primary sense.
المجاز (al-majaz): A word used in a secondary, metaphorical sense based on a relation (علاقة) between the literal and intended meaning.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath that creates legal consequences (e.g., expiation for breaking).
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship or a permissible act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when the literal meaning of a term is blocked by context or custom, the general metaphorical meaning applies, covering all that the custom includes — this is not a combination but the operation of generality.

Metaphorical Usage in Legal Contracts: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on validity of metaphorical words in legal contracts (nikah, talaq, etc.)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits metaphorical usage in contracts like marriage using the concept of legal connection (sabab/hukm) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Metaphor in contracts is valid when a legal connection exists between literal and intended meanings.

This passage addresses a key usul dispute: can contracts like marriage be concluded using words not specifically designated for them (e.g., using 'gift' for marriage)? The Hanafi school, relying on the concept of isti'ara (metaphor) and legal connection (sabab/hukm), argues yes. The Shafi'i school restricts such contracts to their specific words. We will examine the proofs and reasoning of both sides.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is the Hanafi view on metaphorical usage in contracts like marriage? Identify the key terms and the basis for allowing metaphor.
The Hanafis allow using words like 'hiba' for marriage because of a legal connection.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
On what basis does al-Shafi'i restrict marriage to specific words? Note the principle 'aqd khass shuri'a bilafz khass'.
Shafi'i argues marriage is a special contract with countless interests, needing specific words.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
What is their dalil? They compare marriage to testimony and partnership, which also require specific words.
Shafi'i uses analogy to shahada and mufawada.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How do Hanafis rebut this using legal connection (sabab/hukm)? Focus on the connection between tamlik al-'ayn and ownership of usufruct.
The Prophet's marriage with 'hiba' is cited as proof of the connection.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings? Compare the validity of a marriage contract using 'hiba' in the two schools.
Hanafi: valid; Shafi'i: invalid.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خلاف بين الفقهاء أن الاتصال بين اللفظين من قبل حكم الشرع : يصلح طريقاً للاستعارة، وأنه ليس بحكم يختص باللغة؛ لأن طريق الاستعارة: القربُ والاتصال، وذلك ثابت بين كل موجودين من حيث وجدا، والمشروع قائم بمعناه الذي شُرع له، وبسببه الذي تعلق به، فصحت به الاستعارة. ولأن حكم الشرع متعلق بلفظ شرع سبباً أو علة: لا يثبت من حيث يُعقل إلا واللفظ دال عليه لغةً، والكلام فيما يُعقل، ولا استعارة فيما لا يُعقل. ألا يُرى أن البيع لتمليك العين شرعاً، ولذلك وضع لغةً، وكذلك ما شاكله، وهذا في مسائل أصحابنا رحمهم الله لا يُحصى. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : إن الطلاق يقع بلفظ التحرير مجازاً، والعتاق يقعُ بلفظ الطلاق مجازاً. ولم يمتنع أحد من أئمة السلف عن استعمال المجاز، فقد انعقد نكاح النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بلفظ الهبة مجازاً مستعاراً، لا أنه انعقد هبة؛ لأن تمليك المال في غير المال لا يُتصور، وقد كان في نكاحه : وجوب العدل في القسم، والطلاق، والعدةُ، ولم يتوقف الملك على القبض، فثبت أنه كان مستعاراً. ولا اختصاص للرسالة بالاستعارة ووجوه الكلام، بل الناس في وجوه التكلم سواء، فثبت أن هذا فصل لا خلاف فيه. غير أن الشافعي رحمه الله أبى أن ينعقد النكاح إلا بلفظ النكاح، أو التزويج ؛ لأنه عقد شرع لأمورٍ لا تُحصى من مصالح الدين والدنيا، ولهذا شرع بهذين اللفظين. وليس فيهما معنى التمليك، بل فيهما إشارة إلى ما قلنا فلم يصح الانتقال عنه ؛ لقصور اللفظ عن اللفظ الموضوع له في الباب. وهذا معنى قولهم : عقد خاص، شُرع بلفظ خاص. وهذا كلفظ الشهادة، لما كان موجباً بنفسه بقوله: أشهد لم تقم اليمين مقامه ، وهو أن يقول : أحلفُ بالله ؛ لأنه موجب بغيره، فلم تصلح الاستعارة. وكذلك عقد المفاوضة لا ينعقد إلا بلفظ المفاوضة عندكم. كذلك حكي عن الكرخي ؛ لأن غيره لا يؤدي معناه، ولهذا لم يجوزوا رواية الأحاديث بالمعاني. والجواب : أن لفظ البيع والهبة وضع لملك الرقبة، وملك الرقبة سبب لملك المتعة ؛ لأن ملك المتعة يثبت به تبعاً، فإذا كان كذلك: قام هذا الاتصال مقامَ ما ذكرنا من المجاورة التي هي طريق الاستعارة، فصحت الاستعارة بهذا الاتصال بين السببين والحكمين. والجواب عما قال: إن هذه الأحكام من حيث هي غير محصورةٍ جعلت فروعاً وثمرات للنكاح ، وبُني النكاحُ على حكم الملك له عليها؛
There is no disagreement among jurists that the connection between two expressions by virtue of a legal ruling is a valid path for metaphorical usage, and that it is not a ruling specific to language; because the path of metaphor is proximity and connection, and that is established between all existents by virtue of their existence, and the legislated act stands by its meaning for which it was legislated and by its cause to which it is attached, so metaphor is valid thereby. And because a legal ruling attached to an expression that is legislated as a cause or 'illa is not established rationally unless the expression indicates it linguistically, and the discussion is about what is rational, and there is no metaphor in what is not rational. Do you not see that sale is for transfer of ownership of an asset legally, and therefore it is linguistically placed for that, and likewise what resembles it, and this in the issues of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) is countless. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Divorce occurs with the word 'liberation' metaphorically, and manumission occurs with the word 'divorce' metaphorically. None of the early imams refrained from using metaphor, for the marriage of the Prophet (peace be upon him) was contracted with the word 'gift' as a metaphorical usage, not that it was contracted as a gift; because transferring ownership of property in something other than property is inconceivable, and in his marriage there was: obligation of fairness in division, divorce, and waiting period, and ownership was not conditioned on possession, so it is established that it was metaphorical. And the prophetic mission has no exclusivity in metaphor and modes of speech; rather, people are equal in modes of speech, so this is established as a point of no disagreement. However, al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) refused that marriage be contracted except with the word 'nikah' or 'tazwij'; because it is a contract legislated for countless interests of religion and world, and therefore it was legislated with these two words. There is no meaning of transfer of ownership in them, rather in them is an indication to what we said, so transfer from them is not valid due to the inadequacy of the expression relative to the expression originally placed for the topic. This is the meaning of their saying: a specific contract, legislated with a specific word. This is like the word 'shahada' (testimony), since it is binding by itself with the statement 'I bear witness' — the oath does not take its place, that is, saying 'I swear by Allah'; because it is binding by something else, so metaphor is not valid. Likewise, the contract of joint partnership (mufawada) is not contracted except with the word 'mufawada' in your view; similarly narrated from al-Karkhi; because other words do not convey its meaning, and for this reason they did not allow narrating hadith by meaning. The answer: that the words 'sale' and 'gift' are placed for ownership of the corpus, and ownership of the corpus is a cause for ownership of usufruct; because ownership of usufruct is established thereby as a subsidiary. So when that is the case, this connection takes the place of the adjacency we mentioned as the path of metaphor, so metaphor is valid by this connection between the two causes and two rulings. And the answer to what he said: that these rulings, in themselves, being countless, were made branches and fruits of marriage, and marriage was built on the ruling of ownership of the husband over the wife;

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the permissibility of using metaphorical language (isti'ara) in legal contracts. The Hanafi position, advocated by the author, is that when there is a legal connection (such as cause-effect or part-whole) between the literal meaning and the intended meaning, metaphorical usage is valid. The author first establishes general agreement that legal connections can serve as a basis for metaphor. Then he presents the Shafi'i view: al-Shafi'i acknowledges that talaq (divorce) can occur metaphorically using the word 'tahrir' (liberation), and 'itaq' (manumission) using the word 'talaq', but he restricts the contract of marriage exclusively to the words 'nikah' or 'tazwij' because it is a special contract legislated for many purposes. The Hanafi response is that even marriage can be contracted with the word 'hiba' (gift), as exemplified by the Prophet's marriage to Khadijah? Actually, the text says the Prophet's marriage was contracted with the word 'hiba' — this refers to the marriage of the Prophet to Zaynab bint Jahsh? Or another? Actually, the famous case is that the Prophet married Zaynab bint Jahsh with the word 'hiba' (gift) from Allah. The Hanafis argue that since the Prophet used it, it is valid. The Shafi'is counter that marriage is a specific contract requiring specific words. The text then draws analogies to testimony (shahada) and partnership (mufawada) where only specific words are allowed. The author provides two answers: first, that sale and gift words, which imply ownership of corpus, are connected to ownership of usufruct (which is the purpose of marriage), thus the connection serves as the pathway for metaphor. Second, that the countless rulings of marriage are made branches of the underlying ownership concept, so the metaphor is valid. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi reliance on legal connection (qiyas) and istihsan in allowing metaphorical usage, while Shafi'is adhere more strictly to specific linguistic forms for special contracts.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphorical usage of a word beyond its original linguistic meaning, based on a connection (qiyas or legal linkage) between the original and intended meanings.
تمليك العين (tamlik al-'ayn): Transfer of ownership of the corpus/an asset. This is the literal meaning of sale and gift.
عقد خاص شُرع بلفظ خاص (aqd khass shuri'a bilafz khass): A specific contract legislated with a specific expression, meaning that only the designated words can create that contract.

D) The Shafi'i approval of metaphor in divorce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates metaphorical usage in contracts when a legal connection (sabab/hukm) exists between the literal and intended meanings, as exemplified by marriage using the word 'hiba'.

Two Types of Connection in Metaphorical Transference
Usul analysis of isti'ara with two connection types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of connection (ittisal) in Hanafi usul and apply them to determine the permissibility and direction of metaphorical usage in legal language.

Core Idea
Metaphor in law works both ways only if the connection is between ruling and cause; otherwise it is one-way from cause to effect.

In Hanafi usul, the validity of metaphorical language (isti'ara) depends not merely on suitability but on the nature of the connection between the original and intended meanings. This lesson unpacks the passage from Usul al-Bazdawi that classifies connections into two types: bidirectional (ruling-cause) and unidirectional (branch-pure cause). Understanding this unlocks how words like 'nikah', 'ownership', and 'emancipation' can be used metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the premise that words like 'nikah' are legal markers
The passage begins by stating that 'nikah' and 'tazwij' are not coined for ownership but serve as markers (alam) for the marriage contract. This allows them to function like explicit texts in sharia.
Markers operate by coinage, not meaning.
2
Recognize the objection about mutual suitability
An opponent argues that if nikah is suitable for ownership, then the metaphor should work both ways, just as brothers are mutually suitable.
The objection assumes mutual suitability suffices for bidirectionality.
3
Learn the two types of connection
The author replies that connection (ittisal) is of two types: (1) connection of ruling to cause (bidirectional) and (2) connection of branch to pure cause (unidirectional). Only the first allows two-way metaphor.
Key distinction: ruling-cause vs. branch-cause.
4
Apply the bidirectional type to the case of 'ownership' and 'purchase'
In the slave example, 'owning' and 'buying' are ruling and cause respectively. If someone says 'I meant by ownership purchase', he is believed in both legal and religious matters because the metaphor goes from ruling (ownership) to cause (purchase) – a valid bidirectional transfer.
Bidirectional metaphor is valid from either side.
5
Apply the unidirectional type to 'emancipation' and 'divorce'
Emancipation is a cause that necessarily removes ownership of the body, which entails removal of marital rights (divorce) as a consequence. Thus metaphor from emancipation to divorce (cause to effect) is valid, but not the reverse.
Unidirectional metaphor only works from root to branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه أمر معقول معلوم.
وهو
ألا يُرى أن المهر يلزمه بالعقد لها، ولو كان ما ذكرت أصلاً، صح إيجاب العوض على أحدهما ولذلك كان الطلاق بيد الزوج؛ لأنه المالك.
هو
وإذا كان كذلك: قلنا : لمَّا شُرع هذا الحكم بلفظ النكاح والتزويج، ولا يختصان بالملك وضعاً ولغةً، فلأن يثبت بلفظ التمليك والبيع والهبة، وهي للتمليك وضعاً: أولى.
وإنما صَلح الإيجاب بلفظ النكاح والتزويج وإن لم يوضعا للملك؛ لأنهما اسمان جُعلا عَلَماً لهذا الحكم والعَلَمُ يَعمل وضعاً، لا بمعناه، بمنزلة النص في دلائل الشرع.
وإنما تُعتبر المعاني لصحة الاستعارة على نحو ما تُستعمل للقياس، فلما ثبت الملك بهما وضعاً صحتِ التعدية به إلى ما هو صريح في التمليك.
فإن قيل: فهلاً صحت استعارة النكاح للبيع والمناسبة التي ذكرتم قائمةٌ؛ لأنها تقوم بالطرفين جميعاً، لا محالة؛ لأنه لا يُناسب الشيء غيرَه إلا وذلك يناسبه، كالأخوين.
قيل له : الاتصال من هذا الوجه على نوعين:
أحدهما : اتصال الحكم بالعلة.
والثاني : اتصال الفرع بما هو سبب محض، ليس بعلةٍ وُضعت له.
فالأول يجب الاستعارة من الطرفين؛ لأن العلة لم تشرع إلا لحكمها، والحكم لا يثبت إلا بعلته فاستوى الاتصال، فعمت الاستعارة. ولهذا قلنا فيمن قال : إن ملكتُ عبداً فهو حر، فملك نصف عبد، ثم باعه، ثم ملك النصف الباقي: لم يعتق حتى يجتمع الكل في ملكه. ولو قال : إن اشتريتُ عبداً : عَتَقَ النصفُ الباقي وإن لم يجتمع في ملكه.
وفي العبد المعين: يستويان.
وإن قال : عَنّيتُ بالملك الشراء: كان مصدقاً في الحكم والديانة. وإن قال : عَنّيتُ بالشراء الملك: كان مصدقاً في الديانة؛ لأنه استعار الحكم لسببه في الفصل الأول، واستعار السبب لحكمه في الفصل الثاني. وأما الاتصال الثاني : فيصلحُ طريقاً للاستعارة في أحد الطرفين، وهو أن يُستعار الأصل للفرع، والسبب للحكم؛ لأن هذا الاتصال ثابت في حق الفرع؛ لافتقاره.
ولا يصح أن يُستعار الفرع للأصل؛ لأن هذا الاتصال في حق الأصل معدوم؛ لاستغنائه عنه.
وهذا كالجملة الناقصة إذا عُطفت على الجملة الكاملة : توقف أولُ الكلام على آخره؛ لصحة آخره وافتقاره، فأما الأول فتام في نفسه؛ لاستغنائه عنه.
وعلى هذا الأصل قلنا: إن ألفاظ العتق تصلح أن تُستعار للطلاق؛ لأنها وضعت لإزالة ملك الرقبة، وذلك يوجب زوال ملك المتعة تبعاً، لا قصداً على نحو ما قلنا، فصحت الاستعارة.
Because it is a rational, known matter. And it is: Do you not see that the dower becomes obligatory upon him by the contract for her, and if what you mentioned were an origin, then imposing the consideration on one of them would be valid, and for that reason divorce is in the hand of the husband because he is the owner. And when it is so, we say: Since this ruling was legislated with the word 'nikah' and 'tazwij', and they are not specific to ownership by coinage or linguistically, then it is more appropriate that it be established by the word 'tamlik', 'bay' and 'hiba', which are for ownership by coinage. And the obligation is valid with the word 'nikah' and 'tazwij' even though they are not coined for ownership because they are two names made as markers for this ruling, and the marker operates by coinage, not by its meaning, like the explicit text in the evidences of sharia. And meanings are considered for the validity of metaphor in the manner they are used for analogy. So when ownership is established by them by coinage, the transference to what is explicit in ownership becomes valid. If it is said: Why is the metaphor of 'nikah' for sale not valid while the suitability you mentioned is present? Because it exists on both sides inevitably, for a thing is not suitable for another unless that other is suitable for it, like two brothers. It is said to him: The connection from this aspect is of two types: First: connection of the ruling to the cause. Second: connection of the branch to what is a pure cause, not a cause placed for it. The first necessitates metaphor from both sides, because the cause was legislated only for its ruling, and the ruling is not established except by its cause, so the connection is equal, so the metaphor is general. And for this we say about someone who said: If I own a slave, he is free, then he owns half a slave, then he sells him, then he owns the remaining half: he does not become free until all is joined in his ownership. And if he said: If I buy a slave: the remaining half becomes free even if it is not joined in his ownership. And for the specific slave, the two are equal. And if he said: I meant by ownership purchase, he is believed in ruling and religion. And if he said: I meant by purchase ownership, he is believed in religion; because he borrowed the ruling for its cause in the first case, and borrowed the cause for its ruling in the second case. As for the second connection, it is a valid path for metaphor in one of the two sides, which is that the root is borrowed for the branch, and the cause for the ruling, because this connection exists in respect of the branch due to its need. And it is not valid to borrow the branch for the root, because this connection does not exist in respect of the root due to its self-sufficiency. This is like an incomplete sentence when it is conjoined to a complete sentence: the first part of the speech depends on the last part due to the validity of the last part and its need, while the first part is complete in itself due to its self-sufficiency. And upon this principle we said: The words of emancipation are valid to be borrowed for divorce, because they were coined for removing ownership of the body, and that necessitates removal of ownership of pleasure as a consequence, not intentionally, in the manner we said, so the metaphor is valid.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul al-fiqh analysis of metaphorical usage (isti'ara) in legal language, specifically regarding contracts. The author argues that words like 'nikah' and 'tazwij' are valid for establishing marriage despite not being coined for ownership, because they have become legal markers (alam) for that specific ruling. He then discusses the conditions for metaphorical transference: when two things have a bi-directional connection (like ruling and cause), metaphor can work both ways; but when the connection is one-directional (like a branch depending on a root), metaphor only works from root to branch, not vice versa. The author provides examples: if someone says 'if I own a slave, he is free' and then owns half, sells it, and later owns the other half, the slave does not become free until complete ownership is acquired. But if he says 'if I buy a slave', the second half becomes free immediately upon buying, because 'purchase' is the cause of ownership. The same distinction applies to intention: if one says 'I meant by ownership purchase', he is believed in both legal and religious matters; but if he says 'I meant by purchase ownership', he is only believed in religious matters because it is a metaphorical borrowing from ruling to cause. The author concludes that words like 'emancipation' (which remove ownership of the body) can be metaphorically used for 'divorce' (which removes ownership of marital rights) because the former necessarily entails the latter as a consequence.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-isti'ara): Metaphorical borrowing of a word from its original meaning to another meaning due to a connection.
اتصال الحكم بالعلة (ittisal al-hukm bil-illa): Bidirectional connection between a ruling and its cause; each implies the other.
اتصال الفرع بالسبب المحض (ittisal al-far' bis-sabab al-mahd): Unidirectional connection where a branch depends on a cause, but the cause does not depend on the branch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a person says 'I meant by ownership purchase', how is he treated?
A) He is believed in both legal and religious matters.
B) He is believed only in religious matters, not legal.
C) He is not believed in either matter.
D) He is believed only in legal matters, not religious.

Key Takeaway
The direction of metaphorical transference in legal language is determined by the type of connection: bidirectional only between ruling and cause, and unidirectional from cause to effect.

Two Types of Connection (Ittiṣāl) in Metaphorical Extension (Istiʿāra)
Defines two types of connection (ittiṣāl) for metaphorical extension (istiʿāra) in legal language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and partial connections (ittiṣāl) and state the direction of validity for metaphorical extension (istiʿāra) in each type, and apply this to the classic example of emancipation and divorce.

Core Idea
Metaphorical extension in legal language requires a real connection; only the type of connection determines whether the transfer is bidirectional or one-way.

This passage from al-Bazdawi’s Uṣūl al-Fiqh unpacks the conditions for when a word can be used metaphorically (istiʿāra) in legal contexts—critical for understanding oaths and legal constructs. The author identifies two types of connection (ittiṣāl) between a cause and its ruling, or between a root and its branch. The first type is full and mutual, allowing metaphorical transfer in both directions; the second is a one-way dependence, permitting only transfer from root to branch. We will explore these through the examples of ownership, purchase, emancipation, and divorce, and see how the principle of ittiṣāl shapes Hanafi positions on oath enforcement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of connection
The author defines a complete connection (ittiṣāl kāmil) where cause and ruling are inseparably linked, and a partial connection where the branch depends on the root but not vice versa. Look for the terms 'فاستوى الاتصال' (so the connection is equal) for the first type, and 'الاتصال الثاني' (the second connection) for the second.
2
Trace the istiʿāra direction
In the first type, istiʿāra is valid in both directions: the word for the cause can be used for the ruling, and the word for the ruling can be used for the cause. In the second type, only the root (or cause) can be used metaphorically for the branch (or ruling); the reverse is invalid. Find the phrases 'يصلح طريقاً للاستعارة في أحد الطرفين' and 'لا يصح أن يُستعار الفرع للأصل'.
3
Apply the principle to the ownership/purchase example
The text gives a case: one says 'If I own (milk) a slave, he is free.' The term 'milk' (ownership) can be interpreted as 'purchase' because ownership is the effect of purchase; the connection is full, so the metaphorical transfer is valid in both directions (the owner is believed in both ruling and conscience). Work through the two sub-cases: part ownership vs full purchase.
4
Analyze the incomplete sentence analogy
The author compares the second type to an incomplete sentence (jumla nāqiṣa) that depends on a complete one when conjoined. Recognize that this illustrates why the dependent factor cannot be used metaphorically for the independent one: the connection exists only one way.
5
Apply to emancipation and divorce
The Hanafi ruling: words of emancipation (ʿitq) can be used metaphorically for divorce (talāq) because emancipation (full removal of ownership) necessarily entails loss of marital rights (branch). The reverse (using divorce for emancipation) is not valid per Hanafis. Contrast with the Shāfiʿī position to see the practical difference (thamarah al-khilāf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لحكمها، والحكم لا يثبت إلا بعلته فاستوى الاتصال، فعمت الاستعارة. ولهذا قلنا فيمن قال : إن ملكتُ عبداً فهو حر، فملك نصف عبد، ثم باعه ، ثم ملك النصف الباقي: لم يعتق حتى يجتمع الكل في ملكه. ولو قال : إن اشتريتُ عبداً : عَتَقَ النصفُ الباقي وإن لم يجتمع في ملكه. وفي العبد المعين: يستويان. وإن قال : عَنّيتُ بالملك الشراء: كان مصدقاً في الحكم والديانة. وإن قال : عَنّيتُ بالشراء الملك: كان مصدقاً في الديانة؛ لأنه استعار الحكم لسببه في الفصل الأول ، واستعار السبب لحكمه في الفصل الثاني. وأما الاتصال الثاني : فيصلحُ طريقاً للاستعارة في أحد الطرفين، وهو أن يُستعار الأصل للفرع ، والسبب للحكم ؛ لأن هذا الاتصال ثابت في حق الفرع ؛ لافتقاره. ولا يصح أن يُستعار الفرح للأصل؛ لأن هذا الاتصال في حق الأصل معدوم؛ لاستغنائه عنه. وهذا كالجملة الناقصة إذا عُطفت على الجملة الكاملة : توقف أولُ الكلام على آخره ؛ لصحة آخره وافتقاره، فأما الأول فتام في نفسه؛ لاستغنائه عنه. وعلى هذا الأصل قلنا: إن ألفاظ العتق تصلح أن تُستعار للطلاق؛ لأنها وضعت لإزالة ملك الرقبة، وذلك يوجب زوال ملك المتعة تبعاً، لا قصداً على نحو ما قلنا، فصحت الاستعارة.
For its ruling, and the ruling is not established except by its cause, so the connection is equal, and the metaphorical extension is general. And for this we said regarding one who said: 'If I own a slave, he is free,' then he owned half a slave, then sold it, then owned the remaining half: he does not become free until the whole is gathered in his ownership. And if he said: 'If I buy a slave,' the remaining half becomes free even if it is not gathered in his ownership. And regarding a specific slave: the two are equal. And if he said: 'I meant by ownership purchase,' he is believed in ruling and conscience. And if he said: 'I meant by purchase ownership,' he is believed in conscience; because he metaphorically used the ruling for its cause in the first case, and used the cause for its ruling in the second case. As for the second connection: it is suitable as a path for metaphorical extension in one direction, which is that the root is used metaphorically for the branch, and the cause for the ruling; because this connection is established in respect of the branch due to its dependence. And it is not valid that the branch be used metaphorically for the root, because this connection in respect of the root is non-existent due to its independence. This is like an incomplete sentence when conjoined to a complete sentence: the beginning of the speech depends on its end for the validity of its end and its dependence; as for the beginning, it is complete in itself due to its independence. And upon this principle we said: the words of emancipation are valid to be used metaphorically for divorce; because they were placed for removing ownership of the neck (full ownership), and that necessitates the removal of ownership of usufruct (marital rights) as a consequence, not intentionally, as we said, so the metaphorical extension is valid.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh defines the two types of connection (ittiṣāl) that permit istiʿāra (metaphorical extension) in legal language. The first type is a full connection where the ruling and its cause are inseparably linked, allowing istiʿāra in both directions (cause for ruling and ruling for cause). Example: one who says 'If I own (milk) a slave, he is free'—here 'milk' (ownership) can be interpreted as 'purchase' because ownership is the effect of purchase; both directions are valid. The second type is a partial connection where the branch depends on the root but not vice versa; here istiʿāra is only valid from root to branch (e.g., cause for ruling) but not from branch to root. The author analogizes to a incomplete sentence (the branch) that depends on a complete sentence (the root) when conjoined. Applying this principle, Hanafis hold that words of emancipation (ʿitq) can be metaphorically used for divorce (talāq) because emancipation removes full ownership (root) which necessarily entails removal of marital rights (branch) as a consequence, even if not intended.

Key Terms
الاستعارة (al-istiʿāra): استعمال اللفظ في غير ما وضع له لعلاقة المشابهة والمشاركة في المعنى الخاص
الاتصال (al-ittiṣāl): الارتباط بين الشيئين بحيث يستلزم أحدهما الآخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because both involve ending a relationship
B) Because emancipation removes full ownership, and removing marital rights follows as a consequence
C) Because the cause and ruling are identical
D) Because divorce is a stronger form of emancipation

Key Takeaway
The validity of metaphorical extension in legal language depends on the type of connection between the original meaning and the intended meaning: a full connection permits bidirectional transfer, while a partial connection only allows transfer from the independent to the dependent.

Hanafi Condition for Istiʿāra: Mushākalah vs. General Similarity
Refutes Shafiʿi permission of metaphorical extension from ṭalāq to ʿitq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid istiʿāra and evaluate the Shafiʿi counterargument regarding ṭalāq and ʿitq.

Core Idea
Istiʿāra requires specific essential similarity, not any common attribute.

This lesson examines a usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafiʿis over the permissibility of using the term for divorce (ṭalāq) metaphorically for emancipation (ʿitq). The Shafiʿis argue that shared properties like sirāyah and luzūm suffice for metaphorical extension. The Hanafis counter that istiʿāra demands a specific, identity-defining similarity (mushākalah), otherwise linguistic precision collapses. We will explore this key difference and its implications for deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafiʿi position
Identify the Shafiʿi claim that ṭalāq can be used metaphorically for ʿitq and the basis they provide.
Shafiʿis rely on similarity in general meanings (sirāyah, luzūm) as cause for istiʿāra.
2
Identify the Hanafi objection
Understand the Hanafi rejection: the branch-to-root connection is non-existent from the root's perspective, and general similarity is insufficient.
Mushākalah requires a specific attribute that defines the existent.
3
Analyze the Arab usage example
Examine the Hanafi illustration: calling a brave man 'lion' because of courage, not just any similarity.
This shows that istiʿāra is based on a specific, distinguishing quality.
4
Apply the concept to ṭalāq and ʿitq
Compare the literal meanings: ṭalāq removes a restriction (marriage does not confer ownership), while ʿitq establishes legal strength and removes full ownership.
The essential difference prevents metaphorical transfer.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical difference: if someone swears by divorce and then emancipates, or vice versa, the rulings differ between the two schools.
The dispute affects fatwa in such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : أن يُستعار الطلاق للعتق؛ لأنهما يصح يتشابهان في المعاني ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما إسقاط بُني على السراية، واللزوم والمناسبة في المعاني من أسباب الاستعارة، مثل المناسبة في الأسباب. وقلنا : لا تصح هذه الاستعارة؛ لما قلنا في المسألة الأولى: إن اتصال الفرع بالأصل في حق الأصل: في حكم العدم. ولا تصح الاستعارة للمناسبة في المعاني من الوجه الذي قلنا؛ لأن طريق الاستعارة من قبل المعاني: المشاكلةُ في المعاني، التي هي من قبيل الاختصاص الذي به يقوم الموجود، فأما بكل معنى : فلا . وهذا الطريق من الخصم نظير طريقه في أوصاف النص، أن التعليل بكل وصف صحيح من غير أثر خاص. وقلنا نحن : هو باطل؛ لأن الابتلاء يسقط، فكذلك الاستعارة تقع بمعنى له أثر الاختصاص. ألا يُرى أن العرب تسمي الشجاع أسداً؛ للاشتراك في المعنى الخاص، وهو الشجاعة، فأما بكل وصف فلا؛ لأن ذلك يُبطل الامتحان، وتصير الموجوداتُ في الأحكام كلّها متناسبةً ولا مناسبة هذا الوجه ؛ لأن معنى الطلاق ما وضع له اسمه، وما احتمله محله ، وهو رفع القيد ؛ لأن الإطلاق عبارة عنه، والنكاح لا يوجب حقيقة الرق، ولا يَسُلُّب المالكية، وإنما يوجب قيداً: فلا يحتمل إلا إطلاق القيد. وأما الإعتاق : فإثبات القوة الشرعية ؛ لأن ذلك معناه لغةً.
And al-Shafiʿi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: That divorce be used metaphorically for emancipation; because they resemble each other in meanings; because each of them is an annulment built on transmission (sirāyah), and necessity and similarity in meanings are among the causes of metaphorical extension, like similarity in causes. And we said: This metaphorical extension is not valid; because we said in the first issue: the connection of the branch to the root in respect of the root is like non-existence. And metaphorical extension for similarity in meanings is not valid from the perspective we stated; because the path of metaphorical extension in terms of meanings is similarity in meanings that is of the type of specificity by which the existent is established; as for every meaning (general similarity), it is not. And this path of the opponent is similar to his path in the attributes of the text: that every attribute is valid for legal reasoning without a specific effect. And we said: It is invalid; because the test is nullified, and similarly metaphorical extension occurs only with a meaning that has the effect of specificity. Do you not see that the Arabs call a brave person a lion due to sharing in the specific meaning, which is courage; as for every description, no; because that would nullify the test, and all existent things become similar in rulings—and there is no similarity in this respect; because the meaning of divorce is what its name was placed for and what its locus bears, which is removing a restriction; because 'release' (iṭlāq) expresses it, and marriage does not necessitate the reality of slavery, nor does it remove ownership, rather it imposes a restriction: so it bears only the removal of the restriction. As for emancipation: it is establishing legal strength (quwwah shariʿiyyah); because that is its literal meaning.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute between Hanafis and Shafiʿis on whether istiʿāra can be applied between divorce (ṭalāq) and emancipation (ʿitq). Al-Shafiʿi permits using ṭalāq metaphorically for ʿitq because both share properties like sirāyah (transmission of effect) and luzūm (bindingness), and similarity in meanings is a valid basis for istiʿāra, just as similarity in causes. The Hanafis reject this, reiterating that the second type of connection (branch to root) is non-existent from the root's perspective; ʿitq is the root (removes full ownership) and ṭalāq is the branch (only removes a restriction). Istiʿāra based solely on general similarity of meanings is invalid; it requires a specific, essential similarity (mushākalah) that gives the existent its distinct identity. The Hanafis analogize to the Arab usage: a brave man is called 'lion' because of the specific attribute of courage, not because of any general similarity. Allowing istiʿāra for every shared attribute would nullify the 'test' (imtiḥān) of language, making all things equivalent. Furthermore, the literal meaning of ṭalāq is 'release' (rafʿ al-qayd)—marriage imposes a restriction, not ownership, so ṭalāq only releases that restriction and cannot be extended to full emancipation. In contrast, ʿitq literally means establishing legal force (quwwah shariʿiyyah), which is a complete removal of ownership.

Key Terms
السراية (al-sirāyah): Transmission of effect from one item to another, e.g., effect of emancipation spreading throughout parts of a jointly-owned slave.
المشاكلة (al-mushākalah): Specific similarity in essence, not just any shared attribute; the quality that makes an existent what it is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis reject using divorce (ṭalāq) metaphorically for emancipation (ʿitq) despite their similarities?
A) Because divorce has a different legal cause
B) Because the connection between branch (ṭalāq) and root (ʿitq) does not exist from the root's perspective
C) Because emancipation is stronger than divorce
D) Because similarity in meanings is not a valid basis for any metaphorical usage

Key Takeaway
Hanafis condition istiʿāra on specific essential similarity (mushākalah) to preserve legal precision, rejecting general similarity as a basis.

When Literal Meaning Is Impossible (Muta'adhdhir)
Rule for impossible literal meaning with examples and internal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a literal meaning is considered impossible (mutazedhir) under Hanafi usul and apply the rule of shifting to majaz in oath cases.

Core Idea
When the literal act is physically impossible, the oath applies to the metaphorical by consensus.

In this lesson, we examine the first exception to the principle of preferring literal meaning: impossibility (mutazedhir). Imam al-Bazdawi explains that when the literal referent of a word cannot exist for the action in question, the word shifts to its metaphorical meaning by scholarly consensus. Through examples from oaths, we will see how this rule operates and explore an internal Hanafi dispute about cases where someone deliberately performs the impossible literal act.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Identify the rule: if haqiqah is mutazedhir (impossible), turn to majaz by consensus. Understand the rationale: lack of competition between meanings.
Consensus (ijma) is claimed here; note its strength.
2
Examine the oath examples
Consider the examples: swearing not to eat from a date palm, vine, or flour. Compare with sheep or fresh dates. Note the distinction: impossibility vs possibility.
The word is applied to the product (dates, raisins, bread) metaphorically.
3
Analyze the internal dispute
Evaluate the two opinions on whether intentionally performing the impossible literal act (eating flour directly, drinking from well by cupping hand) makes one break the oath. One view says no (it was not intended), another says yes (haqiqah never falls away).
The author prefers the first view, citing the nikaah/zina analogy.
4
Connect to the nikaah analogy
Understand the supporting evidence from our companions: swearing not to marry (nikaah) applies only to the contract, not to zina, even though zina is a literal meaning of 'marriage' in a sense. This shows literal meaning can be set aside.
This analogy strengthens the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كانت الحقيقة متعذرة أو مهجورة: صير إلى المجاز، بالإجماع؛ لعدم المزاحمة. أما المتعذِّر: فمثلُ الرجل يحلف لا يأكل من هذه النخلة، أو: من هذه الكرمة، أو القدر أنه يقع على ما يُتَّخذُ منه مجازاً. بخلاف ما إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذه الشاة، أو: هذا الرطب : فإنه يقع على عينه ؛ لأن الحقيقة قائمة. وكذلك إذا حلف لا يأكل من هذا الدقيق: وقع على ما يتخذ منه؛ لأن الحقيقة متعذرة. وكذلك لو حلف لا يشرب من هذه البئر: لا يقع على الكرع، وهو حقيقة؛ لما قلنا. واختلفوا فيما إذا أكل عين الدقيق، أو تكلف، فكرَعَ من البئر. فقيل : لما كان متعذراً: لم يكن مراداً ، فلا يحنث. وقيل : بل الحقيقة لا تسقط بحال، فيحنث. والأول أشبه ؛ لأن أصحابنا رحمهم الله قالوا فيمن حلف أن لا ينكح أجنبيةٌ : إنه يقع على العقد ؛ فإن زنى بها : لم يحنث، فأسقطوا الحقيقة، وهي حقيقته. وأما المهجورة: فمثل من حلف لا قدمه يضع في دار فلان أن الحقيقة مهجورة، والمجازَ هو المتعارَفُ، وهو الدخول، فيحنث كيف دخل. ومثاله : أن التوكيل بالخصومة صُرِف إلى جواب الخصم مجازاً، فيتناول الإنكار والإقرار بإطلاقه ؛ لأن الحقيقة مهجورة شرعاً، والمهجور شرعاً : مثلُ المهجور عادةً، ألا يُرى أن من حلف لا يكلم هذا الصبي: لم يتقيد بزمان صباه ؛ لأن هجران الصبي: مهجور شرعاً). وعلى هذه الجملة يُخرَّج قولهم : في رجل قال لعبده ومثله يولد وهو معروف النسب من غيره : هذا ابني: إنه يعتق؛ عملاً بحقيقته، دون مجازه ؛ لأن ذلك ممكن، فالنسب قد يثبت من زيد، ويشتهر من عمرو، فيكون المقر مصدقاً في حق نفسه.
And when the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, one turns to the metaphorical, by consensus, due to the absence of competition. As for the impossible: like a man swears not to eat from this date palm, or from this vine, or flour – it applies to what is made from it metaphorically. Contrary to if he swears not to eat from this sheep, or this fresh date – then it applies to the actual thing because the literal meaning exists. Likewise, if he swears not to eat from this flour, it applies to what is made from it because the literal meaning is impossible. Likewise, if he swears not to drink from this well, it does not apply to drinking directly (kar‘) – which is the literal meaning – due to what we said. They differed concerning if he ate the actual flour, or strained to drink directly from the well. It was said: since it was impossible, it was not intended, so he does not break his oath. It was said: rather, the literal meaning does not fall away under any circumstance, so he breaks his oath. The first is more likely, because our companions said concerning one who swears not to marry a foreign woman: it applies to the contract; if he commits zina with her, he does not break his oath – so they disregarded the literal meaning, which is its literal meaning. As for the abandoned: like one who swears that his foot will not be placed in so-and-so's house – the literal meaning is abandoned, and the metaphorical is the customary, which is entering, so he breaks the oath however he enters. Its example: that authorization for litigation is turned to answering the opponent metaphorically, so it includes both denial and admission absolutely, because the literal meaning is abandoned legally, and what is legally abandoned is like what is abandoned by custom. Do you not see that one who swears not to speak to this child is not restricted to the time of his childhood? Because abandoning a child is legally abandoned. And on this basis, their statement is derived: concerning a man who says to his slave, who resembles him and is known to be the son of someone else, 'This is my son' – he is freed, operating on its literal meaning, not its metaphorical, because that is possible, for lineage may be established from Zayd and be famous from 'Amr, so the confirmer is believed regarding himself.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage from Imam al-Bazdawi's Usul explains the rule for preferring haqiqah (literal meaning) over majaz (metaphorical meaning) when the literal is possible. When the literal is impossible (mutazedhir) or abandoned (mahjur), one turns to the metaphorical meaning by consensus. The author provides illustrative examples from Hanafi fiqh. For oaths (yamin), if a man swears not to eat from a date palm, the oath applies to dates made from the palm metaphorically, not the tree itself, because eating the tree is impossible. But if he swears not to eat from a sheep or a fresh date, the literal meaning applies because it is possible. Similarly, for flour and well water, the oath applies to what is made from the flour (bread) and to drinking from a vessel, not to eating flour directly or drinking from the well by cupping the hand, as those are possible. There is a Hanafi internal dispute: if someone deliberately eats flour or drinks from the well directly, does he break his oath? One view says no because the literal meaning is impossible; another says yes. The author prefers the first, citing that the Hanafi scholars (ashabuna) ruled that if one swears not to marry (nikaah) a woman, the oath applies to the contract, not to zina – thus they set aside the literal meaning (zina). For abandoned literal meanings (mahjur), like swearing not to place one's foot in a house – the literal placing of the foot is abandoned, and the customary meaning is entering, so any form of entry breaks the oath. Likewise, authorizing someone for litigation (tawkeel bil khusumah) is interpreted metaphorically to mean responding to the opponent, including denial and admission. When the Shariah itself abandons a literal meaning (e.g., abandoning interaction with a child), it is treated like customary abandonment, supported by the hadith from Tirmidhi: 'He is not of us who does not show mercy to our young.' Finally, if a man tells his slave (who resembles him but is known as another's son) 'This is my son,' the literal meaning is applied – the slave is freed – because lineage can be established from multiple men, and the statement is possible.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as used in its primary sense.
المجاز (al-majāz): The metaphorical meaning of a word, used when the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned.
متعذر (muta‘adhdhir): Impossible in reality, such that the literal referent cannot exist for the action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a man swears not to eat from this date palm, what does his oath apply to?
A) The palm tree itself, as it is the literal meaning.
B) The dates made from the palm, because the literal meaning is impossible.
C) Nothing, because the oath is invalid.
D) Both the palm and dates equally.

Key Takeaway
When the literal meaning of a word in an oath is impossible, the word is interpreted metaphorically by consensus, and intentionally performing the impossible act typically does not break the oath.

When Literal Meaning Is Abandoned (Mahjur)
Rule for abandoned literal meaning with examples from oaths, agency, and paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify abandoned (mahjur) literal meanings and apply the rule of shifting to majaz in cases of oaths, legal authorization, and paternity acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Abandoned literal meanings, whether by custom or Shariah, yield to the customary metaphorical meaning in oaths and legal instruments.

Having covered impossibility, Imam al-Bazdawi now turns to the second exception: abandonment (mahjur). Here, the literal meaning is possible but has fallen out of common or Shariah usage. Through examples from oaths, legal agency (tawkeel), and the case of a slave claiming paternity, we see how the customary metaphorical meaning governs. The lesson also highlights the hadith that supports the Shariah's abandonment of certain literal meanings, such as ignoring a child.

Guided Path
1
Define mahjur
Understand that mahjur means the literal meaning is abandoned either by common usage ('urf) or by Shariah (shar‘). In both cases, the metaphorical meaning becomes the default.
The abandonment can be total or partial; here it leads to complete shift to majaz.
2
Examine the oath examples for mahjur
Consider the example of swearing not to place one's foot in a house. The literal meaning (placing the foot physically) is abandoned; the customary meaning is entering. So any form of entry breaks the oath.
Similarly, swearing not to speak to a child: the literal meaning is avoided because Shariah disapproves of ignoring children.
3
Analyze the tawkeel example
Understand that authorization for litigation (tawkeel bil khusumah) is interpreted metaphorically to mean 'responding to the opponent,' thus covering both denial and admission. The literal meaning (actual litigation) is abandoned by Shariah.
This shows the principle applied beyond oaths.
4
Connect to the hadith and the Shariah basis
The text invokes the hadith 'He is not of us who does not show mercy to our young...' to prove that Shariah abandons the literal meaning of ignoring a child. Thus, oaths about not speaking to a child are not restricted to childhood.
The hadith is from Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud; it serves as a proof that Shariah abandons such behavior.
5
Apply to the slave paternity case
The final example: a man says to his slave 'This is my son' when the slave resembles him but is known as another's son. Here, the literal meaning (paternity) is possible, so it is applied and the slave is freed. This contrasts with mahjur cases where the literal is abandoned.
This illustrates the default: if haqiqah is not impossible or abandoned, it is preferred.

مهجور (mahjūr): Abandoned in common usage or by Shariah, so the literal meaning is no longer customary.
التوكيل بالخصومة (al-tawkeel bil khusumah): Authorization for litigation; its literal meaning is abandoned, and it is interpreted as responding to the opponent.

Two Types of Abandoning Haqiqah: Custom and Linguistic Indication
Classification of ways to abandon haqiqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between abandonment of haqiqah by custom (dalalat al-isti'mal wa al-'adah) and by linguistic indication (dalalat al-lafz fi nafsihi), and apply each type to examples from Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The literal meaning is abandoned when custom redefines it or when the word's own implications exclude instances.

In Hanafi usul, words are presumed to carry their literal meanings, but this presumption can be overridden in two clear ways: by widespread custom that shifts usage, or by the intrinsic properties of the word itself. Al-Bazdawi introduces these two types as the first in a five-part classification. Understanding them is essential for correctly interpreting legal texts, oaths, and vows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the beginning of the passage to recognize that the first type is abandonment by indication of usage and custom, and the second by indication of the word itself.
These are the first two of five total types.
2
Analyze the first type: custom
Study the example of salat (prayer) and hajj. Notice how the original literal meaning is replaced by a customary metaphorical meaning. Understand the Quranic evidence and the role of istihsan in oaths.
Custom (urf) is a strong determinant in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the second type: linguistic indication
Examine the examples: meat not including fish, general freeing not including mukatab, general divorce not including mubattata, and the fruit reversal. Note how the word's own (in)completeness or specificity restricts its application.
The word's intrinsic meaning can imply exclusion.
4
Recognize the Hanafi methodology
Notice that custom-based abandonment relies on istihsan, while linguistic-based abandonment relies on the word's inherent characteristics. Both are valid ways to restrict general terms.
This relates to the principle of takhsis (specification).
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider an oath about 'drink' (sharab). Determine whether it includes all liquids or only customary ones, applying the first type. Then consider a vow about 'riding an animal' – does it include cars? Think analogically.
Modern applications require careful analysis of custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو خمسة أنواع :
باب
جملة ما تترك به الحقيقة
-١- قد تُترك بدلالة الاستعمال والعادة.
۲- وقد تُترك بدلالة اللفظ في نفسه.
وقد تُترك بدلالة سياق النظم. ٤ـ وقد تترك بدلالة ترجع إلى المتكلم.
ه وقد تُترك بدلالة في محل الكلام
١ - أما الأول : فمثل الصلاة، فإنها اسم للدعاء قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَصَلِّ عَلَيْهِمْ﴾ التوبة / ۱۰۳ : أي ادعُ، ثم سُمِّي بها عبادة معلومة مجازاً؛ لما أنها شرعت للذكر، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَأَقِمِ الصَّلَوٰةَ لِذِكْرِي﴾ طه/١٤ ، وكلُّ ذكر دعاء . وكالحج، فإنه قصد في اللغة، وصار اسماً لعبادة معلومة مجازاً؛ لما فيها من قوة العزيمة والقصد، بقطع المسافة.
وكذلك نظائرها من العمرة والزكاة، حتى صارت الحقيقة مهجورة. وإنما صار هذا دلالة على ترك الحقيقة؛ لأن الكلام موضوع لاستعمال الناس وحاجتهم ، فيصير المجاز باستعمالهم كالحقيقة.

و
ومثاله : ما قال علماؤنا رحمهم الله فيمن نذر صلاةً، أو حجاً، أو المشي إلى بيت الله تعالى أو أن يضرب بثوبه حطيم الكعبة: أن ذلك ينصرف إلى المجاز المتعارف ، ومثاله كثير .
و
وقالوا فيمن حلف لا يأكل رأساً : إنه يقع على المتعارف؛ استحساناً، على حسب ما اختلفوا، ويَسقط غيره وهو حقيقة.
و
وكذلك من حلف: لا يأكل بيضاً : إنه يختص ببيض الإوز، والدجاج؛ استحساناً.
وكذلك من حلف لا يأكل طبيخاً، أو شواء: إنه يقع على اللحم خاصة، استحساناً.
وكل عام سقط بعضُه : كان شبيهاً بالمجاز، على ما سبق، وهذا ثابت بدلالة العادة، لا غير.
٢- وأما الثابت بدلالة اللفظ في نفسه فمثل قول الرجل يحلف : لا يأكل لحماً : إنه لا يقع على السمك، وهو لحم في الحقيقة، لكنه ناقص؛ لأن اللحم يتكامل بالدم، فما لا دم له قاصر من وجه، فخرج عن بدلالة اللفظ.
وكذلك قول الرجل : كل مملوك لى حرّ: لا يتناول المكاتب. مطلقه
وكل امرأة لي طالق : لا يتناول المبتوتة المعتدة؛ لما قلنا، فصار مخصوصاً، وللمخصوص شبه بالمجاز.
ومن هذا القسم: ما ينعكس، وذلك مثل رجل حلف: لا يأكل فاكهة : لم يحنث عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله بأكل الرطب والرمان والعنب،
It is of five types: The chapter on the total of what abandons the real meaning. 1- It may be abandoned by the indication of usage and custom. 2- It may be abandoned by the indication of the word itself. And it may be abandoned by the indication of the context of the arrangement. 4- And it may be abandoned by an indication referring to the speaker. 5- And it may be abandoned by an indication in the context of the speech. 1- As for the first: such as prayer (salat), for it is a name for supplication. Allah Almighty said: {Pray (salli) for them} [At-Tawbah 103], meaning supplicate. Then it was named for a known act of worship metaphorically, because it was legislated for remembrance. Allah Almighty said: {Establish prayer (salat) for My remembrance} [Taha 14], and every remembrance is supplication. And like Hajj, for it is 'intention' in language, and became a name for a known act of worship metaphorically, because of the strength of determination and intention in it by crossing distances. And likewise their counterparts of Umrah and Zakat, until the real meaning became abandoned. This only became an indication for abandoning the real meaning because speech is intended for the usage and need of people, so the metaphorical becomes like the real by their usage. And an example of it: what our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding one who vows a prayer, or Hajj, or walking to the House of Allah, or that he strike with his garment the 'Hatim' of the Kaaba: that it is diverted to the conventional metaphorical meaning, and many such examples. And they said regarding one who swears an oath not to eat a 'head': it falls on the conventional, by istihsan, according to their differences, and other than that, which is literal, is dropped. And likewise, one who swears not to eat 'egg': it is specific to eggs of goose and chicken, by istihsan. And likewise, one who swears not to eat 'cooked' or 'roasted': it falls specifically on meat, by istihsan. And every general term some of which is dropped becomes similar to metaphor, as previously, and this is established by custom, not otherwise. 2- As for what is established by the indication of the word itself, such as the saying of a man who swears: 'I will not eat meat': it does not apply to fish, though it is meat in reality, but it is deficient because meat is perfected by blood, so what has no blood is deficient in one aspect, so it departs by the indication of the word. And likewise the saying of a man: 'Every slave I own is free' does not include the mukatab. And 'Every woman of mine is divorced' does not include the divorced woman in her waiting period (mubattata); because of what we said, so it becomes specified, and the specified resembles metaphor. And from this category is what is reversed, such as a man who swears: 'I will not eat fruit' — he does not break his oath according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) by eating ripe dates, pomegranates, and grapes.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul explains the ways in which the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word can be abandoned in favor of a metaphorical meaning (majaz). The author states that there are five types, but here he elaborates on the first two. The first type is abandonment by indication of usage and custom ('urf). For example, the word 'salat' originally meant 'supplication' (du'a), as in the Quranic verse 'And pray for them.' But through custom, it became the name for the specific ritual prayer, which is a metaphorical usage. Similarly, 'Hajj' meant 'intention' but came to denote the pilgrimage. The Hanafi scholars use this principle in practical rulings: if someone vows to perform a 'prayer', it is understood as the ritual prayer, not supplication. Oaths also follow custom: if one swears not to eat 'egg', it applies only to common eggs (goose, chicken), not all eggs. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because custom gives particular meanings. The second type is by the indication of the word itself, considering its linguistic meaning. For instance, if one swears not to eat 'meat', fish is not included because meat in Arabic is perfected by blood, and fish has no blood; thus by the word's own implication it is excluded. Also, freeing slaves does not include mukatab (a slave under contract for freedom), and divorce does not include a woman already irrevocably divorced and in her waiting period because the wording 'my wife' does not apply to her. Another example is the reversal: if one swears not to eat 'fruit', according to Abu Hanifa, ripe dates, pomegranates, and grapes are not considered fruit for the oath because they are not typically called fruit in the custom of the Arabs. The key takeaway: determining the meaning of a word in legal contexts depends on custom and linguistic precision.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The original, literal meaning of a word.
مجاز (majaz): A metaphorical meaning used by custom or linguistic indication.
دلالة الاستعمال والعادة (dalalat al-isti'mal wa al-'adah): The indication of common usage and custom that causes a word to be understood metaphorically.
دلالة اللفظ في نفسه (dalalat al-lafz fi nafsihi): The intrinsic linguistic indication of the word itself, based on its lexical properties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person vows (nidhr) to perform 'salah', what is the intended meaning?
A) Supplication (du'a)
B) The ritual prayer
C) Any act of worship
D) Recitation of Quran

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning of a word is abandoned either by widespread custom (type one) or by the word's own linguistic implications (type two), both of which restrict general terms in legal contexts.
