Custom-Based Specification of General Terms in Oaths
Custom restricts general terms in vows and oaths to conventional meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom (urf) specifies general terms ('amm) in vows and oaths, and derive the correct ruling for a given scenario using this principle.

Core Idea
In vows and oaths, general terms are restricted to their conventional meanings by custom—even if the literal meaning is broader.

This passage from al-Bazdawi illustrates a key Hanafi usul principle: custom can specify general terms. Through several examples, the author shows that when someone makes a vow or oath using a general word, the intended meaning is determined by common usage, not the literal dictionary definition. This specification is a form of takhsis based on 'urf and is justified by istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
Recognize that in vows and oaths, general terms are interpreted according to conventional (customary) meanings, even if the literal meaning is broader. This is a form of takhsis (specification) driven by custom.
The rule is an exception to the default that 'amm is binding on all its instances.
2
Examine the Examples
Go through each example: (1) a vow of prayer, Hajj, or walking to the House of Allah; (2) an oath not to eat 'a head'; (3) an oath not to eat 'an egg'; (4) an oath not to eat 'cooked' or 'grilled' food. Note how the literal meaning is set aside in favor of the conventional specification.
Each example shows a different domain: ibadah, food items, etc.
3
Understand the Mechanism
Observe that after specification, the general term resembles a metaphor (majaz) because part of its original meaning has fallen off. However, the specification here is not from a textual qarinah but from custom.
The text says: 'every general term part of which has fallen off resembles a metaphor.'
4
Recognize the Role of Istihsan
Note that in three of the four examples, the ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). Istihsan overrides strict qiyas or literal interpretation when equity and custom demand it.
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi proof.
5
Generalize the Principle
Abstract the rule: whenever a general term is used in a vow or oath, and there is a well-known conventional meaning, that conventional meaning governs. This applies across different contexts, not just the examples given.
The principle is not limited to these cases; it can be extended to other similar scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله : ما قال علماؤنا رحمهم الله فيمن نذر صلاةً، أو حجاً، أو المشي إلى بيت الله تعالى أو أن يضرب بثوبه حطيم الكعبة: أن ذلك ينصرف إلى المجاز المتعارف ، ومثاله كثير . وقالوا فيمن حلف لا يأكل رأساً : إنه يقع على المتعارف؛ استحساناً، على حسب ما اختلفوا، ويَسقط غيره وهو حقيقة. وكذلك من حلف: لا يأكل بيضاً : إنه يختص ببيض الإوز، والدجاج؛ استحساناً. وكذلك من حلف لا يأكل طبيخاً، أو شواء: إنه يقع على اللحم خاصة، استحساناً. وكل عام سقط بعضُه : كان شبيهاً بالمجاز، على ما سبق، وهذا ثابت بدلالة العادة، لا غير.
And its example: what our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) said regarding one who vows a prayer, or Hajj, or walking to the House of Allah, or striking the Hatim of the Kaaba with his garment: that it is directed to the conventional metaphorical meaning, and its examples are many. And they said regarding one who swore not to eat a head: it applies to the conventional meaning, by istihsan, according to their disagreement, and other than it (the literal) falls off while it (the literal) is the true meaning. And likewise, one who swore not to eat an egg: it is restricted to eggs of geese and chickens, by istihsan. And likewise, one who swore not to eat cooked food or grilled food: it applies specifically to meat, by istihsan. And every general [term] part of which has fallen off: it resembles a metaphor, as previously stated, and this is established by the indication of custom, nothing else.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi principle that customary usage (al-'urf) can specify a general term. The examples show how in vows and oaths, terms are interpreted according to common convention rather than literal meanings. For instance, a vow to 'walk to the House of Allah' means walking to the Masjid al-Haram, not anywhere else. Similarly, an oath not to eat 'a head' is understood as not eating a specific type of head like sheep's head, not any head. The key point is that this specification is based on custom (al-'adah) and is a form of takhsis through 'urf. The passage concludes that any general term that has had part of its meaning removed resembles a metaphor, but the specification here is purely through custom, not through any other indication.

Key Terms
المجاز المتعارف (al-majaz al-muta'araf): The conventional metaphorical meaning that custom dictates as the intended meaning in a particular context.
بدلالة العادة (bi-dalalah al-'adah): By the indication of custom; meaning the ruling is established based on customary practice rather than literal or textual evidence.
استحساناً (istihsanan): By juridical preference; the ruling is based on istihsan, which is a specific method in Hanafi usul to prefer a more equitable outcome over strict analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the general term in a vow or oath interpreted?
A) Always literally
B) According to conventional custom
C) According to the strictest possible meaning
D) According to the intention of the speaker

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) serves as a valid mukhassis (specifier) for general terms in vows and oaths, justified by istihsan, and the ruling follows conventional meaning over literal breadth.

Specification of General Terms by Linguistic Meaning
Linguistic specification of general terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a general term in an oath is specified by its linguistic meaning and apply the Hanafi rule to determine whether the oath is broken.

Core Idea
A general term is restricted by its linguistic derivation, not just by external evidence.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi demonstrates how the very meaning of a word can limit its application in legal contexts. For example, 'meat' does not include fish, and 'fruit' does not include staples like dates. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly interpreting oaths and other legal declarations, as the Hanafi school relies on linguistic precision over mere literal generality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Term
In each example, first locate the general term being used in the oath or declaration (e.g., 'meat', 'slave', 'fruit', 'relish').
The term is the starting point for analysis.
2
Examine Its Linguistic Derivation
Investigate the root meaning of the term. For instance, 'fruit' (fakihah) is derived from 'tafakkuh' meaning enjoyment, indicating it is an accompaniment, not a staple.
Linguistic derivation (ishtiqaq) is key to Hanafi specification.
3
Determine if the Specific Item Has an Additional Quality
Check whether the item in question possesses a quality that goes beyond the term's core meaning. For example, fresh dates can serve as sustenance, so they are not merely accompaniments like fruit.
This extra quality removes the item from the term's scope.
4
Apply to the Examples
Work through the given examples: fish is not meat because it lacks blood; a mukatab is not a full slave; a divorced woman in iddah is not a wife; dates/pomegranates/grapes are not fruit per Abu Hanifa.
Each example follows the same logic.
5
Note the Ikhtilaf
Observe that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree with Abu Hanifa on 'fruit' and 'relish', taking a more literal approach. Recognize the thamarah: practical difference in oath-breaking.
Understanding both positions deepens your grasp of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثابت بدلالة اللفظ في نفسه فمثل قول الرجل يحلف : لا يأكل لحماً : إنه لا يقع على السمك، وهو لحم في الحقيقة، لكنه ناقص؛ لأن اللحم يتكامل بالدم، فما لا دم له قاصر من وجه، فخرج عن بدلالة اللفظ. وكذلك قول الرجل : كل مملوك لى حرّ: لا يتناول المكاتب. وكل امرأة لي طالق : لا يتناول المبتوتة المعتدة؛ لما قلنا، فصار مخصوصاً، وللمخصوص شبه بالمجاز. من هذا القسم: ما ينعكس، وذلك مثل رجل حلف: لا يأكل فاكهة : لم يحنث عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله بأكل الرطب والرمان والعنب، وقالا : يحنث ؛ لأن الاسم مطلق، فيتناول الكامل منه. وقال أبو حنيفة: الفاكهة اسم للتوابع ؛ لأنه مأخوذ من التفكه، وهو التنعم. قال الله تعالى : انقَلَبُواْ فَكِهِينَ ﴾ المطففين / ٣١: أي ناعمين، وذلك أمر زائد على ما يقعُ به القوام، وهو الغذاء، فصار تابعاً، والرُّطَب والعنب قد يصلحان للغذاء، وقد يقع بهما القوام والرمان قد يقع به القِوام ؛ لِمَا فيه من معنى الأدوية، وإذا كان كذلك كان فيها وصف زائد، والاسم ناقص مقيَّد في المعنى، فلم يتناول الكامل. وكذلك طريقه فيمن حلف: لا يأكل إداماً : إنه يقع على ما يتبع الخبر؛ لأن الإدام اسمٌ للتابع، فلم يجز أن يتناول ما هو أصل من وجه، وهو اللحم والجبن والبيض. وعند محمد : يحنث في ذلك، كما في المسألة الأُولى. وعن أبي يوسف: روايتان في هذه المسألة.
As for what is established by the indication of the word itself: e.g., a man swears not to eat meat: it does not apply to fish, even though it is meat in reality, but it is deficient because meat is complete with blood, so what has no blood is deficient in one aspect, so it exits by the indication of the word. And likewise, a man says 'every slave I own is free': it does not include the mukatab. And 'every woman of mine is divorced': it does not include the divorced woman in her waiting period; because of what we said, so it becomes specified, and the specified resembles a metaphor. And from this section: what is inverted, like a man swears not to eat fruit: he does not break his oath by eating fresh dates, pomegranates, or grapes according to Abu Hanifa; but they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he does break, because the noun is absolute, so it includes the complete form. Abu Hanifa said: 'fruit' is a name for accompaniments, because it is derived from tafakkuh, which is enjoyment. Allah said: "they returned joyful" meaning comfortable, and that is something additional to what sustains, which is nourishment, so it becomes an accompaniment. Fresh dates and grapes can serve as nourishment, and they can provide sustenance; pomegranates can provide sustenance due to their medicinal quality. That being the case, they have an additional description, and the noun is incomplete, restricted in meaning, so it does not include the complete. And similarly his way regarding one who swears not to eat 'idam' (relish): it applies to what accompanies bread, because 'idam' is a name for an accompaniment, so it cannot include what is primary in some respect, i.e., meat, cheese, and eggs. And according to Muhammad: he breaks his oath in that, as in the first case. And from Abu Yusuf: two narrations in this case.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage explains how the linguistic meaning of a word itself can specify its application. For example, 'meat' (lahm) does not include fish because fish lacks blood, which is considered an essential component of meat according to Hanafi understanding. Similarly, general statements like 'every slave of mine is free' do not include a mukatab (a slave contracted for emancipation), and 'every woman of mine is divorced' does not include a woman who is already divorced and in her waiting period (mubtawtah). The passage then presents a dispute regarding the word 'fruit' (fakihah). Abu Hanifa holds that fruit refers to items that are merely pleasures (accompaniments to basic food), not staples like fresh dates, grapes, or pomegranates, which can serve as sustenance. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the general term includes all types. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is rooted in the linguistic derivation of 'fakihah' from 'tafakkuh' (enjoyment). The same principle applies to 'idam' (relish), which are accompaniments to bread, not primary items like meat, cheese, or eggs. Imam Muhammad breaks the oath in that case as well, while Abu Yusuf has two narrations.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fākihah): A term derived from tafakkuh (enjoyment), referring to items that are accompaniments to basic food, not staples.
إدام (idām): Relish or accompaniment to bread; something eaten with bread to enhance flavor.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has made a contract with his master to purchase his freedom; considered partially free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, does an oath not to eat 'fruit' (fakihah) break if one eats fresh dates (rutab)?
A) Yes, because dates are a fruit
B) No, because dates can serve as food and are not merely an accompaniment
C) Yes, because the word 'fruit' is general
D) No, because dates are not considered fruit linguistically

Key Takeaway
A general term's inherent linguistic meaning can restrict its legal scope, as demonstrated by Abu Hanifa's specification of 'fruit' to exclude staple foods.

Specification Through Textual Context (Siyaq al-Nazm)
Context can override literal meaning, specifying it as metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of siyaq al-nazm to identify when a text's literal meaning is overridden by context, and derive the correct ruling from a Quranic verse or hadith.

Core Idea
Context specifies meaning: a literal command becomes a metaphorical rebuke when the surrounding text contradicts its apparent sense.

In Hanafi usul, the meaning of a text is not solely determined by its literal form; the surrounding context (siyaq al-nazm) plays a crucial role. This lesson examines how the Quranic verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe...' is reinterpreted from a literal permission to a warning because of the immediately following threat. We will also see a legal application from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir, illustrating how context determines the validity of an aman grant.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the literal meaning
Read the text in isolation: the phrase 'faman sha'a falyu'min...' appears to be a command giving a genuine choice between belief and disbelief.
This is the apparent (zahir) meaning before considering context.
2
2. Locate the contextual clue (qarina)
Look at the immediately following clause: 'inna a'tadna li al-zalimin nara'. This is a severe threat, which contradicts the idea that disbelief is a permissible option.
The threat is a qarina (indicator) that modifies the meaning.
3
3. Apply siyaq to specify
Since the context contradicts the literal permission, the meaning is specified: the verse is not a real command of option but a metaphorical rebuke and warning.
The ruling is that the verse is not a permission to disbelieve.
4
4. Extend to the legal example
In al-Siyar al-Kabir, when a harbi says 'I am safe' to a Muslim and the Muslim responds 'You are safe,' the context (the harbi seeking aman) makes this a valid grant of protection, even if the phrase alone might be ambiguous.
Context overrides any literal ambiguity.
5
5. Generalize the principle
Siyaq al-nazm is a recognized method of takhsis (specification) in Hanafi usul. It can override the literal meaning (haqiqah) and require a metaphorical interpretation (majaz).
This principle applies to both Quran and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثابت بسياق النظم : فمثلُ قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِن وَمَن شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ إِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَارًا ﴾ الكهف / ۲۹: تُركت حقيقة الأمر والتخيير بقوله عزَّ وجلَّ: ﴿إِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّلِمِينَ نَارًا ) ، وحمل على الإنكار والتوبيخ مجازاً. ومثاله : ما قال محمد رحمه الله في السير الكبير في الحربي إذا استأمن مسلماً، فقال له: أنتَ آمِنُ : كان أماناً.
As for what is established by the context of the composition: e.g., the saying of Allah Most High: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire.' [Quran 18:29]. The literal meaning of command and option was abandoned by His saying 'Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire' and it was interpreted as a metaphorical meaning of denial and rebuke. And its example: what Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said in al-Siyar al-Kabir regarding a harbi who seeks aman from a Muslim and he says 'You are safe': it is considered an aman.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage illustrates how the context of a text (siyaq al-nazm) can specify the meaning of a general statement. The Quranic verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe...' appears to be a command granting choice, but the immediately following threat 'We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire' indicates that it is not a literal permission but a warning. The context changes the meaning from literal to metaphorical (ino rebuke). Similarly, in Islamic law of warfare, if a non-Muslim enemy (harbi) seeks safety and a Muslim says 'You are safe,' the context (seeking aman) makes this a valid grant of protection, even though the phrase alone might be construed differently. This demonstrates that the overall context of the text determines its legal import.

Key Terms
سياق النظم (siyāq al-naẓm): The context or composition of the entire textual passage; how words are arranged and the surrounding verses affect meaning.
الأمر (al-amr): A command; literal form indicating obligation, but can be interpreted metaphorically based on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the context (siyaq) of Quran 18:29 change the meaning of 'faman sha'a falyu'min'?
A) It confirms the literal meaning of option
B) It specifies the command to only believers
C) It changes it from a literal command to a metaphorical rebuke
D) It abrogates the verse entirely

Key Takeaway
Context (siyaq al-nazm) can override the literal meaning of a text, requiring a metaphorical interpretation that aligns with the overall composition.

Scope of Derived Nouns in Oaths: Fruit and Condiment
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn on scope of derived names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that derived nouns are restricted to secondary meanings and apply it to similar oath scenarios.

Core Idea
A derived noun applies only to its secondary meaning; if an item serves a primary purpose, the oath does not cover it.

In this passage, we examine a key usul dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on the interpretation of the word 'fruit' in an oath. The ruling depends on whether the name 'fruit' includes dates and grapes that can also serve as staple food. This case illustrates the Hanafi principle that derived nouns (mushaqq) are restricted by their linguistic origin, and how this principle limits the scope of general oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The dispute is whether the name 'fruit' in an oath includes dates, grapes, and pomegranates, which have qualities of both enjoyment and sustenance. Similarly, the name 'condiment' (idam) is examined for its scope.
2
State the positions of Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the name is absolute (mutlaq), so it includes everything commonly called fruit. Abu Hanifa argues that 'fruit' is derived from 'tafakkuh' (enjoyment) and thus applies only to secondary items, not those that can sustain life.
3
Analyze the linguistic derivation
Abu Hanifa supports his view by citing Quran 83:31 where 'fakiheen' means delighted. He argues that the root indicates an extra quality beyond basic nourishment, making the name restricted to purely enjoyable items.
This is a classic example of using etymology (ishtiqaq) to determine the scope of a noun.
4
Apply the same reasoning to 'idam' (condiment)
Abu Hanifa extends the same principle: 'idam' is a name for something secondary to bread, so it does not include meat, cheese, or eggs, which can be primary foods. Muhammad disagrees in this case, while Abu Yusuf has two narrations.
This shows how the principle can be applied analogically (qiyas) within the same madhhab.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi differences
Muhammad holds that the oath is broken for condiment (like fruit), while Abu Yusuf has two narrations. This highlights that later Hanafi scholars sometimes differed from Abu Hanifa's strict etymological approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالا : يحنث ؛ لأن الاسم مطلق، فيتناول الكامل منه. وقال أبو حنيفة: الفاكهة اسم للتوابع ؛ لأنه مأخوذ من التفكه، وهو التنعم. قال الله تعالى : انقَلَبُواْ فَكِهِينَ ﴾ المطففين / ٣١: أي ناعمين، وذلك أمر زائد على ما يقعُ به القوام، وهو الغذاء، فصار تابعاً، والرُّطَب والعنب قد يصلحان للغذاء، وقد يقع بهما القوام والرمان قد يقع به القِوام ؛ لِمَا فيه من معنى الأدوية، وإذا كان كذلك كان فيها وصف زائد، والاسم ناقص مقيَّد في المعنى، فلم يتناول الكامل. وكذلك طريقه فيمن حلف: لا يأكل إداماً : إنه يقع على ما يتبع الخبر؛ لأن الإدام اسمٌ للتابع، فلم يجز أن يتناول ما هو أصل من وجه، وهو اللحم والجبن والبيض. وعند محمد : يحنث في ذلك، كما في المسألة الأُولى. وعن أبي يوسف: روايتان في هذه المسألة.
And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He breaks his oath because the name is absolute, so it includes the complete. And Abu Hanifa said: 'Fruit' is a name for secondary items because it is derived from 'tafakkuh', which is enjoyment. Allah said: '...returned joyful' (Mutaffifin 31): meaning delighted, and that is an extra quality beyond what sustains life, which is nourishment, so it became secondary. And ripe dates and grapes can serve as nourishment and can sustain life, and pomegranate can sustain life because it has medicinal properties. And when that is the case, there is an extra quality in them, and the name is deficient and restricted in meaning, so it does not include the complete. Similarly his way regarding one who swears: 'I will not eat condiment' - it applies to what follows bread, because 'idam' is a name for a secondary item, so it does not include what is primary in some respect, which is meat, cheese, and eggs. According to Muhammad: he breaks the oath in that, as in the first case. And from Abu Yusuf: two narrations in this matter.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute on the scope of derived nouns in oaths. The Sahibayn argue that since the word 'fruit' is absolute (mutlaq), it includes all fruits in their complete form. Abu Hanifa counters that 'fruit' (fakiha) is derived from 'tafakkuh' meaning enjoyment, indicating a secondary quality beyond basic sustenance. Therefore, it excludes items like dates, grapes, and pomegranates that also serve as staple food. Similarly, 'idam' (condiment) is secondary to bread, so it does not include meat, cheese, or eggs which can be primary. Muhammad holds that the oath is broken in the condiment case as well (opposing Abu Hanifa), while Abu Yusuf has two narrations.

Key Terms
فاكهة (fakiha): A name for secondary items derived from tafakkuh (enjoyment), not including staple foods.
إدام (idam): A name for a secondary item accompanying bread, not including primary foods like meat.
تابع (tabi'): Secondary or dependent item, not primary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone swears not to eat 'fruit', which of the following would he be permitted to eat without breaking his oath?
A) Pomegranate
B) Dates
C) Grapes
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The scope of a derived noun in oaths is determined by its linguistic origin; if the derivation indicates a secondary quality, primary items are excluded.

Dalalat Mahall al-Kalam: Restricting Generality by Context
Textual analysis with a Quranic example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of dalalat mahall al-kalam to identify when a general statement is restricted by its subject matter.

Core Idea
The subject matter of speech can restrict a general ruling when the context cannot bear absolute generality.

In Usul al-Fiqh, not every general statement is truly universal. Sometimes the very subject being discussed forces a narrowing of meaning. This lesson unpacks a key Quranic example: Allah's declaration that 'the blind and the seeing are not equal.' At first glance, this seems to deny any equality between blind and seeing people. But the Hanafi school shows how the locus of speech (the context of faith and guidance) restricts the general negation to spiritual eyesight alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general statement
Locate the general negation in the verse: 'And the blind and the seeing are not equal.' The phrase 'are not equal' (لا يستوي) is a general negation that appears to cover all aspects of equality.
The word 'عام' (general) applies here because the negation is unqualified.
2
Analyze the locus of speech (mahall al-kalam)
Examine the surrounding context of the verse in Surah Ghafir. The preceding verses discuss faith and guidance. The 'blind' and 'seeing' refer to the believer and the disbeliever in terms of spiritual insight.
The locus is the subject being discussed: the comparison of believers and disbelievers.
3
Determine if the locus can bear absolute generality
Ask: can the general negation be literally applied to every possible equality? The answer is no—blind and seeing are equal in many ways (e.g., humanity, physical needs). The context cannot accommodate such broad equality.
Hanafi principle: if the locus cannot bear the generality, takhsis is necessary.
4
Restrict to the specific quality indicated
The only equality that fits the context is equality in eyesight (al-basar), meaning spiritual insight. Thus the verse means: the blind (in faith) and the seeing (in faith) are not equal.
This operation is called 'takhsis bi al-mahall' (specification by the subject matter).
5
Confirm the final ruling
The derived ruling is that the verse's generality is restricted to the quality of eyesight (faith). This is an example of indication by the locus of speech (dalalat mahall al-kalam).
This principle is used by Hanafis to narrow the scope of general texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثابت بدلالة محل الكلام فمثل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا يَسْتَوِى الْأَعْمَى وَالْبَصِيرُ ( غافر /٥٨ : سقط عمومه، وذلك حقيقته؛ لأن محل الكلام - وهو المخبَرُ عنه - لا يحتمله ؛ لأن وجوه الاستواء قائمة، فوجب الاقتصار على ما دلّت عليه صيغة الكلام، وهو التغاير في البصر.
And as for what is established by indication of the locus of speech: like the saying of Allah: 'And the blind and the seeing are not equal' (Ghafir 58): its generality dropped, and that is its true meaning; because the locus of speech – that which is being spoken about – does not bear it; because there are many aspects of equality, so it is necessary to restrict to what the form of speech indicates, which is difference in eyesight.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage illustrates the principle of dalalat mahall al-kalam, where the subject matter restricts a general statement. In the verse, the general negation 'are not equal' is about the blind and seeing, but the context (the preceding discussion of faith and guidance) shows that only equality in eyesight (spiritual insight) is meant. Since there are many ways blind and seeing can be equal (e.g., in humanity), the generality is restricted to the specific quality indicated by the context. This is a form of takhsis (specification) by the locus of speech.

Key Terms
دلالة محل الكلام (dalalat mahall al-kalam): Indication from the subject matter that restricts the scope of a general statement.
تغاير في البصر (taghayur fi al-basar): Difference in eyesight (physical or spiritual), the specific quality denied equality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best explains why the generality of 'The blind and the seeing are not equal' is restricted?
A) The verse is abrogated by another ayah.
B) The locus of speech does not bear absolute generality because there are many aspects of equality.
C) The word 'blind' is ambiguous.
D) The Prophet ﷺ clarified it in a hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of dalalat mahall al-kalam shows that the subject matter of a text can restrict a general statement without needing an external exception.

The Particle of Comparison Does Not Indicate Generality
States the rule that the simile particle does not obligate generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that kaaf al-tashbih does not obligate generality to determine whether a given text should be interpreted literally or metaphorically, based on the acceptance of the locus.

Core Idea
Kaaf al-tashbih does not create generality; generality depends on the locus (mahall) accepting it, otherwise the word becomes metaphorical.

In this passage, al-Bazdawi explains a subtle usul rule: the simile particle 'kaaf' does not automatically indicate that everything in the comparison is included. Instead, we must examine the context—the 'mahall'—to see if it can support generality. If not, the statement shifts from literal to metaphorical. This distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting many Quranic and prophetic texts, as we will see with the two hadiths he cites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its justification
State the rule: the particle of comparison (kaaf) does not obligate generality. Understand why: because of inherent difference (mugayara) between the compared items in many respects; the comparison is limited to some aspects.
The rule applies to any simile using kaaf, e.g., 'Zaid is like you.'
2
Analyze the example and the exception
Consider the example 'Zaid is like you.' Generality is not established unless the locus (mahall) – the property being ascribed – can accept generality. Contrast with Imam Ali's statement about dhimmis: their blood is like our blood; here the locus (blood) can accept being equal in sanctity and protection, so the statement is general.
The mahall is the attribute being compared, e.g., blood, wealth.
3
Apply to the first hadith: 'Actions are by intentions'
The word 'actions' cannot mean every action literally because not all actions are subject to a single intention. The locus does not accept the literal interpretation; thus the word 'actions' is metaphorical for 'the reward of actions' or 'the validity of deeds.'
This is a common usul analysis in Hanafi texts.
4
Apply to the second hadith: 'My ummah is forgiven for error and forgetfulness'
The word 'error' cannot be literally removed because error still occurs. The locus (error itself) does not accept the attribute of being removed. Therefore, the mention of error is metaphorical for its consequence (i.e., the sin is forgiven).
Similarly, 'forgetfulness' is metaphorical for its ruling.
5
Summarize the principle
The particle kaaf does not obligate umum; words in such constructions may be literal or metaphorical depending on whether the locus accepts generality. This is determined by reason and context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك كاف التشبيه لا يوجب العموم ؛ لما قلنا من قيام المغايرة من وجوه كثيرة، حتى إذا قيل : زيد مثلك : لم يثبت عمومه إلا أن يقبل المحلَّ العموم. مثل قول علي رضي الله عنه في أهل الذمة : «إنما بذلوا الجزية؛ لتكون كدمائنا، وأموالهم كأموالنا» : فإن هذا عام عندنا؛ لأن المحل يحتمله. و من هذا الباب: قولُ النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إنما الأعمال بالنيات»، و: «رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان: سقطت حقيقته؛ لأن الخطأ غير مرفوع، بل هو متصور، فسقطت حقيقته، وصار ذكرُ الخطأ والعمل مجازاً عن حكمه وموجبه.
Similarly, the particle of comparison (kaaf) does not obligate generality because of the existence of difference in many respects; so if it is said 'Zaid is like you,' its generality is not established unless the locus accepts generality. Like the saying of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding the dhimmis: 'They only paid jizyah so that their blood would be like our blood, and their wealth like our wealth'—this is general in our view because the locus (their blood) accepts it. And from this chapter: the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Actions are by intentions,' and 'My ummah is forgiven for error and forgetfulness': their literal meaning is dropped because error is not actually removed, but it is conceivable; so their literal meaning falls, and the mention of error and action becomes metaphorical for their ruling and consequence.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul rule that the particle of comparison (kaaf) does not imply generality (umum). The author gives the example: if one says 'Zaid is like you,' it does not mean that Zaid shares every attribute with you; it only establishes similarity in some respects unless the context (mahall) allows for generalization. He then cites the statement of Imam Ali regarding dhimmis: 'Their blood is like our blood, their wealth like our wealth.' The Hanafi position holds that this statement is general because the 'mahall' (i.e., the blood and wealth of dhimmis) can receive the attribute of being treated like Muslims' blood and wealth. Next, he applies this principle to two hadiths. The hadith 'Actions are by intentions' does not mean that every action is literally defined by intention; rather, the intent is that reward is based on intention, so the word 'actions' is used metaphorically for their ruling. Similarly, the hadith 'My ummah is forgiven for error and forgetfulness' cannot mean that error itself is removed (since error still occurs), so the mention of error is metaphorical for its consequence (sin). Thus, the literal meaning is abandoned when the mahall cannot bear it.

Key Terms
كاف التشبيه (kāf al-tashbīh): The Arabic letter kaaf used to indicate comparison, which does not obligate generality.
عموم (ʿumūm): Generality; a word covering all individuals of its category.
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning used for what it was originally coined for.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical or figurative meaning used for something other than its original coinage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' not taken literally?
A) Because the literal meaning contradicts consensus
B) Because the locus (actions) does not accept being defined by intention alone; thus 'actions' is metaphorical for the ruling
C) Because it is a metaphorical statement about intention
D) Because it was abrogated

Key Takeaway
The particle of comparison (kaaf) does not obligate generality; whether a statement is general or metaphorical depends on whether the locus (mahall) can accept the attribute, as seen in the examples from Ali and the two hadiths.

The Meaning of Waw: Absolute Conjunction in Hanafi Usul
Linguistic analysis of waw with a fiqh dispute resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that waw (و) indicates absolute conjunction without sequence or simultaneity, and apply this understanding to interpret the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the conditional divorce case.

Core Idea
Waw indicates absolute conjunction — it is the foundational particle, like 'raqaba' in being absolute, not general or ambiguous.

This lesson examines al-Bazdawi's linguistic analysis of the conjunction waw, a cornerstone of Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Through induction from Arabic speech, he demonstrates that waw signifies mere conjunction without specifying order, simultaneity, or delay. This principle has direct bearing on the interpretation of shariʿah texts, such as the wudu verse, and resolves a well-known dispute within the Hanafi school regarding conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic method
Recognize that the meaning of particles is known through induction (istiqraʾ) of Arabic usage, analogous to deriving legal rulings from primary sources. The passage uses this method to establish waw's meaning.
This parallels how usul al-fiqh derives rules from Qur'an and Sunnah.
2
Examine the primary evidence
Study the examples: 'جاءني زيد وعمرو' (togetherness without order), 'لا تأكل السمك وتشرب اللبن' (prohibition of combining), and the poetic line. These show waw indicates conjunction without sequence or simultaneity.
Note that replacing waw with fa would change the meaning.
3
Differentiate waw from other particles
Contrast waw with fa (sequence), maʿa (simultaneity), and thumma (delay). Each has a unique function; if waw indicated order or simultaneity, it would duplicate these particles, violating the principle of distinct meanings.
This linguistic argument supports waw's absolute nature.
4
Understand the analogy to mutlaq
Al-Bazdawi likens waw to the word 'raqaba' (neck) — it is absolute (mutlaq) in scope, not general (ʿamm) or ambiguous (mujmal). This clarifies that waw has a single core meaning: conjunction without additional specification.
This analogy is specific to Hanafi usul; other schools may categorize differently.
5
Apply to the intra-Hanafi dispute
Analyze the divorce case: 'إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق وطالق وطالق'. Abu Hanifa rules one divorce, Sahibayn rule three. The disagreement is not about waw's meaning but about how the condition attaches to each repetition—Abu Hanifa sees intermediaries, others do not. This shows waw's meaning remains constant.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is the number of divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا حكم لا يُعرف إلا باستقراء كلام العرب، وبالتأمل في موضوع كلامهم، كالحكم الشرعي إنما يُعرف من قبل اتباع الكتاب والسنة، والتأمل في أصول الشرع، وكلاهما حجة عليه، ودليل لما قلنا.
أما الأول: فإن العرب تقول : جاءني زيد وعمرو، ويفهم اجتماعهما في المجيء من غير تعرض للقرآن، أو الترتيب في المجيء، ولأن الفاء تختص بالأجزية، ولا يصلح فيها الواو.
حتى إن من قال لامرأته : إن دخلت الدارَ وأنت طالق طلقت في الحال، ولو احتمل الواوُ الترتيبَ لصلح للجزاء، كالفاء.
وقد صارت الواو للجمع في قول الناس : جاءني الزيدون، وأصله: جاءني زيد وزيد وزيد.
وقالوا لا تأكل السمك وتشرب اللبن: معناه : لا تجمع بينهما، من غير تعرض لمقارنة، أو ترتيب في الوجود، ولو استعمل الفاء مكانه : لبطل المراد.
ومثله قول الشاعر:
لا تَنْهَ عن خُلُقٍ وتأتي مثله عار عليك إذا فعلت عظيم
أي لا تجمع بينهما، فهذا لبيان الوضع.
وأما الثاني: فلأن كلام العرب أسماء، وأفعال، وحروف، والأصل في كل قسم منها أن يكون موضوعاً لمعنى خاص يتفرد به.
فأما الاشتراك: فإنما يثبت لغفلة من الواضع، أو عذر دعا إليه، وكذلك التكرار.
وقد وجدنا حروف العطف وغيرها موضوعةً لمعان يتفرد كل قسم بمعناه، فالفاء للترتيب و مع للقران و ثم للتعقيب والتراخي. فلو كان الواو للترتيب لتكررت الدلالة وليس ذلك بأصل، لكن الواو لما كانت أصلاً في الباب كان ذلك دلالة على أنها وضعت لمطلق العطف على احتمال كل قسم من أقسامه، من غير تعرض لشيء منها، ثم انشعبت الفروع إلى سائر المعاني.
وهذا كما وضع لكل جنس اسم مطلق، مثل: الإنسان، والتمر، ثم وضعت لأنواعها أسماء على الخصوص، وصارت الواو فيما قلنا نظير اسم الرقبة في كونه مطلقاً، غير عام ولا مجمل.
ولهذا قلنا: إن حكم النص في آية الوضوء: التحصيل من غير تعرض لمقارنة، أو ترتيب.
وقد ظن بعض أصحابنا أن الواو للمقارنة، وليس كذلك.
وزعم بعضهم أنها عند أبي يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله للمقارنة؛ لأنهما قالا فيمن قال لامرأته قبل الدخول بها : إن دخلت الدارَ فأنتِ طالقٌ وطالق وطالق : إنها إذا دخلت الدار طلقت ثلاثاً، وإنها عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تطلق واحدةً، فدل أنه جعلها للترتيب.
وليس كذلك، بل اختلافهم راجع إلى أن ذكر الطلقات متعاقبة، يتصل الأول بالشرط على التمام والصحة، ثم الثاني، ثم الثالث ما موجبه؟
فقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : موجبه الافتراق ؛ لأن الثاني اتصل بالشرط بواسطة، والثالث بواسطتين، والأول بلا واسطة، فلا يتغير هذا الأصل
And this is a ruling that is not known except by induction from the speech of the Arabs, and by contemplation of the subject matter of their speech, just as the legal ruling is only known by following the Book and the Sunnah, and contemplation of the principles of the Law. Both are proof for it and evidence for what we said.
As for the first: The Arabs say: 'Zayd and Amr came to me,' and the togetherness in coming is understood without indicating sequence or order in coming, because the fa is specific to the apodosis, and the waw is not suitable for that.
Even if a man said to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced (with waw),' she is divorced immediately. If the waw implied order, it would be suitable for the apodosis like the fa.
And the waw came to indicate conjunction in the saying of the people: 'The Zayds came to me,' and its origin: 'Zayd and Zayd and Zayd came to me.'
And they said: 'Do not eat fish and drink milk,' meaning: do not combine them, without indicating simultaneity, or order in existence. If they had used fa in its place, the intended meaning would be nullified.
Similar is the poet's saying:
'Do not forbid a trait and then come with its like, disgraceful upon you if you do it, great.'
Meaning: do not combine them. This is to clarify the literal usage.
As for the second: Because the speech of the Arabs consists of nouns, verbs, and particles. The principle for each category is that it is assigned a specific meaning unique to it.
As for homonymy (ishtirak), it only occurs due to the oversight of the originator or an excuse necessitating it, and likewise repetition.
We have found that the particles of conjunction and others are assigned meanings, each category unique to its meaning. Fa is for sequence, and ma'a for simultaneity, and thumma for succession with delay. If waw were for sequence, there would be duplication of indication, and that is not the principle. But since waw is the foundational particle in this chapter, that indicates it is assigned for absolute conjunction, admitting each of its categories, without specifying any one of them. Then the branches branched out to other meanings.
And this is like that a generic name is assigned for each genus, such as 'human' and 'dates', then specific names are assigned for its types. And the waw, in what we said, is analogous to the word 'neck' (raqaba) in being absolute, neither general nor ambiguous.
For this reason, we said: The ruling of the text in the verse of wudu is: attainment without indicating simultaneity or order.
And some of our companions thought that the waw indicates simultaneity, but it is not so.
And some claimed that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) it is for simultaneity, because they said in the case of a man who said to his wife before consummation: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced and divorced and divorced (with waw),' that when she enters the house she is divorced three times. And according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) she is divorced once. So it indicates that he (Abu Hanifa) made it for sequence.
But it is not so. Rather, their disagreement goes back to the fact that the mention of divorces is successive: the first is connected to the condition completely and validly, then the second, then the third. What is its requirement?
Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Its requirement is separation, because the second is connected to the condition with an intermediary, and the third with two intermediaries, and the first without an intermediary. So this principle does not change.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul deals with the linguistic and legal analysis of the conjunction waw (و) in Arabic. The author begins by stating that the meaning of particles is known through induction (istiqra') of Arabic speech, just as legal rulings are derived from the Qur'an and Sunnah. Two arguments are presented to show that waw indicates conjunction without sequence or simultaneity.

First argument: In Arabic, 'جاءني زيد وعمرو' means Zayd and Amr came together, without specifying order. If waw implied order, it could replace fa in conditional sentences, but it cannot. For example, in 'إن دخلت الدار وأنت طالق', the wife is divorced immediately upon entering, not after. Similarly, 'لا تأكل السمك وتشرب اللبن' means do not combine the two actions, not order. Poetry is cited to reinforce this.

Second argument: Arabic parts of speech have unique meanings. Particles like fa (sequence), ma'a (simultaneity), and thumma (delay) each have distinct functions. If waw indicated order, it would duplicate fa, which is against the principle. Instead, waw is the basic conjunction particle for absolute linkage, analogous to the word 'raqaba' (neck) which is absolute (mutlaq) - not general (amm) or ambiguous (mujmal).

Applying this to fiqh: The verse of wudu (Qur'an 5:6) is understood to mean combining the actions without specifying order or simultaneity, hence the Hanafi view that sequentiality is not required.

A dispute is addressed: Some Hanafis claimed that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad believed waw indicates simultaneity based on a divorce case where repeating 'طالق وطالق وطالق' with waw results in three divorces, while Abu Hanifa rules one. The author clarifies that the disagreement is not about the meaning of waw but about the conditional structure: Abu Hanifa considers each subsequent divorce as connected to the condition via intermediaries, so only the first takes effect immediately, while the Sahibayn consider all as directly connected due to the repetition. Thus, waw remains for absolute conjunction in all views.

Key Terms
الواو (al-wāw): The conjunction particle indicating absolute association (جمع) without specifying sequence, simultaneity, or delay.
الفاء (al-fā'): The conjunction particle indicating immediate sequence (ترتيب) without delay, especially in conditional apodosis.
مع (ma'a): The preposition indicating simultaneity (قران) – that two actions occur at the same time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary meaning of the conjunction waw (و) in Arabic as understood by the Hanafis?
A) It indicates immediate sequence (tarṭīb).
B) It indicates simultaneity (muqārana).
C) It indicates absolute conjunction without order or simultaneity.
D) It indicates delay (tarākhī).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the waw (و) indicates absolute conjunction (عطف مطلق) without specifying order, simultaneity, or delay — a principle that affects the interpretation of shariʿah texts and resolves apparent school disputes.

The Conjunction Waw: Order or Concurrency in Legal Utterances?
Explaining the Hanafi position on the conjunction waw and its effect on sequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the conjunction waw and apply it to distinguish between order and concurrency in legal utterances.

Core Idea
The conjunction waw does not impose order; utterances connected by waw are concurrent unless context indicates otherwise.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the linguistic effect of the conjunction waw (و) is a foundational principle that influences rulings across many chapters, from divorce to manumission. This passage from Kanz al-Wusul lays out the Hanafi view that waw indicates absolute conjunction (al-‘atf al-mutlaq) without implying sequence, and uses concrete examples to show when order or concurrency arises based on the legal nature of the act, not the word itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
State the core Hanafi rule: waw does not require tarteeb (order) but indicates ijtima‘ (concurrency) or ittihad (union).
The text begins by stating this principle clearly.
2
Analyze the linguistic reasoning
Understand why the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the second utterance is an incomplete sentence that shares the first's predicate, making the speech act simultaneous.
The order is in the speech act, not in the legal effect.
3
Examine the illustrative cases
Work through the divorce, manumission of slave women, marriage of sisters, and acknowledgment of freed slaves to see how the Hanafi position plays out.
Note when the rule leads to concurrency (single divorce) vs when the subject's removal prevents the second utterance from taking effect.
4
Contrast with the opposing school
Identify Malik's position that waw can imply sequential order (e.g., three divorces) and understand the Hanafi rebuttal.
The text explicitly labels Malik's view as an error (ghalat).
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: in the divorce case, Hanafi rules one divorce, Maliki rules three. This affects every legal utterance connected by waw.
The waw itself does not produce the difference; the legal subject's continuity does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالواو ؛ لأنه لا يتعرض للقرآن. وقالا موجبه الاجتماع والاتحاد ؛ لأن الثاني جملة ناقصة، فشاركت الأُولى، وهو في الحال تكلُّم بالطلاق، وليس بطلاق، فصح التحصيل والترتيب في التكلم لا في صيرورته طلاقاً، كما إذا حصل التعليق بشروط يتخللها أزمنة كثيرة، فإن الترتيب لا يجب به. وإذا كان موجب الكلام ما قلنا لم يتغير بالواو؛ لأنها لا تتعرض للترتيب لا محالة ولا توجبه فلا يُترَكُ المقيد بالمطلق، وإذا تقدمت الأجزية: فقد اتحد حال التعليق، فصار موجب الكلام: الاجتماع والاتحاد، فلم يُترك بالواو ؛ لما قلنا. فإن قيل : فقد قال أصحابنا فيمن قال لامرأته : أنت طالق وطالق وطالق قبل الدخول بها : إنها تبين بواحدة، وهذا من باب الترتيب. وقال في النكاح من الجامع: فيمن زوّج أمتين من رجل بغير إذن مولاهما، وبغير إذن الزوج ، ثم أعتقهما المولى معاً: إنه لا يبطل نكاح واحدة منهما. ولو أعتقهما في كلمتين منفصلتين : بطل نكاح الثانية. فإن قال : هذه حرة، وهذه حرةٌ متصلاً بواو العطف بطل نكاحُ الثانية، وهذا أيضاً من باب الترتيب. وقال في هذا الباب : فيمن زوّج رجلاً أختين في عقدتين متفرقتين بغير إذن الزوج، فبلغه، فأجازهما معاً بطلا وإن أجازهما متفرقاً بطل الثاني وإن قال: أجزتُ نكاح هذه وهذه بطلا كأنه قال أجزتُهما، وهذا من باب المقارنة. وقال في كتاب الإقرار من «الجامع»: فيمن هلك ثلاثة أعبد قيمتهم سواء، وعن ابن لا وارثَ له غيره، فقال الابن: أعتق أبي في مرض موته هذا وهذا وهذا، فإن أقرَّ به في كلام متصل : عتق من كل واحد ثلثه، وإن سكت فيما بين ذلك: عتق الأول، ونصف الثاني، وثلث الثالث، وهذا من باب القرآن أيضاً. قيل له : أما في المسألة الأولى، فقد قال مالك بن أنس: إنه تقع الثلاث وجعلها للقرآن، لكنه غلط، لما قدمنا والواو للعطف المطلق . ولذلك لم يقع الثاني ؛ لأن الأول وقع قبل التكلم بالثاني لما لم يكن الكلام نصاً على المقارنة، ولم يقف على التكلم بالثاني، فسقطت ولايته لفوات محل التصرف، لا لخلل في العبارة. وكذلك في مسألة نكاح الأمتين؛ لأن عنق الأولى يُبطل محلية الوقف في حق الثانية ؛ لأنه لا حل للأمة في مقابلة الحرة حال التوقف فبطل الثاني قبل التكلم بعتقها، ثم لم يصح التدارك؛ لفوات المحل في حكم التوقف، ولأن الواو لا تتعرض للمقارنة. وأما في نكاح الأختين : فإن صَدْرَ الكلام توقف على آخره، لا لاقتضاء واو العطف، لكن لأن صدر الكلام وضع لجواز النكاح، فإذا اتصل به آخره سَلَبَ عنه الجواز، فصار آخرُه في حق أوله: بمنزلة الشرط.
By the waw; because it does not pertain to the Quran. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: its requirement is concurrence and union; because the second is an incomplete sentence, so it participates with the first, and at the moment he is speaking of divorce, and it is not divorce, so the effect and order are valid in the speech, not in its becoming divorce, just as when a conditional occurs with conditions interspersed by many times, order is not required by it. And if the requirement of speech is as we said, it does not change by the waw; because it does not pertain to order inevitably and does not require it, so the restricted is not abandoned for the unrestricted. And if the parts are prior: then the state of the conditional becomes unified, so the requirement of speech becomes concurrence and union, so it is not abandoned by the waw, as we said. For it is said: our companions said concerning one who said to his wife, 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' before consummation: that she becomes separated by one, and this is an example of order. And it is said in the chapter on marriage from al-Jami': concerning one who married two slave women to a man without the permission of their master and without the permission of the husband, then the master freed them both together: that the marriage of neither is invalidated. And if he freed them in two separate utterances: the marriage of the second is invalidated. And if he said: 'This one is free, and this one is free' connected with the conjunction waw, the marriage of the second is invalidated, and this also is an example of order. And it is said in this chapter: concerning one who married two sisters to a man in two separate contracts without the permission of the husband, then he learned of it and ratified both together, both are invalid; and if he ratified them separately, the second is invalid; and if he said: 'I ratify the marriage of this one and this one,' both are invalid, as if he said 'I ratified them,' and this is an example of concurrency. And it is said in the Book of Acknowledgment from al-Jami': concerning one whose three slaves perished of equal value, and he had no other heir but his son, and the son said: 'My father freed in his terminal illness this one, and this one, and this one,' and the acknowledgment is made in continuous speech: one-third of each is freed; and if he pauses in between: the first is freed, half of the second, and one-third of the third, and this also is an example of concurrency. It was said to him: As for the first issue, Malik ibn Anas said that the three occur, and he considered it by the Quran, but he erred, as we have presented, and the waw is for absolute conjunction. And therefore the second did not occur; because the first occurred before speaking the second, since the speech was not a text on concurrency, and he did not stop at the second utterance, so his authority fell due to the loss of the subject of disposal, not due to a defect in the expression. And likewise in the issue of the two slave women; because the manumission of the first nullifies the permissibility of waiting regarding the second; because there is no permissibility for the slave woman in the presence of a free woman during the waiting period, so the second is invalidated before speaking of her manumission, then rectification is not valid due to the loss of the subject in the ruling of waiting, and because the waw does not pertain to concurrency. And as for the marriage of the two sisters: the first part of the speech was conditional upon the last, not because the waw requires it, but because the first part was placed for the permissibility of marriage, and when the last part is connected to it, it removes the permissibility, so the last part becomes like a condition regarding the first.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul al-fiqh principle: the linguistic effect of the conjunction waw (و) on legal utterances. The discussion centers on whether waw necessitates order (tarteeb) or concurrency (ijtima'). The Hanafi school, specifically Abu Hanifa and his two companions, hold that waw does not impose order; rather, it indicates mere conjunction (al-'atf al-mutlaq). The text begins by stating that the waw does not pertain to the Quran (i.e., it does not affect the divine text's order). Then it presents the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) that the requirement of waw is concurrence and union because the second utterance is an incomplete sentence sharing the same predicate. They argue that the order is in the speech act, not in the legal effect becoming effective, similar to a conditional sentence with multiple conditions where order is not required. The passage then presents several illustrative cases. First, a man says to his wife before consummation: 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced.' The Hanafi ruling is that only one divorce occurs, and the waw does not make it three. This is cited as an example where order might be inferred, but the correct interpretation is concurrency. Second, in the marriage of two female slaves freed together, the marriages remain valid; but if freed separately, the second is invalidated, indicating that waw connected utterances cause concurrency. Third, in the marriage of two sisters, if the husband ratifies both together, both are invalid; if separately, the second is invalid. This shows that waw joined utterances are treated as concurrent. Fourth, in an inheritance case where a son acknowledges his father's freeing of three slaves in continuous speech, each slave is freed one-third; if paused, the later ones are fractionally freed. The passage then criticizes Malik ibn Anas for holding that the waw in the divorce case causes three divorces, calling it an error. The reasoning is that the first divorce occurs immediately, and since the waw does not indicate concurrency, the second utterance cannot take effect because the subject (the wife) has already become separated. Similarly, in the slave case, the first manumission removes the waiting period's applicability, so the second cannot take effect. In the sister case, the initial permission for marriage is conditional upon the whole statement, so the second part retroactively invalidates the first. Throughout, the principle is that waw does not alter the inherent legal effect; the context and the nature of the actions determine concurrency or order.

Key Terms
الواو (al-waw): The conjunction 'and' which, according to this text, indicates mere conjunction without implying order.
الترتيب (al-tarteeb): Order or sequence, where the second utterance takes effect after the first.
الاجتماع والاتحاد (al-ijtima' wa al-ittihad): Concurrency and union, meaning that connected utterances take effect simultaneously.
العطف المطلق (al-'atf al-mutlaq): Absolute conjunction, where the connector does not add any additional meaning like order or causality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, if a man says to his wife before consummation, 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced,' how many divorces occur?
A) Three
B) One
C) Two
D) None

Key Takeaway
The conjunction waw in legal utterances indicates absolute conjunction, not order; the ruling depends on whether the first utterance removes the subject of disposal, not on the waw itself.

Dependency of a Statement's Beginning on Its End in Conjunction
Analyzing when the beginning of a legal statement depends on its end due to conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a statement's beginning depends on its end only when the end changes its legal implication, and differentiate between cases of dependency and non-dependency using examples from talaq, marriage, and iqrar.

Core Idea
A statement's beginning depends on its end when the end alters the beginning's ruling; otherwise, it does not.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi explores a key principle in understanding conjunctive legal statements: whether the first part of a compound utterance is legally dependent on the second part when connected by a conjunction like 'and'. The author illustrates with four distinct cases: istithna in divorce, triple talaq before consummation, marrying two slave-women, and iqrar regarding a slave's freedom and debt. By analyzing these, you will learn to assess when a conjunction changes the legal effect of the first clause and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Recognize that the beginning of a statement depends on its end when the end modifies the ruling of the beginning, contingent on continuity for istithna.
The principle is stated with the istithna example: 'You are divorced if Allah wills' requires the exception to be connected.
2
Examine the istithna case
In divorce with istithna, the beginning (divorce) depends on the end (exception) only if the utterance is continuous. This is a general rule explained later in Bab al-Bayan.
Continuity (ittisal) is a condition for the dependency in istithna.
3
Contrast with triple talaq before consummation
Saying 'divorced, and divorced, and divorced' does not make the first divorce dependent because the subsequent repetitions do not change the first talaq's effect.
The beginning remains unchanged; hence no dependency.
4
Contrast with marrying two slave-women
Joining the freeing of the second to the first does not invalidate or change the first marriage, so no dependency arises.
The first marriage remains valid despite the conjunction.
5
Analyze the iqrar case and intra-Hanafi dispute
In iqrar 'he is free and on him is a thousand dirhams', the beginning changes: Abu Hanifa says from freedom to slavery (slave becomes musasta), the two companions say from innocence to debt burden. Both agree the beginning depends on the end.
The dispute is about how the beginning changes, not about the fact of dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاستثناء في قول الرجل : أنتِ طالق إن شاء الله ، وصَدْرُ الكلام يتوقف عليه بشرط الوصل؛ لِمَا نبين في باب البيان إن شاء الله تعالى، فكذلك هذا.
وهذا لا يوجد في قول الرجل : أنت طالق وطالق وطالق قبل الدخول ؛ لأن صَدْرَ الكلام لا يتغير بآخره، فلم يتوقف عليه. وكذلك في مسألة نكاح الأمتين: لا يتغير صدر الكلام بآخره؛ لأن عتق الثانية إن ضُمَّ إلى الأولى: لم يتغير نكاح الأولى عن الصحة إلى الفساد، وعن الوجود إلى العدم.
- وكذلك في مسألة الإقرار : صَدْرُ الكلام يتغير بآخره، ألا يُرى أن موجب صدره عتقه بلا سعاية وإذا انضم الآخر إلى الأول: تغير الصدر : عن عتق إلى رِقّ عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ؛ لأن المستسعى بمنزلة المكاتب عنده رحمه الله.
وعندهما يتغير عن براءة إلى شغل بدَين السعاية، فلذلك وقف صدره على آخره.
And the exception in a man's statement: 'You are divorced if Allah wills' — the beginning of the statement depends on it (i.e., the exception) on the condition of continuity (of utterance); as we will explain in the chapter of Bayan if Allah wills. So likewise this. And this (dependency) does not exist in a man's statement: 'You are divorced, and divorced, and divorced' before consummation; because the beginning of the statement does not change by its end, so it does not depend on it. Likewise, in the issue of marrying two slave-women: the beginning of the statement does not change by its end; because if the freeing of the second is joined to the first, the marriage of the first does not change from validity to invalidity, nor from existence to non-existence. And likewise, in the issue of acknowledgment (iqrar): the beginning of the statement changes by its end. Do you not see that the implication of its beginning is his freedom without earning (sa'yah), but when the second (part) is joined to the first, the beginning changes: from freedom to slavery according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) because the one compelled to earn (musasta) is like a mukatab in his view. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it changes from innocence to being occupied with the debt of earning, therefore its beginning depends on its end.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi examines the legal principle of whether the beginning of a conjunctive statement depends on its end. The author first discusses the case of istithna (exception) in a divorce pronouncement, 'You are divorced if Allah wills,' where the beginning (divorce) is contingent on the end (exception) only if the utterance is continuous (wusul). This is a general rule. He then contrasts this with a triple talaq before consummation: repeating 'divorced, and divorced, and divorced' does not make the beginning dependent on the end because the beginning (first talaq) remains unchanged regardless of the subsequent repetitions. Similarly, in the case of marrying two slave-women (where one may own two slaves and marry them with conditions), joining the second does not alter the validity of the first marriage. Finally, he discusses the case of iqrar (acknowledgment) where a man says, 'He is free, and he owes a thousand dirhams.' Here, the beginning changes: according to Abu Hanifa, the first part (freedom) changes to slavery because the second part (debt) implies the slave must earn to pay, making him like a mukatab. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the beginning changes from innocence (freedom without debt) to being burdened with a debt. In both views, the beginning depends on the end, requiring the entire statement to be considered as one unit.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna): An exception that makes the beginning of a statement contingent on its end if the utterance is continuous.
صدر الكلام (sadr al-kalam): The initial part of a compound statement whose legal effect may depend on a subsequent part joined by conjunction.
العطف (al-ataf): Grammatical conjunction, especially using waw, which can combine clauses and may affect the legal ruling of the first clause.
السعاية (al-sa'yah): Earning or labor required from a slave to pay off a debt; the slave compelled to earn is called musasta.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, in the iqrar case where a man says, 'He is free, and on him is a thousand dirhams,' what happens to the first part of the statement?
A) The slave becomes free immediately without any debt.
B) The beginning changes from freedom to slavery, and the slave must earn to pay the debt.
C) The beginning changes from innocence to being occupied with the debt, but the slave remains free.
D) The beginning remains unchanged, and the statement is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a conjunctive statement's beginning is legally dependent on its end only when the end modifies the beginning's ruling; otherwise, each part retains its independent effect.

Waw with Complete Sentences: No Predicate Sharing Required
Explaining that when waw precedes a complete sentence, a shared predicate is not obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a complete and an incomplete sentence when analyzing the effect of waw, and apply the Hanafi rule that a complete sentence joined by waw does not share the predicate of the first clause.

Core Idea
Waw between complete sentences does not force predicate sharing; duty to share arises only when the second clause is incomplete.

In Hanafi usul, the conjunction waw can have different effects on legal utterances depending on the grammatical completeness of the clauses it joins. This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi clarifies a common point of confusion: when the second clause is a complete sentence with its own predicate, waw does not oblige it to borrow the predicate from the first. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting legal statements, especially in divorce (talaq) and conditional oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two clauses
Find the clauses joined by the waw. The first clause is the complete statement; the second is after the waw.
2
Check the second clause's completeness
Does the second clause have its own subject and predicate (habar)? If it is a complete sentence, it is independent.
The passage says: 'وقد تدخل الواو على جملة كاملة بخبرها' – waw enters upon a complete sentence with its own predicate.
3
Apply the rule of predicate sharing
If the second clause is incomplete (lacking a predicate), it must share the predicate of the first. If complete, it does not share.
The author says: 'فلا تجب به المشاركةُ في الخبر' – so shared participation in the predicate is not obligatory.
4
Test with the divorce example
In 'This woman is thrice divorced, and this woman (is divorced) once,' the second clause is complete (it has its own predicate), so it does not share 'thrice' and thus is only a single divorce.
Contrast with the conditional example where the second clause is incomplete and shares the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تدخل الواو على جملة كاملة بخبرها، فلا تجب به المشاركةُ في الخبر، مثل قول الرجل : هذه طالق ثلاثاً، وهذه طالق أن الثانية تطلق واحدةً، فسمى بعضُهم هذه : واو الابتداء، أو : واو النظم، وهذا فضل من الكلام، وإنما هي للعطف على ما هو أصلها، لكن الشركة في الخبر كانت واجبةً؛ لافتقار الكلام الثاني إذا كان ناقصاً فأما إذا كان تاماً: فقد ذهب دليل الشركة.
ولهذا قلنا: إن الجملة الناقصة تشارك الأُولى فيما تم به الأُولى بعينه، حتى قلنا في قول الرجل : إن دخلت الدارَ فأنت طالق وطالق إن الثاني يتعلق بذلك الشرط بعينه ولا يقتضي الاستبداد به، كأنه أعاده.
وإنما يُصار إلى هذا ؛ لضرورة استحالة الاشتراك، مثل قولك: جاءني زيد وعمرو : إن الثاني يختص بمجيء على حِدَة؛ لأن الاشتراك في مجيء واحد لا يتصور.
And indeed the waw may enter upon a complete sentence with its own predicate, so shared participation in the predicate is not obligatory by it, as in a man's statement: 'This woman is thrice divorced, and this woman (is divorced)' - the second is divorced once. So some have called this: 'waw of incepting' or 'waw of arrangement,' and this is an excess of speech. Indeed it is for conjunction in its original sense, but the sharing in the predicate was obligatory due to the second clause being incomplete. As for when it is complete, then the evidence for sharing is gone. Therefore we say: that the incomplete sentence shares with the first in that by which the first was made complete, so that we said concerning a man's statement: 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced, and (you are) divorced' - the second is attached to that same condition and does not require independence from it, as if he repeated it. And recourse is only to this due to the impossibility of sharing, as in your saying: 'Zaid came to me and Amr' - the second is specific to a separate coming, because sharing in a single coming is not conceivable.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi discusses the effect of the conjunction waw when it joins a complete sentence (a sentence with its own predicate). If the second clause is complete with its own predicate, then the waw does not force it to share the predicate of the first clause. An example: 'This woman is thrice divorced, and this woman (is divorced) once' — here the second 'this woman' is a complete sentence with its own predicate (implicitly 'is divorced'), so it does not share the 'thrice' from the first sentence. Some grammarians call this waw a 'waw of incepting' or 'waw of arrangement,' but the author clarifies it is still a conjunction, but because the second clause is complete, the reason for sharing the predicate (which was the incompleteness of the second clause) is absent. In contrast, an incomplete sentence (lacking a predicate) must share the predicate of the first sentence. For example, in a conditional statement: 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced, and (you are) divorced' — here the second 'divorced' (without repeating the condition) is an incomplete sentence, so it depends on the same condition and results, meaning the second divorce is also contingent on the same entry. The author explains that this joint participation is necessary because if it were not joint, the meaning would require a separate coming (like 'Zaid came and Amr came'), which is not possible in the context of a single condition.

Key Terms
الجملة الناقصة (al-jumla al-naqisa): A sentence lacking a predicate (habar), which when joined by waw shares the predicate of the first sentence.
الجملة التامة (al-jumla al-tamma): A sentence that is complete with its own subject and predicate; when joined by waw, it does not share a predicate with a prior sentence.
واو الابتداء (waw al-ibtida): A term used by some grammarians for waw that introduces a new sentence; the author argues it is still a conjunction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the statement 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced and (you are) divorced,' why does the second 'divorced' refer to the same condition?
A) Because the second clause is a complete sentence with its own predicate.
B) Because the second clause is an incomplete sentence that must share the predicate (the condition and result) from the first.
C) Because the waw in this context is a waw of isti'naf, not conjunction.
D) Because the word 'divorced' implies a new condition automatically.

Key Takeaway
A complete sentence joined by waw does not share the predicate of the first; only incomplete sentences require such sharing.

Waw of Conjunction and Waw of Circumstance in Hanafi Usul
Defining the two usages of waw with Quranic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the conjunctional and circumstantial uses of the Arabic waw in Quranic texts and apply the Hanafi principle that the default is conjunction unless a contextual indicator (qarīna) shifts it to the circumstantial meaning.

Core Idea
Waw defaults to conjunction; it is only borrowed for circumstance when context requires.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the letter waw is a key conjunction. Its default function is to join sentences (ʿaṭf al-jumla). However, it can be borrowed (mustaʿār) to indicate a circumstantial clause (ḥāl) when the context supports it. This lesson examines the evidence from the Quran and the principle behind this shift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of waw
Recognize that waw can function as a conjunction (ʿaṭf al-jumla) or as a circumstantial particle (wāw al-ḥāl). The passage states the default is conjunction.
2
Examine the Quranic examples
Look at the verses cited: al-Nur 4, al-Shura 24, Aal Imran 7 for conjunction; al-Zumar 73 for circumstance. Note how the last verse is interpreted as 'when they come to it and its gates are open' (i.e., with gates already open).
The OCR in the source has corrupted some verses; rely on the standard Quranic text.
3
Understand the reasoning for borrowing
The passage explains that the waw can be borrowed for ḥāl because the circumstance coexists with the subject (musāḥabah), and the absolute meaning of waw can bear this shift.
This is an example of istiʿāra (metaphorical usage) accepted in Hanafi usul.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule
The default is conjunction; a shift to ḥāl requires a clear contextual indicator (qarīna). In the absence of such evidence, waw is taken as simple conjunction.
This principle affects how legal texts (like marriage contracts) are interpreted.

Commentary
This passage begins by stating a principle about the Arabic conjunction waw. It first discusses 'sharing' (ishterāk) in a sentence, illustrated by a man saying 'So-and-so owes me a thousand, and so-and-so' – here the second recipient is necessarily included. Then the author defines 'atf al-jumla (sentence conjunction) and provides Quranic examples such as the verse from Surah al-Nur about the slanderers, and others. The key point is that waw can be used metaphorically (istiʿārah) to express a circumstantial clause (ḥāl) because a circumstance coexists with its subject. The Quranic example from Surah al-Zumar ('until when they come to it and its gates are opened') is interpreted as 'and its gates open', i.e., they come to it with gates already opened, showing waw as ḥāl. The author establishes that waw's default is conjunction, but context can shift it to a circumstantial meaning.

Key Terms
عطف الجملة (ʿaṭf al-jumla): Conjunction of sentences where the waw joins two independent statements without imposing a condition or circumstance.
واو الحال (wāw al-ḥāl): The waw used to introduce a circumstantial clause, indicating the state in which the action occurs.
استعارة (istiʿāra): Metaphorical usage or borrowing of a word from its original meaning to another.

Key Takeaway
The waw in Arabic primarily serves to conjoin sentences, but it can be borrowed for the circumstantial clause when context provides evidence of simultaneity.

The Particle 'Thumma': Meaning and Hanafi Dispute
Analyses the conjunction thumma and its legal effect on conditional divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi interpretations of 'thumma' in conditional pronouncements and derive the resulting ruling for a conditional divorce scenario.

Core Idea
Thumma indicates delay, but the dispute is whether it severs or merely postpones the connection to the condition.

Words matter, especially in the law of divorce. The simple conjunction 'thumma' (then) sparks a fundamental disagreement among the early Hanafi masters about whether it creates full discontinuity or just a temporal gap. How we read this particle determines how many divorces fall when a condition is attached.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic function of 'thumma'
State that 'thumma' is a conjunction particle (حرف عطف) that indicates delay (تراخي) between the events it conjoins.
This is its subject (موضوع) – the primary meaning.
2
State the two Hanafi interpretations of the delay
Abu Hanifa says the delay implies full discontinuity (انقطاع), making the second clause independent. The Sahibayn say the delay is only in existence (وجود), while the utterance remains connected (متصل).
The difference is whether the connection is in the speech or only in the occurrence.
3
Apply to the conditional divorce example
Take the case of a man saying to his non-consummated wife: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house' (condition after). Under Abu Hanifa, only the first divorce is tied to the condition; the rest are void. Under the Sahibayn, all three are suspended.
The condition's placement (before or after) changes the outcome.
4
Note the impact of condition placement
If the condition is advanced, Abu Hanifa rules that the first divorce is suspended, the second occurs unconditionally, and the third is void. The Sahibayn, however, suspend all regardless of order.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf – the practical consequence of the disagreement.
5
Recognize the Quranic and hadith evidence
The passage shows that 'thumma' can be used metaphorically as 'waw' (and), citing Quran verses and the hadith about oath expiation. This supports the Sahibayn's view that the delay may not be absolute.
The variant wordings of the hadith provide a qarinah (contextual clue) for metaphorical interpretation.
