[Letter: Thumma]
As for 'thumma' for conjunction in the manner of delay, that is its subject; it is specified and means something that is unique.
Our companions differed regarding the effect of delay:
Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, said: It means discontinuity, as if it is an independent ruling, fully implementing delay.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: The delay pertains to existence, but in terms of utterance it is connected.
Its clarification: In the case of a man saying to his wife before consummation: "You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced if you enter the house."
Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The first occurs, and what follows is void, as if he paused after the first.
If he advanced the condition: The first is suspended, the second occurs, and the third is void, as if he said: "If you enter the house, you are divorced, divorced, divorced."
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, said: All are suspended, and they descend in order, whether the condition is advanced or delayed.
And if she was after consummation: The first and second descend, and the third is suspended if the condition is delayed, and if he advanced it: The first is suspended and the rest descend according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him.
According to both of them, all are suspended. He mentioned it in 'Al-Nawadir'.
And 'thumma' may be used metaphorically in the sense of the conjunctive 'waw' due to their adjacency. Allah the Exalted said: "Then he was among those who believed" (Al-Balad 17), "Then Allah is witness over what they do" (Yunus 46).
For this reason we said regarding the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: "Whoever swears an oath and sees something better than it, let him do that which is better, then expiate his oath."
And it has wordings: From Abdur Rahman bin Samura, the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: "If you swear an oath and see something better than it, do that which is better and expiate your oath." In another wording: "Expiate your oath and do that which is better." Both agreed upon. In another wording: "If you swear an oath, expiate your oath, then do that which is better." Narrated by Nasa'i and Abu Dawud.
From Adi bin Hatim, the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: "If one of you swears an oath and sees something better than it, let him expiate it and do that which is better." Narrated by Muslim.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the Arabic conjunction particle 'thumma' (then) and its legal implications, particularly in conditional divorce oaths. The author first states that 'thumma' is used for conjunction with delay (tarakhi), and it is specifically for this purpose. Then he presents the disagreement among Hanafi scholars regarding the effect of this delay. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that 'thumma' indicates discontinuity (inqita'), meaning that the second and subsequent clauses are independent of the first, as if the speaker paused. Therefore, in a conditional divorce where the condition is stated after the divorce pronouncements, only the first divorce takes effect and the rest are void because they are not connected to the condition. If the condition is placed first, then the first divorce is suspended on the condition, the second occurs unconditionally, and the third is void — similar to repeating 'taliq' without 'thumma'. Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) argue that the delay pertains only to the existence (wujud) of the events, not to the utterance; thus all pronouncements are connected in speech. Consequently, all divorces are suspended on the condition and descend in order, regardless of condition placement. The author then discusses the case of a wife after consummation (madkhul biha), where the rulings differ: Abu Hanifa allows the second divorce to fall immediately even if the condition is delayed, while the third is suspended. The Sahibayn hold that all are suspended. The author notes that this latter ruling is reported in 'Al-Nawadir' (rare transmissions), indicating it is not from the standard Zahir al-Riwayah corpus. Finally, the passage shows that 'thumma' can be used metaphorically in the sense of 'waw' (and) due to adjacency, citing Quranic verses and the hadith about oath expiation where the order of expiation and action varies.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): A conjunction particle meaning 'then', indicating delay (tarakhi) between the conjoined elements.
تراخي (tarakhi): Delay or postponement; the characteristic of thumma that separates the conjoined events in time.
انقطاع (inqita'): Discontinuity; Abu Hanifa's interpretation that thumma makes the following clause independent and disconnected from the first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All three take effect in order.
B) Only the first takes effect.
C) The first and second take effect, the third is suspended.
D) None take effect because the condition is postponed.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'thumma' is not merely linguistic—it determines the number of divorces that fall when a condition is added, creating a clear practical difference between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

The Particle Bal: Correction in Informative vs. Performative Speech
Deriving rulings for the particle bal in declaratory and constitutive speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for the particle 'bal' to derive rulings in both informative and performative utterances.

Core Idea
Bal corrects in informative speech but accumulates in performative speech — the first utterance's effect is immediate.

The particle 'bal' (بل) is used in Arabic to correct or replace a prior statement. However, its legal effect depends on whether the utterance is informative (ikhbar) or performative (insha'). In this passage, the author explains the Hanafi position, contrasts it with Zufar's view, and applies it to cases of debt admission and divorce. Understanding this distinction is essential for parsing legal utterances correctly.

Guided Path
1
Define the particle and its literal function
Identify the definition of 'bal' from the text: it affirms what comes after and disregards what comes before by way of correction (tadarruk).
The Arabic term 'tadarruk' is key.
2
Distinguish informative vs. performative speech
Note the crucial distinction: informative speech (ikhbar) reports a fact and can be corrected; performative speech (insha') creates an immediate legal effect (e.g., divorce) and cannot be retracted.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul in this context.
3
Apply to the debt admission example
Examine Zufar's view that 'bal' nullifies the first, leading to 3000 dirhams. Then see the majority response: the first admission is binding, so both amounts are cumulative (3000 dirhams).
The majority agrees with Zufar on the result but differs on the reasoning.
4
Apply to the divorce example
For a direct divorce statement ('you are divorced once, rather twice'), the majority holds that three divorces occur because the first is effective immediately and the second adds two more. However, if it is a report ('I divorced you yesterday once, rather twice'), it is informative and only two divorces occur.
The distinction turns on whether the utterance is a direct pronouncement or a report.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference between the positions: Zufar's reasoning would also lead to cumulative liability but his principle is different. The majority's distinction between ikhbar and insha' gives consistent results across various cases.
The term 'thamarah al-khilaf' refers to the practical consequence of the scholarly disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[حرف : بل : ] وأما : بل: فموضوع لإثبات ما بعده، والإعراض عما قبله على سبيل التدارك، يقال: جاءني زيد، بل عمرو. ولهذا قال زفر رحمه الله : فيمن قال لفلان عليَّ ألف درهم، بل ألفان: إنه يلزمه ثلاثة آلاف درهم؛ لأنه أثبت الثاني، وأبطل الأول، لكنه غير مالك إبطال الأول، فلزماه. كما لو قال لامرأته : أنت طالق واحدةً، لا بل ثنتين : إنها تطلق ثلاثاً. وقلنا نحن : إنما وُضعت هذه الكلمة للتدارك، وذلك في العادات بأن يُنفى انفراده، ويُراد بالجملة الثانية كمالها بالأولى، وهذا في الإخبار ممكن، كرجل يقول: سنّي ستون بل سبعون أي سبعون بزيادة عشرة على الأول. وأما الإنشاء : فلا يحتمل تدارك الغلط، فلذلك وقع ثلاث تطليقات، حتى إذا قال: كنتُ طلقت أمس امرأتي واحدةً، بل ثنتين، أو : لا بل ثنتين : وقعت ثنتان ؛ لما قلنا. ولهذا قلنا : فيمن قال لامرأته : أنت طالق واحدة، لا بل ثنتين، أو: بل ثنتين ولم يدخل بها : إنها تطلق واحدةً ؛ لأنه قَصَدَ إِثبات الثاني مَقامَ الأول، ولم يملك ؛ لأنها بانت بالأُولى. ولهذا قالوا جميعاً فيمَن قال لامرأته قبل الدخول بها: إن دخلت الدارَ فأنت طالق واحدةً لا بل ثنتين أو بل ثنتين: إنها إذا دخلت:
[Section: The Particle 'Bal'] As for 'bal': it is placed to affirm what comes after it and to disregard what comes before it, by way of correction. It is said: 'Zaid came to me, rather 'Amr.' That is why Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) said concerning one who said to someone, 'I owe one thousand dirhams, rather two thousand': he is liable for three thousand dirhams because he affirmed the second and nullified the first, but he does not have the authority to nullify the first, so both are binding. Similarly, if he said to his wife, 'You are divorced once, nay twice,' she is divorced three times. But we say: this word is placed for correction, and that customarily means that the first is negated as standalone, and the second sentence is intended as a completion of the first. This is possible in informative speech, as when a man says, 'My age is sixty, rather seventy,' meaning seventy with an increase of ten over the first. As for constitutive speech, it does not allow correction of error; therefore three divorces occur. However, if he said, 'I divorced my wife yesterday once, rather twice,' or 'nay twice,' two divorces occur, due to what we said. That is why we say concerning one who said to his wife without consummation, 'You are divorced once, nay twice,' or 'rather twice,' she is divorced once because he intended to establish the second in place of the first, but he does not have the authority (since she is already irrevocably divorced by the first). And that is why they all said concerning one who said to his wife before consummation, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced once, nay twice,' or 'rather twice': when she enters (the ruling is incomplete in the text).
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage discusses the particle 'bal' (بل) and its legal implications. The author defines 'bal' as a word used to affirm the second statement and disregard the first, functioning for correction (tadarruk). In informative speech (ikhbar), correction is possible: e.g., 'I met Zaid, rather Amr' means the first is replaced. However, Zufar holds that when someone says 'I owe one thousand dirhams, rather two thousand,' both are binding because he cannot unilaterally nullify the first admission. Similarly in divorce: saying 'You are divorced once, nay twice' results in three divorces according to Zufar. The majority Hanafi position (we say) distinguishes between informative speech (ikhbar) and performative speech (insha'). In informative speech, 'bal' can indicate a completion or correction, so 'my age is sixty, rather seventy' means seventy with an increase. In performative speech (like divorce), correction is not allowed because the first utterance has immediate legal effect and cannot be nullified. Therefore, if one says 'You are divorced once, rather twice' in a single session, it results in three divorces (one from the first and two from the second). However, if he says 'I divorced you yesterday once, rather twice,' it is interpreted as a report and only two divorces occur (the first is corrected). For a man who says to his wife before consummation 'You are divorced once, rather twice,' she is divorced once because the first divorce made her irrevocable, and he cannot add more. The passage ends with a conditional case where the ruling is cut off.

Key Terms
بل (bal): A particle used for correction (tadarruk), affirming the second and disregarding the first.
التدارك (al-tadarruk): Correction or rectification of a previous statement by a subsequent one.
الإخبار (al-ikhbar): Informative speech that reports a fact and can be corrected.
الإنشاء (al-insha'): Constitutive speech that creates a legal effect immediately, such as divorce or oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, what is the ruling if a man says to his wife before consummation: 'You are divorced once, rather twice'?
A) She is divorced three times.
B) She is divorced once.
C) She is divorced twice.
D) The divorce is suspended.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'bal' corrects in informative speech but adds to the first in performative speech due to the immediate legal effect of the first utterance.

The Particle Lākin (but) in Hanafi Juristic Language: Function and Legal Implications
Explains the function of 'lākin' for istidrāk and its legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a statement containing 'lākin' functions as a conjunctive (istidrāk) and when it is a new sentence, applying the Hanafi condition of ittisāq al-kalām.

Core Idea
Lākin affirms what follows after negation, but its legal effect depends on whether the speech is connected (muttasil) or disconnected.

This passage delves into the juristic function of the particle 'lākin' (but). While it is commonly used to rectify a negation, Hanafi usul introduces a crucial condition: the connection of speech (ittisāq al-kalām). Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly interpreting legal statements that involve negation and exception, especially in acknowledgments and testimonies. The text provides a definition, a condition, and several fiqh examples to illustrate the rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic function of lākin
Identify that lākin is used for istidrāk (rectification) after negation. It affirms what follows it, while the negation of the first part stands by its own evidence. Note the contrast with 'bal'.
2
Distinguish connected vs. disconnected speech
Recognize that conjunction (istidrāk) with lākin is valid only when the speech is connected (muttasil). If disconnected (munqati‘), the clause after lākin is a new sentence (musta’naf) and does not attach to the negation.
The condition is syntactic and semantic continuity.
3
Apply the condition to the slave acknowledgment example
Analyze the example from al-Jami' al-Kabir: a man acknowledges a slave belongs to someone, but that person denies and says 'It was never mine, but it belongs to X.' Determine whether the speech is connected or disconnected to decide legal outcome.
If connected → slave to X; if disconnected → acknowledgment reverts to acknowledger.
4
Apply to the property judgment example
Examine the case where one adjudicated owner says 'It was never mine, but it belongs to so-and-so.' The text rules the speech is connected, so property goes to the second. But the speaker's testimony is invalid due to partiality.
Connection creates an explanation of negation, not a new claim.
5
Apply to marriage and loan examples
Distinguish when speech is disconnected: master's refusal followed by conditional authorization is not connected, so lākin starts a new sentence. In the loan vs. usurpation example, speech is connected, so negation applies to the cause, not the obligation.
The test is whether the negation and affirmation are about the same act or different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما لكن فقد وضع للاستدراك بعد النفي، تقول: ما جاءني زيد لكن عمرو، فصار الثابت به إثبات ما بعده، فأما نفي الأول: فيثبت بدليله. بخلاف كلمة : بل، غير أن العطف إنما يستقيم عند اتساق الكلام، فإذا اتسق الكلام: تعلّق النفي بالإثبات الذي وصل به وإلا فهو مستأنف. مثاله : ما قال علماؤنا في «الجامع الكبير»: في رجل في يده عبد، فأقرَّ أنه لفلان، فقال فلان ما كان لي قط، لكنه لفلان آخر : فإن وَصَلَ الكلامَ فهو للمقر له الثاني، وإن فَصَلَ : يُرَدُّ على المقر ؛ نفسه، فاحتمل أن يكون نفياً نفسه أصلاً، عن فيرجع إلى الأول. ويحتمل أن يكون نفياً إلى غير الأول، فإذا وَصَلَ: كان بياناً أنه نفاه إلى الثاني، وإذا فصل : كان مطلقاً، فصار تكذيباً للمقر.
- وقالوا في المقضي له بدارٍ بالبينة : إذا قال ما كانت لي قط، لكنها لفلان، وقال فلان إنه باعني بعد القضاء، أو : وهبني : إن الدار للمقر له، وعلى المقضي له القيمة للمقضي عليه ؛ لأنه نفاه عن نفسه إلى الثاني أيضاً، حيث وَصَلَ به البيان، إلا أنه بالإسناد صار شاهداً على المقر له، فلم تصح شهادته، على ما بينا في شرح الجامع، في الباب الثالث من دعواه. وقال في نكاح الجامع: في أمة تزوجت بغير إذن مولاها بمائة فقال المولى: لا أجيز النكاح ، لكن أجيزه بمائة وخمسين، أو: زدتني خمسين : إن هذا فسح للنكاح ، وجُعل لكن : مبتدأ ؛ لأن الكلام غير متسق؛ لأنه نفي فعل، وإثباته بعينه، فلم يصلح للتدارك.
وفي قول الرجل : لك عليَّ ألف درهم قرض، فقال المقر له: لا، ولكنه غصب: إن الكلامَ متّسق، فصح الوصل لبيان أنه نفى السبب، لا الواجب.
And as for 'lākin', it has been placed for rectification after negation. You say: 'Zayd did not come to me, but Amr [came].' So what is established by it is the affirmation of what comes after it. As for the negation of the first, it is established by its own evidence. Unlike the word 'bal'. However, conjunction is only proper when the speech is connected. So when the speech is connected, the negation attaches to the affirmation that is joined to it; otherwise, it is a new start. Its example: what our scholars said in al-Jami' al-Kabir concerning a man who had a slave in his possession and acknowledged that it belonged to a certain person, and that person said: 'It was never mine, but it belongs to another person.' If he connects the speech, it is for the second acknowledged recipient; if he disconnects, it is returned against the acknowledger himself. It is possible that it is a negation of himself originally, so it reverts to the first. It is also possible that it is a negation of other than the first. If he connects, it is an explanation that he negated it to the second; if he disconnects, it is absolute, so it becomes a denial of the acknowledger. And they said concerning the one adjudicated a property by evidence: if he says, 'It was never mine, but it belongs to so-and-so,' and that so-and-so says, 'He sold it to me after the judgment, or he gifted it to me,' then the property belongs to the acknowledged recipient, and the adjudicated party must pay its value to the one against whom it was adjudicated, because he negated it from himself to the second as well, where he connected the explanation with it. However, by the attribution he becomes a witness against the acknowledged recipient, so his testimony is not valid, as we explained in the commentary on al-Jami' in the third chapter of claims. And he said in the Nikah chapter of al-Jami': concerning a slave girl who married without her master's permission for one hundred [dirhams], and the master said: 'I do not authorize the marriage, but I authorize it for one hundred and fifty, or you have increased me by fifty.' This is an expansion [fash] for the marriage, and 'lākin' is made as a new start because the speech is not connected; because it is a negation of an act and its affirmation in itself, so it is not suitable for rectification. And in the statement of a man: 'You have one thousand dirhams as a loan,' and the acknowledged recipient says: 'No, but it is usurpation,' the speech is connected, so the connection is valid to indicate that he negated the cause, not the obligation.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage analyzes the particle 'lākin' (but) and its legal effects in statements involving negation and exception. The author begins by stating that 'lākin' is used for istidrāk (rectification) after a negation. For example, 'Zayd did not come to me, but Amr did.' The effect is that what follows 'lākin' is affirmed, while the negation of the first part is proven by its own evidence. He distinguishes 'lākin' from 'bal', noting that conjunction with 'lākin' is valid only when the speech is connected (muttasil). If the speech is connected, the negation applies to the affirmation joined to it; otherwise, the clause after 'lākin' is a new sentence (musta'naf). He then provides examples from Hanafi fiqh. The first is from al-Jami' al-Kabir: a man acknowledges that a slave belongs to someone, and that person denies ownership but says 'but it belongs to another.' The ruling depends on whether the speech is connected or disconnected. If connected, the slave goes to the second person; if disconnected, the acknowledgment reverts to the acknowledger, making him liable. The second example involves a property dispute after a judicial decision: the party says 'it was never mine, but it belongs to so-and-so.' Here the speech is considered connected, so the property goes to the second person, but because the speaker is witnessing against the first, his testimony is invalid. The third example from the Nikah section: a master refuses to authorize a marriage but then says 'but I authorize it with an increase.' The speech is disconnected because the negation and affirmation are about the same act, so 'lākin' starts a new sentence and the master's statement is not a rectification but an extension. The final example: a loan acknowledgment met with denial of the cause. Here the speech is connected, so the negation is taken to negate the cause (usurpation) not the obligation (loan), thus the debt remains.

Key Terms
لَكِن (lākin): حرف استدراك بعد النفي (a particle for rectification after negation)
استدراك (istidrāk): إثبات ما بعد النفي مع بقاء النفي على حاله (affirming what follows after negation while the negation remains)
اتساق الكلام (ittisāq al-kalām): ارتباط الجملتين بحيث يصح عطف إحداهما على الأخرى (connection of two clauses such that conjunction is valid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from al-Jami' al-Kabir about a slave acknowledgment, if the respondent says 'It was never mine, but it belongs to so-and-so' and he connects (wasala) the speech, what is the ruling?
A) The slave goes to the acknowledger.
B) The slave goes to the first acknowledged person.
C) The slave goes to the second person mentioned.
D) The slave is divided between the two.

Key Takeaway
Lākin affirms what follows negation, but conjunction requires speech continuity (ittisāq); otherwise, the clause is a new statement.

The Particle 'Aw' (أو) in Hanafi Legal Reasoning: Alternatives and Doubt
Defines the primary function of 'aw' as denoting one of two alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary function of the particle 'aw' in Hanafi usul and distinguish its effect in commands versus declarative statements.

Core Idea
The particle 'aw' signifies one of two alternatives, not doubt; doubt is only a consequence of its use in statements.

When we say 'Zayd or Amr came,' do we mean both? The Hanafi jurists analyze particles like 'aw' to determine legal scope in oaths, contracts, and texts. This lesson examines the core definition of 'aw' and clarifies a common confusion: is 'aw' itself about doubt?

Guided Path
1
Identify the elements 'aw' connects
The author states that 'aw' enters between two nouns or two verbs. Recognize that it links two parallel items.
This is the grammatical context.
2
State the primary function of 'aw'
'Aw' covers one of the two mentioned items, not both. For example, 'Zayd or Amr came' means one of them.
This is its wad' (original placement).
3
Understand the negation of doubt as purpose
The author emphasizes that 'aw' was not placed for doubt; doubt is not an intended purpose of speech. Rather, the purpose is to present alternatives.
Doubt is a side effect, not the core function.
4
Apply to a khabar (statement) context
When 'aw' is used in a declarative sentence, it covers one of the alternatives unspecified, leading to uncertainty in the listener's mind.
This is why doubt arises in statements, not from the particle itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أو : فإنها تدخل بين اسمين، أو فعلين، فتتناول أحد المذكورين، هذا موضوعها الذي وضعت له. يُقال: جاءني زيد أو عمرو أي أحدهما ، ولم توضع للشك، وليس الشك بأمر مقصود يُقصد بالكلام وضعاً، لكنها وضعت لما قلنا. فإن استعملت في الخبر: تناولت أحدهما غيرَ عَيْنٍ، فأفضى إلى الشك.
And as for 'aw', it enters between two nouns or two verbs, and it covers one of the two mentioned; this is its subject matter for which it was placed. It is said: 'Zayd or Amr came to me,' meaning one of them. And it was not placed for doubt, and doubt is not a matter intended by speech as a subject, but it was placed for what we said. If it is used in a statement, it covers one of them unspecified, leading to doubt.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage defines the particle 'aw' (or) and its primary function. The author states that 'aw' is placed between two nouns or two verbs to indicate one of the two mentioned. For example, 'Zayd or Amr came to me' means one of them, not both. He emphasizes that 'aw' was not originally placed to express doubt; doubt is not the intended purpose of the speaker. Rather, its function is to present alternatives. However, when used in declarative sentences (khabar), it results in ambiguity because the listener does not know which one is meant, and this leads to doubt. This analysis is crucial in Hanafi legal reasoning, especially in oaths, acknowledgments, and contracts where 'aw' affects the scope of obligation or prohibition.

Key Terms
أَو (aw): حرف لتناول أحد المذكورين (a particle for covering one of the mentioned items)
تناول أحد المذكورين (tanāwul aḥad al-madhkūrayn): انصراف اللفظ إلى فرد غير معين من الاسمين أو الفعلين (the word's reference to an unspecified individual of the two nouns or verbs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To express doubt.
B) To indicate one of two things.
C) To connect two similar items.
D) To negate both options.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'aw' is primarily for indicating one of two alternatives; doubt is an incidental effect in statements, not its original purpose.

The Particle Aw: Context-Dependent Meaning in Khabar and Insha'
Defining the particle 'aw' and its function in informative and creative speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the function of the particle 'aw' in different speech contexts (khabar vs insha') and apply the Hanafi rule to derive the legal effect in manumission and divorce utterances.

Core Idea
The particle 'aw' gives choice in creative speech and doubt in informative speech; its meaning depends on context.

The particle 'aw' (or) seems simple, but its legal implications are profound. In Hanafi usul, its meaning shifts depending on whether it is used in khabar (informative) or insha' (creative speech). This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how it determines rulings in manumission and divorce cases. By understanding this, you will see how linguistic analysis directly shapes fiqh outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of speech
Distinguish between khabar (informative speech that can be true/false) and insha' (creative speech like commands, oaths, and contracts).
This distinction is foundational in Hanafi usul.
2
State the core meaning of 'aw'
The particle 'aw' indicates one of two mentioned items without specifying which. It is not originally for doubt.
The author explicitly rejects that 'aw' was placed for doubt.
3
Determine the implication based on context
In khabar, 'aw' leads to doubt because the hearer does not know which alternative is true. In insha', 'aw' gives choice (takhyir) because doubt is not applicable to creative speech.
The change comes from the nature of the speech, not from the particle itself.
4
Apply to manumission and divorce utterances
When a man says 'This slave is free or this one' or 'You are divorced by one or two', the statement is insha' and thus 'aw' gives choice. The intended effect is like saying 'One of you is free' or allowing the wife to choose the number of divorces.
The author references al-Jami' and al-Ziyadat for detailed cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حرف : أو : وأما أو : فإنها تدخل بين اسمين، أو فعلين، فتتناول أحد المذكورين، هذا موضوعها الذي وضعت له. يُقال: جاءني زيد أو عمرو أي أحدهما ، ولم توضع للشك، وليس الشك بأمر مقصود يُقصد بالكلام وضعاً، لكنها وضعت لما قلنا. فإن استعملت في الخبر: تناولت أحدهما غيرَ عَيْنٍ، فأفضى إلى الشك. من غير شك. وإذا استعملت في الابتداء والإنشاء: تناولت أحدهما تقول : ائت زيداً أو عَمرواً فتكون للتخيير؛ لأن الابتداء لا يحتمل الشكَّ، فعلمت أن الشك إنما جاء من قبل محل الكلام. وعلى هذا قلنا في قول الرجل : هذا حرّ أو هذا، وهذه طالق أو هذه: إنه بمنزلة قوله : أحدكما حر. وهذا الكلام إنشاء يحتمل الخبر: فأوجب التخيير على احتمال بيان حتى جُعل البيانُ إنشاء من وجه وإظهاراً من وجه، على ما ذكرنا في مسائل العتاق في «الجامع»، و«الزيادات».
The letter 'aw' (or). As for 'aw', it enters between two nouns or two verbs, and signifies one of the two mentioned. This is its original subject matter. It is said: 'Zayd or Amr came to me,' meaning one of them. It was not placed for doubt, and doubt is not an intended matter to be intended by speech in terms of usage, but it was placed for what we said. If it is used in informative speech (khabar), it signifies one of them without specification, thus it leads to doubt without doubt. And if it is used in initiatory and creative speech (ibtida' and insha'), it signifies one of them. You say: 'Come to Zayd or Amr,' so it is for choice (takhyir); because initiation does not admit doubt. Thus it is known that doubt only comes from the context of the speech. And on this basis we said regarding the statement of a man: 'This one is free or this one,' and 'This one is divorced or this one,' that it is like his saying: 'One of you is free.' This speech is creative (insha') that contains informative (khabar) potential, so it necessitates choice on the probability of clarification, such that the clarification is made creative from one perspective and declarative from another, as we mentioned in the chapters on manumission in al-Jami' and al-Ziyadat.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage introduces the particle 'أو' (or). The author states its basic meaning: it is used between two nouns or verbs to indicate one of them without specification. It is not originally for doubt; rather, doubt only arises when it is used in khabar (informative) contexts, because the hearer does not know which one. In insha' (creative speech like commands, manumission, divorce), it gives takhyir (choice) because doubt is not applicable. The example of saying 'this is free or this' is treated like saying 'one of you is free,' which is an insha' statement that has aspects of both insha' and khabar due to the need for clarification. The lesson clarifies that the meaning of 'aw' is determined by the nature of the speech: if it is informative, it leads to doubt; if creative, it gives choice.

Key Terms
أو (aw): A particle indicating one of two things; in khabar it leads to doubt, in insha' it leads to choice.
خبر (khabar): Informative speech that can be true or false.
إنشاء (insha'): Creative speech like commands, prohibitions, and contracts.
تخيير (takhyir): Choice given to the addressee between alternatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his two daughters: 'I will give this house to Amina or Fatima.' According to the Hanafi rule, what is the legal effect of the particle 'aw'?
A) The statement is void due to doubt.
B) The man must give the house to both daughters equally.
C) The man has the choice to give the house to either daughter.
D) The daughters have the choice to decide which one receives the house.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of the particle 'aw' in Hanafi usul is context-dependent: it produces doubt in informative speech and choice in creative speech, a distinction that directly impacts legal rulings in manumission, divorce, and other contractual utterances.

The Particle 'Aw' in Legal Language: Choice vs. Doubt
Explains the legal effect of 'aw' in ibtida' and insha'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on 'aw' in ibtida' and insha' contexts and apply it to examples of emancipation and agency.

Core Idea
In ibtida' and insha', 'aw' always indicates choice—never doubt.

The particle 'aw' (or) appears frequently in legal statements. Its meaning depends on context. Hanafi usul distinguishes between its use in inaugural statements (ibtida') and constructive speech (insha'), where it conveys genuine takhyir (choice), versus informative statements (khabar) where it may indicate doubt. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this distinction and applies it to emancipation, divorce, and agency examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Determine whether the statement containing 'aw' is an ibtida' (commencement of speech without prior context), insha' (a speech act creating a legal effect), or khabar (informative). Only ibtida' and insha' guarantee choice according to Hanafi usul.
If the statement is a response or news, 'aw' may indicate doubt.
2
Recall the Hanafi rule
Hanafi usul holds that 'aw' in ibtida' is unequivocally for takhyir because commencement excludes doubt. In insha', the same applies by default, as evidenced by the examples of emancipation and agency.
Shafi'i usul treats 'aw' as primarily for doubt unless context clarifies.
3
Apply to emancipation examples
When someone says 'This is free or this' (referring to two slaves), it is interpreted as 'One of you is free' – a valid declaration of emancipation with choice to the speaker to designate which one.
The statement is insha' and thus falls under the rule.
4
Apply to agency examples
When someone says 'I appointed so-and-so or so-and-so as agent to sell this slave,' the agency is valid by istihsan (juristic preference), and either agent may sell. The 'aw' gives choice, and the agency is a constructive act.
Even if the appointment appears ambiguous, the legal effect is upheld.
5
Distinguish from khabar
If 'aw' appears in a purely informative statement (e.g., 'I met Zayd or Amr'), it may indicate doubt about which one was met. Hanafi usul restricts this to khabar; in ibtida' and insha', choice is the default.
This distinction is the core of the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا استعملت في الابتداء والإنشاء: تناولت أحدهما تقول : ائت زيداً أو عَمرواً فتكون للتخيير؛ لأن الابتداء لا يحتمل الشكَّ، فعلمت أن الشك إنما جاء من قبل محل الكلام.
وعلى هذا قلنا في قول الرجل : هذا حرّ أو هذا، وهذه طالق أو هذه: إنه بمنزلة قوله : أحدكما حر.
وهذا الكلام إنشاء يحتمل الخبر: فأوجب التخيير على احتمال بيان حتى جُعل البيانُ إنشاء من وجه وإظهاراً من وجه، على ما ذكرنا في مسائل العتاق في «الجامع»، و«الزيادات».
ولهذا قلنا : فيمن قال : وكَلتُ فلاناً أو فلاناً ببيع هذا العبد : إن التوكيل صحيح، ويبيع أيُّهما شاء؛ لأن أو في موضع الابتداء تخيير، والتوكيل صحيح؛ استحساناً، وأيهما باعه: صح.
وكذلك إذا قال : وكَلْتُ به أحد هذين.
وكذلك إذا قال: بع هذا أو هذا: إنه صحيح، وله أن يبيع أيهما شاء؛ لأن أو في موضع الابتداء للتخيير والتوكيل إنشاء، والتخيير لا يمنع الامتثال.
And when it is used in commencement and construction: it encompasses one of them. You say: 'Come to Zayd or Amr' so it is for choice; because commencement does not admit doubt, so it is known that doubt only comes from the context of the speech. And based on this we said regarding a man's statement: 'This is free or this' and 'This is divorced or this': it is like his saying: 'One of you is free.' And this speech is construction that implies information: so it necessitated choice with the possibility of clarification, so that clarification is made construction in one aspect and manifestation in another aspect, as we mentioned in the chapters of emancipation in al-Jami' and al-Ziyadat. And for this reason we said: regarding one who said: 'I appointed so-and-so or so-and-so as agent to sell this slave': the agency is valid, and either of them may sell; because 'aw' in the position of commencement is for choice, and the agency is valid by istihsan, and whichever of them sells it, it is valid. And likewise if he said: 'I appointed one of these two as agent.' And likewise if he said: 'Sell this or this': it is valid, and he may sell whichever he wishes; because 'aw' in the position of commencement is for choice, and agency is construction, and choice does not prevent compliance.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the particle 'aw' (or) in legal language. The author first distinguishes its use in ibtida' (commencement/beginning of speech) and insha' (construction/creating a legal effect). In ibtida', 'aw' always indicates takhyir (choice) because the speaker is initiating a command or statement without prior context of doubt. For example, 'Bring Zayd or Amr' gives a genuine choice. In insha', such as declarations of emancipation or divorce, 'aw' also gives choice by default, as the statement is a legal construction that could potentially bear information. The author applies this to a man saying 'This is free or this'—it is interpreted as 'One of you is free,' giving him the choice. Similarly, in agency (wakala), if someone says 'I appointed so-and-so or so-and-so as agent,' the agency is valid by istihsan (juristic preference), and either agent may act. The key point is that 'aw' in inaugural positions does not create ambiguity but genuine choice, and this choice does not prevent the validity of the act.

Key Terms
أو (aw): A particle that, in contexts of ibtida' and insha', conveys genuine choice (takhyir) between the two items.
الابتداء (al-ibtidā'): The commencement of speech without prior context; where 'aw' indicates pure choice.
الإنشاء (al-inshā'): A speech act that creates a legal effect (e.g., emancipation, divorce); 'aw' here gives choice by default.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, when 'aw' is used in a statement of commencement (ibtida'), what does it indicate?
A) Doubt and ambiguity
B) Genuine choice (takhyir)
C) Both options are invalid
D) The first option is preferred

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'aw' in ibtida' and insha' contexts conveys genuine choice, not ambiguity—a key principle for interpreting legal speech acts.
